The Four Stations of Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Author outlines the two negative and two positive stations of his critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the four stations of Ibn Taymiyya's critique as negative or positive and associate each with definitions (tasawwur) or syllogisms (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claims from both sides: refuting their necessity and then their sufficiency.

Ibn Taymiyya structures his critique of the logicians into four clear stations (maqamat). Two are negative—refuting the logicians' claims that definition is the only path to conceptualization and that syllogism is the only path to assent. Two are positive—showing that even if definition and syllogism are used, they fail to deliver certain knowledge. This framework organizes the entire book and is essential for following his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two negative stations
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the logicians claim definition is necessary for tasawwur and syllogism for tasdiq. The negative stations refute these claims by denying necessity.
2
Identify the two positive stations
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that even if definition and syllogism are used, they do not yield knowledge. The positive stations attack the sufficiency of these tools.
3
Associate each station with its subject
Match: first negative station is about definitions (tasawwur), second negative about syllogisms (tasdiq). First positive is about definition yielding knowledge, second positive about syllogism yielding knowledge.
4
Understand the order in the book
Note that the book begins with the first negative station (on definitions). This helps you navigate the text: look for the negative argument on definitions first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا شيء آخر، فنقول: الكلام في أربع مقامات، مقامين سالبين ومقامين موجبين. فالأولان: أحدهما في قولهم: إن التصور المطلوب لا ينال إلا بالحد؛ والثاني: إن التصديق المطلوب لا ينال إلا بالقياس؛ والآخران: في أن الحد يفيد العلم بالتصورات؛ وأن القياس أو البرهان الموصوف يفيد العلم بالتصديقات. أما ترتيب هذه المقامات الأربع عند البحث في الكتاب فهكذا: الأول: المقام السلبي في الحدود والتصورات.
And the intended here is something else, so we say: The discussion is in four stations, two negative and two positive. The first two: one is regarding their statement that the desired conceptualization is attained only through definition; and the second that the desired assent is attained only through syllogism. And the other two: that definition yields knowledge of conceptualizations; and that the syllogism or demonstrative described yields knowledge of assents. As for the order of these four stations in the book, it is thus: first: the negative station on definitions and conceptualizations.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage sets out the author's plan for critiquing the logicians. He organizes his critique into four stations (maqamat): two negative and two positive. The negative stations refute the logicians' claims that definition is necessary for conceptualization (tasawwur) and that syllogism is necessary for assent (tasdiq). The positive stations argue that even if definition and syllogism were used, they do not actually produce certain knowledge. The footnote indicates that in the book, the order begins with the negative station on definitions.

C) المقام الثاني: إن التصديق لا ينال إلا بالقياس
D) المقام الرابع: أن القياس يفيد العلم بالتصديقات

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique has four stations: two negative (refuting necessity of definition and syllogism) and two positive (refuting their sufficiency), and the book begins with the negative station on definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Complete Conceptualization
Ibn Taymiyya argues that tasdiq does not require complete conceptualization through real definition; minimal conceptualization suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's key objection that tasdiq does not require complete conceptualization and evaluate its implications for the role of definition in Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Complete conceptualization is impossible; assent works with partial knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya targets the logicians' claim that complete conceptualization (tasawwur tamm) is necessary for knowledge. He highlights their own admission that minimal conceptualization suffices for assent, then argues that complete conceptualization is unattainable anyway. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for how definitions function in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the logicians' concession
Find where Ibn Taymiyya reports the logicians' view on the relation between tasdiq and definition. What do they admit about the necessity of complete conceptualization for assent?
Look for the phrase 'لا يقف على التصور التام' – 'does not depend on complete conceptualization'.
2
2. Extract the minimal conceptualization claim
According to the logicians, what minimal conceptualization suffices for assent? Specifically, what term does Ibn Taymiyya mention as sufficient?
The term is 'الخاصة' – specific property, not a real definition.
3
3. Understand Ibn Taymiyya's further argument
Ibn Taymiyya adds a stronger point: even the notion of 'complete' conceptualization is flawed. Why is it impossible to have a truly complete conceptualization?
He argues that any conceptualization can be surpassed by a more complete one, and we never conceive all necessary properties of a thing.
4
4. Reflect on the implication for definitions
If complete conceptualization is both unnecessary (per the logicians' own admission) and unattainable, what happens to the role of real definitions (hadd haqiqi) in acquiring knowledge?
This undermines the foundational role of definitions in classical mantiq and suggests that descriptive knowledge (rasm) suffices for many purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهم يقولون : إن التصديق لا يقف على التصور التام الذي يحصل بالحد تركيب فيه الحقيقي، بل يكفي فيه أدنى تصوّر ولو بـ «الخاصة»، وتصوّرُ العقول من هذا الباب . وهذا اعتراف منهم بأن جنس التصور لا يقف على الحد الحقيقي، لكن يقولون: الموقوف عليه هو تصور الحقيقة أو التصور التام. وسنبين إن شاء الله أنه ما من تصور إلا وفوقه تصور أتمُّ منه، وأنا نحن لا نتصور شيئاً بجميع لوازمه حتى لا يشذ عنا منها شيء، وأنه كلما كان التصور لصفات المتصوّر أكثر كان التصور أتم .
And they say: Verily, assent (tasdiq) does not depend on the complete conceptualization (tasawwur tam) that is obtained by the real definition, but rather the minimal conceptualization suffices for it, even if by 'specific property' (khassa). And the conceptualization of intellects is of this category. This is an admission from them that the genus of conceptualization does not depend on real definition, but they say: What it depends on is the conceptualization of the essence or the complete conceptualization. And we will show, God willing, that there is no conceptualization except that above it is a more complete conceptualization, and that we do not conceive anything with all its necessary properties such that none of them escape us, and that the more one conceives of the attributes of the conceived thing, the more complete the conceptualization.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique by highlighting an admission from the logicians themselves. They concede that assent (tasdiq) – the judgment that something is true – does not require a complete conceptualization (tasawwur tam) obtained through a real definition (hadd haqiqi). Instead, even a minimal conceptualization, such as knowing the specific property (khassa) of a thing, suffices for assent. Ibn Taymiyya points out that this undermines their claim that definitions are needed for conceptualization. He then raises a further point: there is no conceptualization that is absolutely complete; we can always conceive more attributes of a thing. Therefore, the notion of a 'complete conceptualization' is relative and unattainable, making the logicians' criterion impossible to satisfy.

Key Terms
التصديق
التصور التام
الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what do the logicians concede about the relationship between assent (tasdiq) and definition?
A) Assent requires the complete conceptualization obtained by definition
B) Assent suffices with minimal conceptualization, even by specific property
C) Assent is impossible without definition
D) Assent and conceptualization are identical

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that the logicians' own admission undermines the necessity of real definition for knowledge; complete conceptualization is both unnecessary and unattainable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the ruling on 'nabidh' (date wine). Using Ibn Taymiyya's point, explain why knowing its intoxicating property (sakr) suffices to declare it forbidden, without needing a complete definition of its essence.
?
Identify a real-world fighi example where a partial description (khassa) is enough to apply a ruling. Write one sentence.

The Ninth Aspect: Perception Through External Senses
Beginning of the ninth aspect: existing things are perceived through external senses or through...

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's alternative source of conceptualization—external senses—and contrast it with the logicians' claim that definitions are the sole source.

Core Idea
External senses are a legitimate source of conceptualization alongside definitions.

Ibn Taymiyya is about to argue that conceptualization does not only come from definitions (hadd), but also from direct sense perception. Although the passage is incomplete, his opening dichotomy sets the stage for a critique of the logicians' over-reliance on definitions. Here we will examine how this epistemological move challenges the foundations of Aristotelian logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the dichotomy
The text says 'existing conceptualized things are either conceived through external senses or...'. What is the alternative likely to be? Ibn Taymiyya's full argument would contrast perception through senses with conceptualization through definitions.
The passage is incomplete; we must infer the missing alternative from Ibn Taymiyya's broader critique.
2
Identify the target of the critique
The logicians claim that all conceptualization comes from definitions. Ibn Taymiyya contends that external senses also provide concepts. Which concepts can only be known through senses?
For example, the concept of 'sweetness' cannot be defined without reference to taste.
3
Assess the epistemic role of senses
In Islamic epistemology, the senses are a valid source of knowledge. How does this relate to the concept of 'tawatur' (mass transmission) in hadith?
Sensory experience is the basis for empirical certainty.
4
Apply the incomplete dichotomy to a real case
Consider the concept of 'sunrise'. Is it better known through definition or direct observation? What does this tell you about the limits of definitions?
This prepares for Ibn Taymiyya's full argument that definitions are not the only path to conceptualization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه التاسع : التاسع : إن الموجودات المتصوَّرة إما أن يتصورها الإنسان بحواسه الظاهرة،
The ninth aspect: The ninth: The existing conceptualized things are either conceived by man through his external senses,
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the beginning of the ninth aspect, but the text cuts off. Ibn Taymiyya is about to present an argument about how existing things are perceived – either through external senses or through internal cognition. The full argument is not available in this passage. We can only note that he is classifying modes of perception, likely to argue that definitions are not the only source of conceptualization.

Key Terms
الحواس الظاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Either through external senses or through other means (not specified)
C) Only through intellectual intuition
D) Only through divine revelation

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya begins his ninth aspect by asserting that external senses are a source of conceptualization, laying the groundwork for his critique of the primacy of definitions in logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Ibn Taymiyya's Circularity Argument Against Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition cannot produce new conception because it presupposes prior knowledge of the definiendum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's circularity argument against the claim that definitions produce new concepts.

Core Idea
Definition cannot give you new knowledge; it only makes explicit what you already know implicitly.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational claim of Aristotelian logic that definition (ḥadd) is the primary tool for acquiring concepts (taṣawwur). He reveals a vicious circle: understanding a definition presupposes prior knowledge of the thing being defined. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Recognize that logicians assert definitions (ḥudūd) are a means to acquire new concepts (taṣawwur) for the learner. Ibn Taymiyya targets this claim.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's diagnosis
He argues that to understand a definition, you must already have some conception of the definiendum. Without that, the words of the definition are meaningless.
Example: If someone defines 'human' as 'rational animal', you must already know what 'human' means in some sense to recognize that the definition applies.
3
Understand the vicious circle (dawr qablī)
The circle: 'He did not conceive the meaning until he heard and understood it, and he could not understand what is meant by the word unless he had conceived that meaning before that.' This makes definition epistemically useless for producing new concepts.
4
Compare with names (asmā')
Ibn Taymiyya parallels definitions with names: both indicate a single meaning, but names require prior acquaintance with the named thing. Definitions are no different; they only provide details of what the name summarizes.
Text: 'just as this is mentioned concerning the indication of names... it is exactly the same concerning the indication of definitions.'
5
Draw the conclusion
Definitions cannot be the source of new conception; they only articulate what is already known summarily. The proper source of new concepts is sensory experience or direct acquaintance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأب والأم لم يعرف دلالة اللفظ عليه. وإذا كان متصوراً لمسمى اللفظ ومعناه قبل استماعه - وإن لم يعرف دلالة اللفظ عليه - امتنع أن يقال: إنه إنما تصوّره باستماع اللفظ، لأن في ذلك دَوْراً قَبْلياً، إذ يستلزم أن يقال : «لم يتصور المعنى حتى سمع وفَهمه، ولم يمكن أن يفهم المراد باللفظ حتى يكون قد تصوّر ذلك المعنى قبل ذلك». وهذا كما أنه مذكور في دلالة الأسماء على مسمياتها المفردة فهو بعينه وارد في دلالة الحدود على المحدودات، إذ كلاهما إنما يدل على معنى مفرد لكن الحد يفيد تفصيل ما دل عليه الاسم بالإجمال.
The father and mother did not know the indication of the word to it. And if one has a conception of the named of the word and its meaning before hearing it - even if he does not know the indication of the word to it - it is impossible to say that he only conceived it by hearing the word, because in that is a prior circularity, since it necessitates saying: 'He did not conceive the meaning until he heard and understood it, and he could not understand what is meant by the word unless he had conceived that meaning before that.' And just as this is mentioned concerning the indication of names to their individual name-bearers, it is exactly the same concerning the indication of definitions to the defined things, since both only indicate a single meaning, but the definition provides a detail of what the name indicates in summary.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is a critical passage in which Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that definitions (ḥudūd) produce new concepts (taṣawwur) for the learner. He identifies a vicious circle (dawr qablī): if a person hears a definition, they must already have some conception of the definiendum in order to understand the words of the definition; otherwise, the definition would be meaningless. For example, if someone defined 'human' as 'rational animal', the listener must already know what 'animal' and 'rational' mean, and more importantly, must already have a prior concept of 'human' to even recognize that the definition is about it. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is exactly the same as the way names (asmā') indicate their bearers: a name like 'Zayd' indicates a particular person, but you already know that person before the name can refer. Definitions are no different; they merely detail (tafṣīl) what the name already summarizes (ijmāl). Thus, definitions cannot be the source of new conception; they only articulate what is already known.

Key Terms
دور قبلي (dawr qablī): A vicious circle where understanding a definition presupposes prior knowledge of the defined term, making the definition epistemically useless. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses this term polemically; later logicians (like al-Akhdari) acknowledge that tasawwur can be acquired through other means but still defend definition as a means of clarification.]
حد (ḥadd): A definition that details (yufaṣṣil) what the name indicates in summary; it does not produce new conception. [Note: In standard logic (e.g., Isaghuji), ḥadd is a statement giving genus and differentia to produce a new concept. Ibn Taymiyya redefines it as merely explicating prior knowledge.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, why can a definition not produce a new concept (tasawwur)?
A) Because definitions are too complex to be understood
B) Because understanding the definition requires prior conception of the definiendum, leading to circularity
C) Because definitions only apply to sensible objects, not abstract concepts
D) Because the logicians do not allow definitions for simple concepts

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's circularity argument shows that definitions cannot be the source of new concepts; they only make explicit what is already implicitly known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take any technical term from an Islamic science (e.g., 'sunnah', 'ijma', 'tawatur') and explain why, according to Ibn Taymiyya, hearing its definition cannot give you the full concept unless you already have some prior acquaintance with it.
?
Write a short dialogue: a logician claims definitions are necessary for new concepts; Ibn Taymiyya responds with his circularity argument. Show how the logician might defend himself.

Ibn Taymiyya's Eighth Objection: Conception Does Not Require Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya argues conception of meanings does not require words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's eighth objection against the necessity of definitions for conception and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
Definitions are words; conception of meanings can occur without words.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents his eighth objection to the logicians' claim that simple concepts can only be conceived through definitions (ḥudūd). He argues that if definitions are verbal statements (qawl al-ḥādd), and conception of meanings does not require words, then the logicians' claim is false. Both speaker and listener can grasp meanings without linguistic expression. This objection strikes at the heart of the logicians' epistemology, questioning whether definitions are truly necessary for thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
What is the claim that Ibn Taymiyya is objecting to? Find it in the text: that simple concepts are only conceived through definitions.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's premise about definitions
He states that the definition is 'qawl al-ḥādd' – the statement of the definer. This reduces definitions to linguistic utterances.
3
Pinpoint his key argument
He asserts that 'conception of meanings does not depend on words' (taṣawwur al-maʿānī lā yaftaqiru ilā al-alfāẓ). This is the core premise that undermines the necessity of verbal definitions.
4
Analyze his supporting examples
He gives two examples: a speaker can conceive the meaning of what he says without uttering the word, and a listener can conceive meaning without any speech at all (e.g., through gestures or context).
5
Evaluate the objection's strength
Consider whether the logicians could respond by arguing that definitions are mental, not merely verbal. Does Ibn Taymiyya's argument still hold if definitions are internal mental constructs?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثامن : إن الحدّ إذا كان هو قول الحادّ فمعلوم أن تصوّر المعاني لا يفتقر إلى الألفاظ . فإن المتكلم قد يتصور معنى ما يقوله بدون لفظ، والمستمع قد يمكنه تصوّر تلك المعنى بدون المعاني من غير تخاطب بالكلية. فكيف يمكن أن يقال : لا تُتصوّر المفردات إلا بالحدّ الذي هو قول الحادّ !
The eighth aspect: that if the definition is the statement of the definer, then it is known that the conception of meanings does not depend on words. For the speaker may conceive the meaning of what he says without a word, and the listener may be able to conceive that meaning without the meanings [words?] without any discourse at all. So how can it be said: 'Simple concepts are not conceived except by the definition, which is the statement of the definer'!
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the eighth of Ibn Taymiyya's objections against the logicians' claim that definitions (ḥudūd) are necessary for conceiving simple concepts (tasawwur al-mufradāt). He argues that if a definition is a verbal statement (qawl al-ḥādd), and conception of meanings does not require words, then it is false to say that conception only occurs through definitions. He points out that a speaker can conceive the meaning of what he says without actually uttering the word; likewise, a listener can understand a meaning without any speech at all (e.g., through gestures or context). Therefore, the logicians' assertion that simple concepts can only be acquired through definitions is untenable. This reinforces his broader critique of the epistemic utility of definitions.

Key Terms
قول الحادّ (qawl al-ḥādd)
تصور المعاني (taṣawwur al-maʿānī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the eighth objection, why can simple concepts be conceived without definitions?
A) Because definitions are only for complex concepts
B) Because conception of meanings does not depend on words
C) Because definitions are always derived from sensory experience
D) Because the logicians admit that definitions are not necessary

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that since conception can occur without language, verbal definitions cannot be the sole path to understanding simple concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Reproduce Ibn Taymiyya's argument in your own words using a different Islamic concept (e.g., 'tawhid' – can it be conceived without a definition?). Does your example support or weaken his objection?
?
2. Consider a counterargument: a logician might reply that definitions are mental representations, not just uttered words. Based on Ibn Taymiyya's text, how could he respond? (Hint: his focus is on words, but could mental definitions also be considered 'qawl'?)

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Ibn Taymiyya's Sensory Epistemology and the Rejection of Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya classifies conception as sensory and denies the need for definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's classification of conception into external and internal sensory sources and explain why definitions are not needed for conception according to his view.

Core Idea
All conception is sensory: external senses for physical objects, internal senses for affective states, and definitions are superfluous.

Ibn Taymiyya contends that the logicians' emphasis on definitions (al-ḥudūd) as the primary route to conception (taṣawwur) is misguided. In this ninth objection, he offers an alternative epistemology: every conceived existent is perceived either through the external senses or through internal sensory faculties. This leads to the powerful conclusion that definitions are not only unnecessary but also impossible for fundamental conceptions—a direct challenge to the foundational role of definitions in Aristotelian logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sensory sources
Read the passage and list the two categories: external senses (al-ḥawāss al-ẓāhira) for physical qualities like taste, color, smell, and bodies; internal senses (al-mashāʿir al-bāṭina) for affective states (wijdiyyāt) such as hunger, love, hatred, knowledge, etc.
The internal senses here are not the standard Avicennan five but rather a broader set including emotions and cognitive states.
2
Distinguish particular and universal conception
Ibn Taymiyya notes that each of these sensory experiences can be conceived as a particular (muʿayyan) or as an absolute/universal (muṭlaq/ʿāmm). Observe that universals are derived from particular sensory instances, not from definitions.
3
Extract the key claim about definitions
Find the sentence: 'And all these conceptions are independent of definition' (wa hādhihi al-taṣawwurāt jamīʿuhā ghanīya ʿan al-ḥadd). Note that Ibn Taymiyya asserts that definitions are not needed for any conception.
This contradicts the logicians' doctrine that definitions are necessary for acquiring conceptions.
4
Understand how the unseen is known
Ibn Taymiyya adds that anything not directly sensed (al-ghāʾib) is known through analogy (qiyās) and consideration (iʿtibār) of what has been witnessed (shāhid). This completes the empirical epistemology: all knowledge originates in sensory experience, either directly or via analogical extension.
Here qiyās refers to juristic analogy, not the categorical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه التاسع : إن الموجودات المتصوَّرة إما أن يتصورها الإنسان بحواسه الظاهرة، كالطعم، واللون والريح والأجسام التي تحمل هذه الصفات؛ وإما أن يتصورها بمشاعره الباطنة كما يَتَصَوَّر الأمور الحسية الباطنة الوجدية، مثل الجوع والسبع، والعشق، والحب والبغض، والفرح والحزن، واللذة والألم، والإرادة والكراهية والعلم والجهل، وأمثال ذلك. وكل من الأمرين قد يتصوره معيّناً، وقد يتصوره مطلقاً أو عاماً. وهذه التصورات جميعها غنيّة عن الحد ولا يمكنه تصور شيء بدون مشاعره الظاهرة والباطنة وما غاب عنه يعرفه بالقياس والاعتبار بما شاهده .
The ninth aspect: that the conceived existents are either conceived by man through his external senses, like taste, color, smell, and the bodies that carry these attributes; or he conceives them through his internal senses as he conceives the internal sensory affective matters, like hunger, satiety, passionate love, love and hatred, joy and sadness, pleasure and pain, will and aversion, knowledge and ignorance, and the like. And each of the two may be conceived as particular, or may be conceived as absolute or universal. And all these conceptions are independent of definition, and he cannot conceive anything without his external and internal senses, and what is absent from him he knows by analogy (qiyās) and consideration (iʿtibār) of what he has witnessed.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the ninth objection against the logicians. Ibn Taymiyya argues that all objects of conception (al-mawjūdāt al-mutaṣawwara) are perceived either through the external senses (al-ḥawāss al-ẓāhira) or through the internal senses (al-mashāʿir al-bāṭina). External senses perceive things like taste, color, smell, and physical bodies. Internal senses perceive internal affective states (wijdiyyāt) such as hunger, satiety, love, hatred, joy, sadness, pleasure, pain, will, aversion, knowledge, ignorance, etc. Each of these can be conceived either as a particular instance (muʿayyan) or as an absolute/general concept (muṭlaq or ʿāmm). Ibn Taymiyya then asserts that all these conceptions are independent of definitions (ghaniyya ʿan al-ḥadd). Moreover, a person cannot conceive anything at all without recourse to his senses. For things not directly sensed (ghāʾib), knowledge is acquired through analogy (qiyās) and consideration (iʿtibār) based on what has been witnessed. This radical empiricism aims to show that definitions, as verbal constructs, are not the source of conception; rather, sensory experience (both external and internal) is the foundation.

Key Terms
مشاعر باطنة (mashāʿir bāṭina)
قياس (qiyās)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the ninth aspect, how does a person conceive a universal concept like 'knowledge'?
A) Through a definition of knowledge provided by logicians
B) Through internal sense experience of particular instances of knowledge
C) Through divine revelation alone
D) Through the combination of external senses only

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's ninth objection establishes that all conception originates in sensory experience (external or internal), rendering definitions superfluous—an empirical challenge to the logicians' epistemological hierarchy.

The Relativity of Intuitive and Acquired Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya argues the badihi/nadari distinction is relative, not inherent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the absolute distinction between badihi and nadari, and identify examples of relativity in Islamic knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge being intuitive or acquired depends on the knower, not the known.

In classical logic, knowledge is divided into intuitive (badihi) and acquired (nadari) as if these categories are inherent. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this, arguing that what is intuitive for one person may be theoretical for another. This relativity has profound implications for how we understand learning and definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrine under attack
Ibn Taymiyya targets the logicians' claim that the distinction between intuitive and acquired knowledge is an inherent, fixed property of propositions. He calls this a 'great error' and 'contrary to reality'.
The logicians believe that if a proposition is badihi, it is badihi for everyone; similarly for nadari.
2
Extract the examples of relativity
Ibn Taymiyya provides several examples: Zayd may be certain of what 'Amr conjectures; something may be sensory for one person and report-based for another; some know via tawatur, others via conjecture. Notice how the same truth is classified differently across individuals.
These examples show that the classification depends on the subject's circumstances, not the object.
3
Understand the concept of idafi (relative)
Ibn Taymiyya uses the term idafi to describe the intuitive/acquired distinction. This means the attribute is relational, not essential. Just as a proposition can be certain for one and conjectural for another, so too can a concept be intuitive for one and theoretical for another.
The term idafi is key to undermining the logicians' absolute categories.
4
Reflect on the epistemological significance
If the distinction is relative, then the logicians' universal claim that 'all concepts require definitions except axioms' collapses. What is axiom for one may require proof for another. This opens the door for multiple pathways to knowledge.
This argument prepares the ground for Ibn Taymiyya's later critique of definitions as necessary for conceptualization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : كون العلم بديهياً أو نظرياً من الأمور الاضافية وليس من لوازم المعلومات . وأما المعارضة للحدّ بحدّ آخر فظاهر . فإذا كان المستمع للحد يُبطله بـ«النقض» تارة، وبـ«المعارضة» أخرى، ومعلوم أن كليهما لا يمكن إلا بعد تصور المحدودِ عُلم أنه يمكن تصوّر المحدود بدون الحدّ . وهو المطلوب. الحادي عشر : أن يقال: هم معترفون بأن من التصورات ما يكون بديهياً لا يحتاج إلى حد، وإلا لزم الدَّوْر أو التسلسل . وحينئذ فيقال: كون العلم بديهياً أو نظرياً هو من الأمور النسبية الإضافية، مثل كون «القضية يقينية أو ظنّيّة، إذ قد يتيقّن زيد ما يظنه عمرو، وقد يبدَه زيداً من المعاني ما لا يعرفه غيره إلا بالنظر، وقد يكون حسّياً لزيد من العلوم ما هو خَبَري عند عمرو. وإن كان كثير من الناس يحسب أن كون العلم المعيَّن ضرورياً أو كسبيّاً، أو بديهياً، أو نظرياً، هو من الأمور اللازمة له بحيث يشترك في ذلك جميع الناس. وهذا غلط عظيم، وهو مخالف للواقع . فإن مَنْ رأى الأمور الموجودة في مكانه وزمانه كانت عنده من الحسيات / المشاهدات ، وهي عند مَنْ علمها بالتواتر من المتواترات، وقد يكون بعض الناس إنما علمها بخبر ظنّي فتكون عنده من باب الظنّيّات، ومَنْ لم يسمعها فهي عنده من المجهولات. وكذلك العقليات، فإن الناس يتفاوتون في الإدراك تفاوتاً لا يكاد ينضبطُ طَرَفاه. ولبعضهم من العلم البديهي عنده والضروري ما ينفيه غيره، أو يشكّ فيه . وهذا بين في التصورات و التصديقات. وإذا كان ذلك من الأمور النسبية والإضافية أمكن أن يكون بديهياً عند بعض الناس من التصوّرات ما ليس بديهياً لغيره فلا يحتاج إلى حد، وهذا هو الواقع. وإذا قيل : فمن لم يحصل له تلك المحدودات بالبديهة حصلت له بالحدّ ، قيل : كثير منهم يجعل هذا حكماً عاماً في جنس النظريات الجنس الناس، وهذا خطأ واضح. ومن تفطّن لما ذكرناه يقال له : ذلك الشخص الذي لم يعلمها بالبديهة يمكن أن تصير بديهية له بمثل الأسباب التي حصلت لغيره، فلا يجوز أن يقال : لا يعلمها إلا بالحدود.
The first station: knowledge being intuitive or acquired is among relative matters and not among the necessary attributes of the known. As for opposing a definition with another definition, it is obvious. For if the listener of the definition invalidates it sometimes by 'invalidation' and other times by 'opposition', and it is known that both are impossible except after conceptualizing the defined, it is known that conceptualizing the defined is possible without the definition. That is what is sought. The eleventh point: it is said: They admit that among concepts there are what are intuitive and do not need a definition, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow. Then it is said: Knowledge being intuitive or acquired is among relative, relational matters, like a proposition being certain or conjectural, for Zayd may be certain of what 'Amr conjectures, and meanings may occur suddenly to Zayd that others only know through reasoning, and some knowledge may be sensory for Zayd while it is report-based for 'Amr. Although many people think that a particular knowledge being necessary or acquired, or intuitive or theoretical, is among its necessary attributes such that all people share in it. This is a great error, and it contradicts reality. For whoever sees things existing in his place and time, they are among the sensory/observed things for him, while for those who know them through tawatur they are among the mutawatirat, and some people may only know them through conjectural reports, so they are for him among conjectural things, and whoever has not heard them, they are for him among unknowns. And likewise for rational matters, for people differ in perception in a way whose extremes can hardly be captured. Some have intuitive and necessary knowledge that others deny or doubt. This is clear in concepts and assents. And if that is among relative and relational matters, it is possible that some concepts are intuitive for some people and not intuitive for others, so they do not need a definition. This is the reality. And if it is said: Whoever has not obtained those defined things intuitively, he obtains them through definition. It is said: Many of them make this a general ruling concerning the genus of theoretical matters and the genus of people, and this is an obvious error. And whoever reflects on what we have mentioned, it is said to him: That person who did not know them intuitively can become intuitive for him through the same causes that occurred for others, so it cannot be said: He only knows them through definitions.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument from Ibn Taymiyya critiquing the logicians' claim that definitions are necessary for conceptualizing all concepts. The author opens by stating that the distinction between intuitive (badihi) and acquired (nadari) knowledge is relative, not a necessary attribute of the known things themselves. He then connects this to the issue of definitions: if a listener can invalidate a definition by invalidation (naqd) or opposition (mu'arada), both of which require prior conceptualization of the defined, then it is possible to conceptualize the defined without a definition. This is the desired conclusion. He then presents the eleventh argument: the logicians themselves admit that some concepts are intuitive and need no definition, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would ensue. Given that, he insists that the intuitive/acquired distinction is relative, like certainty vs. conjecture. He gives examples: Zayd may be certain of what 'Amr conjectures; someone may know something through sensory perception while another knows it through tawatur (widespread transmission) or conjecture. Many people mistakenly think the distinction is universal and inherent. In reality, what is intuitive for one may be acquired for another. Therefore, if someone does not have a concept intuitively, it can become intuitive for him through the same means that made it intuitive for others, not necessarily through a definition. The error is in assuming definitions are the sole means.

Key Terms
بديهي
نظري
اضافي
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the fundamental error in the logicians' classification of knowledge as intuitive or acquired?
A) They deny that any knowledge is intuitive.
B) They treat the classification as absolute and inherent, whereas it is relative.
C) They claim that definitions are never needed for conceptualization.
D) They ignore the role of sensory knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive/acquired distinction is relative to the knower, not an inherent property of the known.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

10 min
Why Definitions Are Not Necessary for Conceptualization
Ibn Taymiyya argues that conceptualization is possible without definitions, using naqd and mu'arada as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that conceptualization can occur without a definition, using the examples of naqd and mu'arada.

Core Idea
If you can invalidate a definition, you already understand the defined.

Logicians often claim that definitions are required to conceptualize any non-intuitive concept. Ibn Taymiyya cleverly counters by pointing out that the very acts of invalidating (naqd) or opposing (mu'arada) a definition presuppose that one already has a conceptualization of the thing being defined. This argument, combined with the relativity of intuitive knowledge, shows that definitions are not the only path to conceptualization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about definitions
The logicians assert that all concepts that are not intuitive must be obtained through definitions (hadd). Without a definition, the concept remains unknown. Ibn Taymiyya targets this claim.
This is the standard Aristotelian position: definition produces conceptualization (tasawwur).
2
Understand naqd and mu'arada
Ibn Taymiyya refers to two types of objections: naqd (invalidation) and mu'arada (opposition). When someone hears a definition, they may challenge it by showing it fails to include or exclude properly (naqd) or by presenting an alternative definition (mu'arada).
These are terms from the science of munazara (dialectics).
3
Recognize the logical necessity of prior conceptualization
In order to invalidate or oppose a definition, the objector must already understand the concept being defined. How else could they know that the definition is deficient? Therefore, conceptualization must be possible without the definition.
This is a powerful reductio: if the definition were necessary for conceptualization, then challenging it would be impossible.
4
Link to the relativity of intuitive knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya then adds that what is intuitive for one person may be theoretical for another. So even if a concept is not intuitive for me, it may become intuitive through other means (e.g., experience, sense, testimony) without requiring a formal definition.
Thus, the logicians' monopoly on definitions is broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ibn Taymiyyah's Refutation of Ghazali on Definition
Ibn Taymiyyah refutes Ghazali on the difficulty of definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyyah's critique of the logician's definitional theory and distinguish between taswir and tamayyiz as the purpose of definition.

Core Idea
Definitions do not depict essences; they only distinguish.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyyah directly challenges al-Ghazali's claim that definitions are difficult because they require depicting the essence of the defined. He argues that this supposed difficulty is based on an impossible goal, and that the true function of definition is simply to provide distinction (tamayyiz). This critique has major implications for how definitions are used in the Islamic sciences, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ghazali's Claim
Ghazali argues that definitions are difficult because they must depict the essence of the defined using specific conditions (e.g., essential attributes). Ibn Taymiyyah acknowledges the difficulty if that were the goal, but he denies the validity of the goal.
Focus on the phrase 'what he mentioned about the difficulty of definition under the conditions he stated is true if the purpose is depiction.'
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyyah's Refutation
He asserts that depiction (taswir) is impossible via mere definition. He also rejects the distinction between essential constitutive attributes and external attributes as baseless. Thus, the logicians' framework is flawed.
Look at the sentence: 'What they mentioned about the difference between essential constitutive attributes... is false and has no reality.'
3
Identify the Alternative Purpose: Tamayyiz
Ibn Taymiyyah states that all definitions only serve to distinguish (tamayyiz). This can be achieved by differentia (fasl) or property (khassa) alone, without the impossible requirement of depicting essence.
The key line: 'And all the definitions they mention only serve to distinguish.'
4
Contrast with Mutakallimun Method
Ibn Taymiyyah argues that the method of the mutakallimun (theologians) is more correct because it does not insist on impossible conditions. He responds to Ghazali's objection that this method is far from the purpose, noting that no definition achieves depiction anyway.
Notice the endorsement: 'the method of the mutakallimun is more correct in definitions for achieving the correct purpose.'

Source Text

قلت : ما ذكره من صعوبة الحدّ على الشروط التي ذَكَرها حق لو كان المقصود بالحد تصوير المحدود كما يدَّعونه وكان ذلك ممكناً، لكنّ ما ذكروه في الحد باطل، فإنه يمتنع [۲۰] أن يحصل بمجرد الحد تصوير المحدود . وما ذكروه من الفرق بين الصفات الذاتية المقوّمة الداخلة في الماهية والصفاتِ الخارجة اللازمة أمر باطل لا حقيقة له. وما أوجبوه من ذكر الصفتين في الحدود هو مما يحظره المتكلمون فيمتنعون منه في الحد. والتحقيق أنه لا واجب ولا محظور، كما قد بيناه في موضع آخر . وكل ما يذكرونه من الحدود فإنما يفيد التمييز، وإذا كان لا يحصل بالحد إلا التمييز فالتمييز قد يحصل بـ «الفصل» و«الخاصة». فعلم أن طريقة المتكلمين أسد في الحدود اسد تحصيل المقصود الصحيح بالحدود. وأما قوله : إن ذلك في غاية البعد عن غَرَض التعريف لذات المحدود» فيقال: وكذلك سائر الحدود هي غير محصّلة لتصوير ذات المحدود، كما سنبينه إن شاء الله تعالى. وما ذكره من الوجوه حجةٌ عليهم .
I say: What he mentioned about the difficulty of definition under the conditions he stated is true if the purpose of definition is to depict the defined as they claim and that were possible, but what they mentioned about definition is false, because it is impossible that mere definition yields a depiction of the defined. And what they mentioned about the difference between essential constitutive attributes entering into the quiddity and external necessary attributes is false and has no reality. And what they required of mentioning two attributes in definitions is something that the mutakallimun prohibit and refrain from in definition. And the truth is that there is neither obligation nor prohibition, as we have explained elsewhere. And all the definitions they mention only serve to distinguish. And if nothing but distinction is obtained by definition, then distinction may be obtained by 'differentia' and 'property.' So it is known that the method of the mutakallimun is more correct in definitions for achieving the correct purpose of definitions. As for his statement: 'That is extremely far from the purpose of defining the essence of the defined,' it is said: Likewise, all definitions do not achieve a depiction of the essence of the defined, as we will explain, God willing. And what he mentioned of the aspects is an argument against them.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This is a critical passage where Ibn Taymiyyah refutes al-Ghazali's claim that definitions are difficult because they require depicting the essence. He argues that definitions only provide distinction (tamayyiz), not depiction of essence. He rejects the logicians' distinction between essential and accidental attributes. He asserts that the method of the mutakallimun is superior because they do not require the impossible. He also responds to Ghazali's objection that this method is far from the goal of defining the essence, noting that no definition achieves depiction of essence.

Definitions serve only to distinguish, not to depict essences; the mutakallimun method is superior because it does not demand the impossible.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Essential-Necessary Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logical distinction between essential property and necessary accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the distinction between essential (dhati) and necessary accident (lazim) is merely conventional and assess its implications for the validity of definitions in logic.

Core Idea
The dhati/lazim distinction is not real; definitions based on it are invalid.

In this passage from his *Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin*, Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of the logicians' art of definition. He targets the distinction between essential properties (dhati) and necessary accidents (lazim), which underlies the theory of definition. He argues that this distinction is not a real feature of the world but a mere convention (wad') imposed by the logicians. If this foundation is shaky, the entire edifice of real definitions collapses. Let us walk through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the logicians' claim that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking. He quotes al-Ghazali saying that the inseparable necessary accident (lazim) is easily confused with the essential (dhati). But Ibn Taymiyya's critique goes further: he says that in truth there is no real difference between them at all.
The logicians' distinction is meant to separate properties that constitute the quiddity from those that always accompany it but are not part of the definition.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Ibn Taymiyya states that the difference is only by 'mere convention and terminology' (mujarrad al-wad' wa al-istilah). He accuses the logicians of arbitrarily distinguishing between similar things and equating different things. This convention, he says, contradicts clear reason.
For Ibn Taymiyya, a real distinction must be based on what things are in themselves, not on the choice of a positor.
3
Trace the consequence for definitions
Since the entire art of definition (sina'at al-hudud) rests on this distinction, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the art is invalid. Definitions that claim to capture real essences are in fact based on an arbitrary convention.
This is a radical critique: it undermines the logicians' claim to provide certain knowledge through definitions.
4
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's alternative
Ibn Taymiyya explains that what logicians call 'essence' (mahiyya) is merely a set of conceived meanings (ma'ani mutasawwara) in the mind. For example, conceiving a body as growing, sentient, willing, rational, and laughing—each part is just a mental concept. The mahiyya depends on the mind, not on external reality.
This nominalist view denies that definitions can capture mind-independent essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: وأما الوجه الثاني: فقوله: (اللازم) الذي لا يفارق في الوجود والوهم يشتبه بـ (الذاتي) غاية الاشتباه» كلام صحيح، بل ليس بينهما في الحقيقة فرق إلا بمجرد الوضع والاصطلاح، كما قد بين في غير هذا الموضع وبين أنهم في هذا الوضع المنطقي والاصطلاح المنطقي فرقوا بين المتماثلين وسووا بين المختلفين. وهذا وضع مخالف لصريح العقل، وهو أصل صناعة الحدود الحقيقية عندهم، فتكون صناعة باطلة، إذ الفرق بين الحقائق لا يكون بمجرد أمر وضعي، بل بما هي عليه الحقائق في نفسها وليس بين ما سموه ذاتياً وما سموه لازماً للماهية في الوجود والذهن فرق حقيقي في الخارج، وإنما هي فروق اعتبارية تتبع الوضع واختيار الواضع وما يفرضه في ذهنه. وهذا قد بسطناه في غير هذا الموضع، وذكرنا ألفاظ أئمتهم في هذا الموضع وتفسيرها. وإن حاصل ما عندهم أن ما يسمونه ماهية هي تلك الأمور المتصورة. فإذا تصور جسماً نامياً، حسّاساً، متحركاً بالإرادة، ناطقاً، أو ضاحكاً، وكل جزء من هذه الأجزاء هو المعنى المتصور، فالماهية هي ما يتصور الذهن، فإن أجزاء الماهية تابعة لما يتصوره الإنسان، لا للحقائق.
The second aspect: As for the second aspect, his saying: 'The necessary accident which does not separate in existence and in the imagination is confused with the essential to the utmost confusion' is correct speech. Rather, there is in truth no difference between them except by mere convention and terminology, as has been explained elsewhere. And he explained that they in this logical convention and logical terminology made a distinction between two similar things and equated between two different things. And this convention is contrary to clear reason, and it is the foundation of the art of real definitions according to them, so the art becomes invalid. For the distinction between realities is not by a mere conventional matter, but by what the realities are in themselves. And there is no real external difference between what they called essential and what they called necessary for the essence in existence and the mind; rather they are only notional distinctions following convention and the choice of the positor and what he supposes in his mind. And we have expounded this elsewhere, and mentioned the words of their leaders in this regard and their interpretation. And the sum of what they have is that what they call 'essence' are those conceived things. So if one conceives a body, growing, sentient, moving by will, rational, or laughing, and every part of these parts is the conceived meaning, then the essence is what the mind conceives; for the parts of the essence depend on what the human conceives, not on realities.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the second of al-Ghazali's points in his work on definitions. Al-Ghazali had stated that the inseparable necessary accident (al-lazim) is extremely confused with the essential (al-dhati). Ibn Taymiyya agrees that they are confused, but his critique is that there is no real difference between them—only a conventional (wad'i) distinction. According to Ibn Taymiyya, the logicians arbitrarily distinguish between similar things and equate different things. This convention (wad') contradicts clear reason. Since the entire art of definition (sina'at al-hudud) is based on this distinction, the art is invalid. He argues that real distinctions (furq bayn al-haqa'iq) must be based on what things are in themselves, not on a positor's choice. He then explains that what logicians call 'essence' (mahiyya) is merely a set of conceived meanings (ma'ani mutasawwara). For example, one can conceive 'body, growing, sentient, willing, rational, laughing'—all parts are merely mental concepts. Thus the mahiyya is dependent on the mind's conception, not on external realities. This undermines the claim that definitions capture real essences.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Mahiyya as Mental Conception
Explains that mahiyya (essence) follows mental conception, not external realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique that mahiyya is a mental construct and contrast it with the logicians' claim that mahiyya corresponds to real essences.

Core Idea
Mahiyya is not a reality but a mental image.

Logicians treat mahiyya (essence) as the real quiddity of a thing, defined by genus and differentia. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this mahiyya is nothing but the collection of mental pictures formed in the mind. In this passage, he deconstructs the logicians' account of definitional denotation (mutabaqa, tadamun, iltizam) to show that it all depends on what a person conceives, not on external realities. Understanding this critique is key to seeing how definitions in Islamic sciences are grounded in mental concepts, not Platonic forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
According to Ibn Taymiyya's summary, what does the term 'mahiyya' refer to in the logicians' system? Answer: the collection of conceived mental images.
2
Trace the parts of mental conception
The author gives an example: conceiving a body that is growing, sentient, etc. How does each part relate to the conception? Answer: each part is included in the conception.
3
Understand the three denotations
The passage equates the whole mahiyya with mutabaqa (correspondence), the constituent part with tadamun (implication), and the necessary concomitant with iltizam (commitment). But note: these are applied to mental images, not real things.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author states that this entire structure follows what man conceives, whether it corresponds to reality or not. Therefore, mahiyya is not dependent on the realities themselves. What does this imply for the logicians' concept of essence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنّ حاصل ما عندهم أن ما يسمونه «ماهية» هي تلك الأمور المتصوّرة. فإذا تصور جسماً نامياً، حسّاساً، متحركاً بالإرادة، ناطقاً، أو ضاحكاً وكل جزء من هذه الأجزاء هو المعنى المتصوّر، وكان داخلاً في هذا المتصوّر. وإن تصور حيواناً ناطقاً كان أيضاً كل منهما جزءاً مما تصوره، داخلاً فيه. وكان ما يلزم هذه الصورة الذهنية مثل كونه «حياً» و«حساساً» و«نامياً» هو لازماً لهذا المتصوّر في الذهن. فالماهية بمنزلة المدلول عليه بـ «المطابقة»، وجزؤها المقوّم لها الداخل فيها الذي هو وصف ذاتي لها، بمنزلة المدلول عليه بـ «التضمّن»؛ واللازم لها الخارج عنها، بمنزلة المدلول عليه بـ «الالتزام». ومعلوم أن هذا أمر يتبع ما يتصوره الإنسان، سواء كان مطابقاً أو غير مطابق، ليس هو تابعاً للحقائق في نفسها.
And the summary of what they hold is that what they call 'essence' (mahiyya) are those conceived things. So if one conceives a body that is growing, sentient, moving by will, rational, or laughing, and every part of these parts is the conceived meaning and is included in this conception. And if one conceives a rational animal, then each of the two is also a part of what he conceived, included in it. And what necessarily follows this mental image, such as its being 'living', 'sentient', 'growing', is a necessary concomitant for this conception in the mind. Thus the essence is like the denoted by 'correspondence' (mutabaqa), and its constituent part that is internal to it and is an essential description of it is like the denoted by 'implication' (tadamun), and the necessary concomitant external to it is like the denoted by 'commitment' (iltizam). And it is known that this is something that follows what man conceives, whether it corresponds [to reality] or not; it is not dependent on the realities themselves.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage explains the logicians' view of mahiyya (essence) and then criticizes it. The author summarizes that for the logicians, the mahiyya is nothing but the collection of conceived mental images. For example, if one conceives a body with the attributes of growing, sentient, moving by will, rational, or laughing, each of these attributes is a part of the conception and is internal to it. Similarly, if one conceives 'rational animal', both genus and differentia are parts of the conception. The necessary concomitants such as 'living', 'sentient', 'growing' are mental luwazim (necessary consequents) of this mental image. The author then draws an analogy: the mahiyya (essence) as a whole is like the meaning denoted by the word 'correspondence' (mutabaqa), i.e., the full definition. Its constituent essential parts are like the meaning denoted by 'implication' (tadamun), i.e., the parts of the definition. The necessary concomitant external to the essence is like the meaning denoted by 'commitment' (iltizam), i.e., necessary effects. The author criticizes that this entire structure is dependent on what a person conceives in his mind, regardless of whether that conception corresponds to external reality or not. Thus, the logicians' mahiyya is not a real essence but a mental construct. An example: when a jurist defines 'khamr' as 'intoxicating beverage', the mental conception includes 'intoxicating' and 'beverage', but the external reality is independent of that mental picture.

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya on Mental Denotation and the Logicians' Error
Ibn Taymiyya analyzes mental denotation and the misidentification of mental with real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's three types of mental denotation (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and explain why he considers the logicians' confusion of mental with real to be the root error.

Core Idea
The logicians' core mistake: treating mental definitions as if they directly correspond to external reality.

Ibn Taymiyya here dissects the logicians' theory of definition. He shows that their threefold division of denotation (correspondence, inclusion, implication) is purely mental. It describes how concepts relate in the mind, not how things exist outside. Understanding this distinction is crucial to grasping his larger critique of logic as a conventional, not essential, science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of mental denotation
Find in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya explains mutabaqa (المطابقة), tadammun (التضمن), and iltizam (الالتزام). Note how each corresponds to a part of the mental conception: the whole quiddity, its constitutive parts, and necessary external attributes.
2
Understand the mental nature of this structure
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says that this denotation follows what a human conceives, whether it corresponds to reality or not. It is not dependent on external realities. Underline this claim in the translation.
3
Locate the root error
Find the phrase 'the root of the error of these people is that they confused what is in minds with what is in external existents.' This is the core accusation.
4
Apply to the example of defining 'human'
Ibn Taymiyya gives the definition 'body, sensitive, growing, moving by will, rational.' He says each term has a meaning that is a part of the statement. Explain why he considers this definition mental rather than a real description of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان داخلاً في هذا المتصوّر. وإن تصور حيواناً ناطقاً كان أيضاً كل منهما جزءاً مما تصوره، داخلاً فيه. وكان ما يلزم هذه الصورة الذهنية مثل كونه «حيا» و«حساساً» و«نامياً هو لازماً لهذا المتصوّر في الذهن. فالماهية بمنزلة المدلول عليه بـ «المطابقة»، وجزؤها المقوّمُ لها الداخلُ فيها الذي هو وصف ذاتي لها، بمنزلة المدلول عليه بـ «التضمّن»؛ واللازم لها الخارج عنها، بمنزلة المدلول عليه بـ «الالتزام». ومعلوم أن هذا أمر يتبع ما يتصوره الإنسانُ، سواء كان مطابقاً أو غير مطابق، ليس هو تابعاً للحقائق في نفسها . (1) وأصل غلط هؤلاء أنه اشتبه عليهم ما في الأذهان بما في الأعيان، فالإنسان إذا حد شيئاً كـ «الإنسان» مثلاً فقال : هو جسم حسّاس، نام، متحرّك بالإرادة، ناطق» كان هذا القول له لفظ و معنى . فكل لفظ من ألفاظه له معنى من هذه المعاني - جزء هذا الكلام
And it was included in this mental conception. If one conceives of a rational animal, each of them is also a part of what he conceived, included in it. And what is necessary for this mental form, like its being 'alive', 'sensitive', and 'growing', is a necessary attribute of this mental conception. So the quiddity is like the denotation by 'correspondence'; its constitutive part that is internal to it, which is an essential description of it, is like the denotation by 'inclusion'; and the necessary attribute external to it is like the denotation by 'implication'. And it is known that this is a matter that follows what a human conceives, whether it corresponds (to reality) or does not correspond; it is not dependent on the realities in themselves. (1) The root of the error of these people is that they confused what is in minds with what is in external existents. So when a person defines something, like 'human', saying: 'He is a body, sensitive, growing, moving by will, rational' — this statement has a wording and a meaning. Every wording of its words has a meaning from these meanings — a part of this statement.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of how logicians treat mental concepts. Ibn Taymiyya begins by describing the mental image of a thing and its parts. If one conceives of 'animal' or 'rational animal', each part (like 'body', 'sensitive', 'rational') is included in the mental conception. Necessary attributes like 'alive' or 'sensitive' are also inherent in that mental image. He then draws an analogy to the modes of verbal denotation: the whole quiddity corresponds to 'matching' (mutabaqa), the essential parts correspond to 'inclusion' (tadammun), and the external necessary attributes correspond to 'implication' (iltizam). Crucially, he stresses that this entire structure is mental and does not necessarily reflect external reality; it is merely a construct of the mind. The fundamental error of the logicians, he asserts, is that they confuse these mental constructs with actual external things. He provides an example: when someone defines 'human' as 'body, sensitive, growing, moving by will, rational', each term has a meaning, but that meaning is a mental part, not a real part of the essence. This sets up his critique of the logicians' theory of definition as a conventional, not real, art.

Definitions Cannot Produce New Concepts
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions cannot produce knowledge of the defined, similar to names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the epistemological role of definitions and identify the circularity objection.

Core Idea
Definitions, like names, only indicate what is already known; they cannot create new concepts.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that definitions (ḥudūd) produce conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur). In this passage, he draws a parallel between definitions and names, arguing that both merely point to something already conceptualized. Understanding this critique is essential for evaluating the role of definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that there is no dispute among rational people that a mere name does not cause a depiction of the named for someone who has not already conceptualized it.
This is the foundational premise.
2
Trace the analogy between name and definition
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya argues that the indication of a definition is like the indication of a name: both require prior conceptualization of what they refer to.
The key analogy: 'dilālat al-ḥadd ka-dilālat al-ism'.
3
Unpack the circularity argument
Understand why Ibn Taymiyya says that if names were meant to convey meanings, circularity (dawr) would follow: you would need to know the meaning to know the assignment, and vice versa.
This applies to definitions as well.
4
Formulate the conclusion
Synthesize: since the defined is conceptualized before the definition, the definition cannot produce new conceptual knowledge—it only indicates what is already known.
The conclusion is: 'imtanaʿa an tataṣawwara al-mahdūdāt bi-mujarrad al-ḥudūd'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذن فلا نزاع بين العقلاء أن مجرّد الاسم لا يوجب تصوير المسمى لمن لم يتصوره بدون ذلك، وإنما الاسم يفيد الدلالة عليه والإشارة إليه . ولهذا قالوا : إن المقصود باللغات ليس هو دلالة اللفظ المفرد على معناه. وذلك لأن اللفظ المفرد لا يُعرف دلالته على المعنى إن لم يُعرف أنه موضوع له، ولا يُعرف أنه حتى يتصوّر اللفظ والمعنى. فلو كان المقصود بالوضع استفادة معاني الألفاظ المفردة لزم الدور. وإذا لم يكن المقصود من الأسماء تصوير معانيها المفردة، ودلالة الحد كدلالة الاسم، لم يكن المقصود من الحدّ تصوير معناه المفرد وإذا كان دلالة الاسم على مسمّاه مسبوقاً بتصور مسمّاه وَجَبَ أن تكون دلالة الحدّ على المحدود مسبوقاً بتصور المحدود. وإذا كان كل من المحدود والمسمَّى متصوّراً بدون الاسم والحد، وكان تصور المسمّى والمحدود [۲۹] مشترطاً في دلالة الحدّ والاسم على معناه، امتنع أن تتصور المحدودات بمجرد الحدود ، كما يمتنع تصور المسمّيات بمجرد الأسماء. وهذا هو المطلوب .
Thus there is no dispute among rational people that the mere name does not cause a depiction of the named for one who has not conceptualized it without that; rather the name only indicates it and points to it. For this reason they said: the purpose of languages is not the indication of the single word to its meaning. That is because the meaning of the single word is not known unless it is known that it is assigned (to that meaning), and it is not known that it is assigned until the word and meaning are conceptualized. So if the purpose of assignment were to obtain the meanings of single words, circularity would follow. And if the purpose of names is not to depict their individual meanings, and the indication of the definition is like the indication of the name, then the purpose of the definition is not to depict its individual meaning. And if the indication of the name to its named is preceded by conceptualization of the named, then necessarily the indication of the definition to the defined is preceded by conceptualization of the defined. And if both the defined and the named are conceptualized without the name and the definition, and the conceptualization of the named and defined is a condition for the indication of the definition and the name to their meaning, then it is impossible to conceptualize the defined things by mere definitions, just as it is impossible to conceptualize the named things by mere names. This is what is required.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logical claim that definitions (ḥudūd) produce conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) of the defined. Ibn Taymiyya begins by establishing consensus: a name alone does not create a concept of the named for someone who does not already know it. The name only serves to point and indicate. He then draws an analogy: since the purpose of language is not to convey meanings of single words (otherwise circularity would arise because you'd need to know the word and meaning to know it's assigned), similarly the purpose of definitions is not to depict the meaning of the defined term. Just as a name's indication presupposes prior conceptualization of the named, a definition's indication presupposes prior conceptualization of the defined. Therefore, both the defined and the named are known without the definition or name, and their conceptualization is a condition for the definition/name to function. Hence, definitions cannot produce new concepts—they only point to what is already known. This refutes the logicians' claim that definitions are a path to new conceptual knowledge.

Key Terms
حد
اسم
دور

A) Because definitions are too vague.

C) Because definitions are only for experts.
D) Because definitions use unfamiliar terms.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that definitions cannot be sources of new conceptual knowledge; they only indicate what is already understood.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
advanced
8 min
Critique of the logicians' pure concept (tasawwur) without truth value
Ibn Taymiyya argues that a definition is not a mere term but a complete sentence, and that a concept without affirmation or negation is not knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' view that a concept without truth value constitutes knowledge, and apply his requirement that a definition must be a complete sentence.

Core Idea
A bare concept without affirmation or negation is mere fantasy, not knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at the logicians' claim that a definition (hadd) can be a mere term or a concept stripped of any truth value. He argues that for a definition to be meaningful, it must be a complete sentence (a predicate with an omitted subject), and that a concept without any judgment about its existence or impossibility is not knowledge at all—it is a kind of mental whisper. This critique challenges the very foundation of the logicians' epistemology, which divides knowledge into pure concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' position
The logicians, represented here by al-Sa'd, hold that knowledge is of two types: tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). Tasawwur is considered knowledge even when it is stripped of all affirmation or negation. Find this claim in the passage.
Look for the phrase 'التصوّر المجرَّد عن كل نفي وإثبات'.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that a definition (hadd) must be a complete sentence—a predicate with a deleted subject—to be beneficial. He also argues that a concept without any truth value (neither affirmed nor denied existence) is not knowledge; it is like the fantasy of a 'sea of quicksilver'. Identify these two claims in the Arabic.
The definition requirement: 'الحد خبر مبتدإ محذوف لتكون جملة تامة'.
3
Analyze the argument's structure
Ibn Taymiyya uses an analogy: imagining a sea of quicksilver without considering its existence or possibility is a kind of whisper (waswasa), not knowledge. This shows that mere mental representation does not suffice. Why does he think this undermines the logicians' concept of tasawwur?
It parallels requiring a definition to be a complete sentence: both require a judgment element.
4
Apply the critique to an Islamic science example
Take the definition of 'salah' (prayer) from a fiqh text. If a jurist defines salah as 'a set of actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim', is this a complete sentence? Does it include any assertion about its obligation or validity? Compare with Ibn Taymiyya's requirement.
The definition in fiqh is often a description; does it meet his condition?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرف أن مجرَّد الاسم ومجرَّد الحدّ لا يفيد ما يفيده «الكلام» بحال، عُلم أن الحدّ خبر مبتدإ محذوف لتكون جملة تامة . ثم قد بينا فساد قولهم سواءً جُعل الحدّ مفرداً كالأسماء، أو مركباً كالجمل؛ وأنه على التقديرين لا يفيد تصوير المسمى . وهو المطلوب. و دعوى المدعي أن الحد مجرّد المفردِ المقيَّدِ كدعواهم أن التصوّر - الذي هو أحد نوعى العلم - هو التصوّر المجرَّد عن كل نفي وإثبات . ومعلوم أن مثل هذا لا يكون علماً عند أحد من العقلاء، بل إذا خطر ببال الإنسان شيء ما ولم يخطر له ثبوته ولا انتفاؤه بوجه من الوجوه لم يكن قد علم شيئاً ؛ مثل من خطر له «بحرُ زِنْبَقِ» أو «جبل ياقوت» خاطراً مجرّداً عن كون هذا التصور ثابتاً في الخارج أو منتفياً، ممكناً أو ممتنعاً، فإن هذا من جنس الوَسْواس، لا من جنس العلم. وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذا في مواضع متعددة، مثل الكلام على «المحصل»، وبينا أن المشروط في التصديق من جنس العلم المشروط في «القول». فمن صدق بما لم يتصوّره كان قد تكلم بغير علم، ومن صدق بما تصوّره كان كالمتكلم بعلم . بهامش الأصل: كون التصور ليس بعلم والمناطقة يجعلونه علماً، كما قال السعد: «العلم إن كان إذعاناً لنسبة فتصديق، وإلا فتصور، فجعله قسماً من العلم والحق ما قاله المصنف رحمه الله .
And if it is known that the mere name and the mere definition do not benefit what 'speech' benefits at all, it is known that the definition is a predicate of a deleted subject to form a complete sentence. Then we have shown the corruption of their statement, whether the definition is made an isolated term like nouns, or composite like sentences; and that on both suppositions it does not benefit the depiction of the named. That is what is sought. And the claim of the claimant that the definition is merely an isolated restricted term is like their claim that the concept — which is one of the two kinds of knowledge — is the concept stripped of all negation and affirmation. And it is known that such a thing is not knowledge in the view of any rational person; rather, if something occurs to the mind of a person and its affirmation and negation do not occur to him in any way, he has not known anything — like someone who thinks of a 'sea of quicksilver' or a 'mountain of ruby' as a bare thought, without regard to whether this concept is existent in the external world or non-existent, possible or impossible; this is a kind of whisper (waswasa), not a kind of knowledge. And we have expanded on this in multiple places, such as in our discussion of 'al-Muhassal'. And we explained that the condition for judgment (tasdiq) is like the condition for knowledge in 'speech'. So whoever affirms something he has not conceived has spoken without knowledge, and whoever affirms what he has conceived is like one speaking with knowledge. In the margin of the original: 'The concept is not knowledge — the logicians make it knowledge, as al-Sa'd said: "Knowledge, if it is an assent to a relation, then it is a judgment; otherwise it is a concept." So he made it a division of knowledge. The truth is what the author said, may Allah have mercy on him.'
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique of the logicians' notion that a definition (hadd) can convey a concept (tasawwur) even when it is a mere isolated term or a complex expression without any truth claim. Ibn Taymiyya first asserts that a definition must be a complete sentence (a predicate with an omitted subject) to be meaningful. He then attacks the idea that a concept can be known without any affirmation or negation, using the example of imagining a 'sea of quicksilver' or a 'mountain of ruby' — such a mental image is not knowledge, it is mere fantasy (waswasa). He links this to his earlier works and emphasizes that genuine knowledge requires both a concept and a judgment (tasdiq). The marginal note reinforces his position against the logicians who classify pure concept as a type of knowledge.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
