Two Purposes of Explanation: Clarifying Meaning vs. Verifying Truth
Author distinguishes explanation of intended meaning from verification, tying to taswir and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two purposes of tafsir al-kalam (explaining speech) and identify which purpose requires definitions and which requires evidence.

Core Idea
Explaining speech is either about what the speaker meant or whether it is true – each needs a distinct tool.

Ibn Taymiyya here steps back from the mechanics of definition to ask: why do we explain speech at all? He identifies two distinct goals—clarifying the speaker's intended meaning and verifying the speech's truth—each demanding its own method. This distinction maps onto the classical logical pair of conception (taswir) and assent (tasdiq), but he roots it in the practical need to understand or evaluate a statement. This lesson will help you recognize which explanatory task you are performing when you engage with a text or argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purposes
In the Arabic, find the two phrases: 'clarifying the intended meaning of the speaker' (tabyin murad al-mutakallim) and 'clarifying its correctness and establishment' (tabyin sihhatihi wa taqririhi). Note that the first is about understanding the speaker's intention, the second about verifying the truth of the speech.
2
Match each purpose to required knowledge
For clarifying meaning, Ibn Taymiyya states it requires knowledge of the definitions (hudud) of the words. For verifying truth, it requires knowledge of the evidence (dalil) for correctness. Extract this pairing from the passage.
3
Connect to taswir and tasdiq
He explicitly says the first involves explaining the depiction (taswir) of his speech, the second explaining assent (tasdiq) to his speech. Notice that taswir here is the process of forming a conception (often through definition or translation), while tasdiq is assent based on evidence.
4
Analyze the analogy with translation
He compares depicting speech to translating names (tarjama). Sometimes the hearer already knows the referent (musamma) but not the name; sometimes they do not know the referent at all, so one points to the actual instance or a likeness. This shows definitions can be for the name or the named. Reflect on how this connects to the purpose: if clarifying meaning, you might need to define the word or point to the thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا تفسير الكلام وشرحه إذا أريد به تبيين مراد المتكلم؛ فهذا يبنى على معرفة حدود كلامه. وإذا أريد به تبيين صحته وتقريره فإنه يحتاج إلى معرفة دليل صحة الكلام. فالأول فيه بيان تصوير كلامه والثاني بيان تصديق كلامه. وتصوير كلامه كتصوير مسميات الأسماء بـ «الترجمة» تارةً لمن يكون قد تصور المسمى ولم يعرف أن ذلك اسمه، وتارةً لمن لم يكن قد تصور المسمى فيشار له إلى المسمى بحسب الإمكان - إما إلى عينه وإما إلى نظيره. ولهذا يقال: الحد تارة يكون لـ«الاسم»، وتارة يكون لـ«المسمى». وأئمة المصنفين في صناعة الحدود على طريقة المنطقيين يعترفون عند التحقيق بذلك، كما ذكر أبو حامد الغزالي في كتاب معيار العلم الذي صنفه في المنطق، بعد أن قال: «البحث النظري الجاري في الطلب إما أن يتجه إلى تصور أو إلى تصديق».
And from this is the interpretation of speech and its explanation when it is intended to clarify the intended meaning of the speaker; this is based on knowledge of the definitions of his speech. And when it is intended to clarify its correctness and establishment, it needs knowledge of the evidence for the correctness of the speech. The first involves an explanation of the depiction of his speech, and the second involves an explanation of assent to his speech. And the depiction of his speech is like the depiction of the referents of names by 'translation' sometimes for one who has conceived the referent and does not know that it is its name, and sometimes for one who has not conceived the referent, so one points to the referent according to possibility—either to its exact instance or to its likeness. Hence it is said: the definition is sometimes for the 'name' and sometimes for the 'named'. And the leading authors in the craft of definitions according to the method of the logicians acknowledge this upon investigation, as narrated by Abu Hamid al-Ghazali in his book Mi'yar al-'Ilm which he wrote on logic, after he said: 'Theoretical inquiry that proceeds in seeking is either directed toward conception or toward assent.'
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage extends Ibn Taymiyya's discussion of definition (hadd) to the broader topic of explaining speech (tafsir al-kalam). He distinguishes two purposes: explaining the speaker's intended meaning (tabyin murad al-mutakallim) and verifying the correctness of the speech (tabyin sihhat al-kalam). The first requires knowledge of the definitions (hudud) of the words; the second requires evidence (dalil) for the speech's truth. The first corresponds to the explanation of conception (taswir) and the second to assent (tasdiq). Depicting speech (taswir al-kalam) is like translating names (tarjama): sometimes the hearer already knows the referent (musamma) but not that this particular name refers to it, and sometimes he does not know the referent at all, so one points to its actual instance or a similar example. Therefore, definitions can be either for the name (li al-ism) or for the named (li al-musamma). Ibn Taymiyya then cites al-Ghazali from his Mi'yar al-'Ilm, where Ghazali admits that theoretical inquiry is either toward conception (tasawwur) or toward assent (tasdiq). This serves as an authoritative confirmation of Ibn Taymiyya's own position.

Key Terms

تصوير
تصديق
الحد
الدليل
الترجمة

المسمى

A) Evidence for the speech's truth.
B) The definitions of the words used.

Key Takeaway
Explaining speech has two distinct aims—clarifying meaning (requires definitions) and verifying truth (requires evidence)—and conflating them undermines sound reasoning.

Ibn Sina on Self-Conceived Concepts
Ibn Sina's admission that certain concepts are self-conceived

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three primary self-evident concepts according to Ibn Sina and explain why they cannot be defined using genus and differentia.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina admits that existence, thing, and necessary are self-evident and cannot be defined.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya turns the tables on the logicians by quoting their own master, Ibn Sina. Ibn Sina himself acknowledged that certain fundamental concepts cannot be defined—they are known directly. This undermines the claim that all knowledge must be built on definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three concepts
In the quote from al-Shifa, Ibn Sina names three specific concepts: 'existent' (al-mawjūd), 'thing' (al-shay'), and 'necessary' (al-ḍarūrī). Note that these are presented as primary and self-evident.
2
Understand the claim: imprinted in the soul
Ibn Sina says their meanings are 'engraved in the soul with a primary engraving' (irtisām awwalī). This means we are born with innate conceptual knowledge of these; they do not need to be acquired through definition.
This is a foundational epistemological claim about innate ideas.
3
Distinguish definition from reminder
The passage contrasts two operations: a true definition (ḥadd) which gives knowledge of an unknown quiddity, and a reminder (tanbīh) which merely draws attention to what is already present. For these primary concepts, only a reminder is possible.
4
Recognize the logical implication
If these core concepts cannot be defined, then the universal theory of definition (that all concepts must be defined by genus and differentia) fails. This is the point Ibn Taymiyya uses against the logicians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته اعتراف ابن سينا بأن من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته وهكذا يقول حذاقهم في تحديد أمور كثيرة قد حدّها غيرهم. يقولون: لا يمكن تحديدها. تحديد تعريف لماهياتها بل تحديد تنبيه وتمييز. كما قال ابن سينا في الشفاء. قال: فنقول: إن الموجود والشيء والضروري معانيها ترتسم في النفس ارتساماً أولياً - ليس ذلك الارتسام مما يحتاج أن يُجلب بأشياء هو أعرف منها. فإنه كما أنه في باب التصديق مبادىء أولية يقع التصديق بها لذاتها، ويكون التصديق لغيرها بسببها. وإذا لم يخطر بالبال أو لم يفهم اللفظ الدال عليها لم يمكن التوصل إلى معرفة ما يعرف بها. وإن لم يكن التعريف الذي يحاول إخطارها بالبال، أو تفهيم ما يدل عليها من الألفاظ محاولاً لإفادة علم ما ليس في الغريزة، بل منبهاً على تفهيم ما يريده القائل أو يذهب إليه. وربما كان ذلك بأشياء هي في أنفسها أخفى من المراد تعريفه لكنها لعلة ما وعبارة ما صارت أعرف. كذلك في التصورات أشياء هي مبادىء للتصوّر - هي متصوّرات لذاتها. وإذا أريد أن يُدَلَّ عليها لم يكن ذلك بالحقيقة تعريفاً لمجهول، بل تنبيهاً وإخطاراً بالبالباسم العلامة. وربما كانت في نفسها أخفى منه لكنها لعلة ما وحال ما صارت أعرف.
The second station - the fourth aspect: Among things are those that are self-conceived. Ibn Sina's admission that among things are those that are self-conceived. And thus the acute among them say regarding the definition of many things that others have defined: They cannot be defined. It is not a definition of their quiddities, but a definition of reminder and distinction. As Ibn Sina said in al-Shifa: He said: So we say: The meanings of 'existent', 'thing', and 'necessary' are engraved in the soul with a primary engraving - that engraving does not need to be brought by things that are more known than it. For just as in the domain of assent there are primary principles that are assented to for themselves, and assent to others is due to them. And if they do not occur to the mind or the word indicating them is not understood, it is not possible to arrive at knowledge of what is known through them. And if the definition that attempts to make them occur to the mind, or to make understand what indicates them from words, is not an attempt to give knowledge of something not in the innate disposition, but a reminder of what the speaker intends or goes by. And perhaps that is by things that are in themselves more hidden than what is intended to be defined, but due to some cause and some expression become more known. Similarly in conceptions, there are things that are principles for conception - they are self-conceived. And when one wants to indicate them, that is not in reality a definition of the unknown, but a reminder and an occurrence to the mind by the name of the sign. And perhaps they are in themselves more hidden than it, but due to some cause and some state become more known.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological argument from authority: Ibn Taymiyya quotes Ibn Sina to show that even the leading logician admits that some concepts are self-evident and cannot be strictly defined. The passage begins by stating that there are things that are self-conceived (mutasawwar bi-dhātihi). The author notes that the acute among the logicians say that some things cannot be defined; their 'definition' is really just a reminder (tanbīh) and distinction (tamyīz). Then follows the quote from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. Ibn Sina says that the meanings of 'existent' (al-mawjūd), 'thing' (al-shay'), and 'necessary' (al-ḍarūrī) are imprinted in the soul primarily—they do not need to be brought by something more known. He draws an analogy to assent (taṣdīq): just as there are primary principles that are assented to themselves and enable assent to others, so in conception (taṣawwur) there are primary concepts that are self-conceived. If one does not already have these concepts in mind, no definition can produce them; one can only remind or indicate them by a sign. Sometimes the indicator is in itself more hidden than what is indicated, but due to a particular circumstance becomes more known. The upshot is that Ibn Sina himself concedes that there are foundational concepts that are not acquired through definition, supporting Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the universal applicability of ḥadd. The passage is important for understanding the limits of logical definition and the role of direct intuition.

Key Terms
الموجود
الشيء
الضروري
التصور
التصديق

According to Ibn Sina in the quoted passage, which of the following best describes the status of concepts like 'existent' and 'thing'?
A) They can be defined by genus and differentia
B) They are self-evident and require only a reminder, not a definition
C) They are unknown to most people and must be taught by definition
D) They are derived from sensory experience through induction

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina himself concedes that some concepts are known directly and cannot be defined, supporting the epistemological critique of the universal need for definition.

The Analogy Between Conception and Assent
The parallel between primary concepts and primary propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the parallel structure between primary concepts (taṣawwur) and primary principles (taṣdīq) as presented by Ibn Sina and used by Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Conception mirrors assent: both have self-evident foundations that enable all other knowledge.

Ibn Sina draws a powerful analogy: just as there are self-evident truths in propositions (assent), there are self-evident concepts in conception. This structural parallel shows that the mind does not need definition for everything—some ideas are simply given.

Guided Path
1
Locate the analogy in the Arabic
Find the sentence that begins with 'فإنه كما أنه في باب التصديق...' and continues with 'كذلك في التصورات...'. This is the core of the analogy.
2
Identify the two domains
The analogy compares two epistemological domains: the domain of assent (bāb al-taṣdīq) and the domain of conception (al-taṣawwurāt). In each, there are primary, self-evident foundations.
Primary principles in assent are propositions assented to for themselves; primary concepts are those conceived without definition.
3
Explain how the analogy supports Ibn Taymiyya's argument
By showing that even Ibn Sina admits an analogy between self-evident assent and self-evident conception, Ibn Taymiyya reinforces his claim that definition is not always necessary. The analogy implies that just as we have immediate assent to certain truths, we have immediate conception of certain ideas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته اعتراف ابن سينا بأن من الأمور ما هو متصور بذاته وهكذا يقول حذاقهم في تحديد أمور كثيرة قد حدّها غيرهم. يقولون: لا يمكن تحديدها. تحديد تعريف لماهياتها بل تحديد تنبيه وتمييز. كما قال ابن سينا في الشفاء. قال: فنقول: إن الموجود والشيء والضروري معانيها ترتسم في النفس ارتساماً أولياً - ليس ذلك الارتسام مما يحتاج أن يُجلب بأشياء هو أعرف منها. فإنه كما أنه في باب التصديق مبادىء أولية يقع التصديق بها لذاتها، ويكون التصديق لغيرها بسببها. وإذا لم يخطر بالبال أو لم يفهم اللفظ الدال عليها لم يمكن التوصل إلى معرفة ما يعرف بها. وإن لم يكن التعريف الذي يحاول إخطارها بالبال، أو تفهيم ما يدل عليها من الألفاظ محاولاً لإفادة علم ما ليس في الغريزة، بل منبهاً على تفهيم ما يريده القائل أو يذهب إليه. وربما كان ذلك بأشياء هي في أنفسها أخفى من المراد تعريفه لكنها لعلة ما وعبارة ما صارت أعرف. كذلك في التصورات أشياء هي مبادىء للتصوّر - هي متصوّرات لذاتها. وإذا أريد أن يُدَلَّ عليها لم يكن ذلك بالحقيقة تعريفاً لمجهول، بل تنبيهاً وإخطاراً بالبالباسم العلامة. وربما كانت في نفسها أخفى منه لكنها لعلة ما وحال ما صارت أعرف.
The second station - the fourth aspect: Among things are those that are self-conceived. Ibn Sina's admission that among things are those that are self-conceived. And thus the acute among them say regarding the definition of many things that others have defined: They cannot be defined. It is not a definition of their quiddities, but a definition of reminder and distinction. As Ibn Sina said in al-Shifa: He said: So we say: The meanings of 'existent', 'thing', and 'necessary' are engraved in the soul with a primary engraving - that engraving does not need to be brought by things that are more known than it. For just as in the domain of assent there are primary principles that are assented to for themselves, and assent to others is due to them. And if they do not occur to the mind or the word indicating them is not understood, it is not possible to arrive at knowledge of what is known through them. And if the definition that attempts to make them occur to the mind, or to make understand what indicates them from words, is not an attempt to give knowledge of something not in the innate disposition, but a reminder of what the speaker intends or goes by. And perhaps that is by things that are in themselves more hidden than what is intended to be defined, but due to some cause and some expression become more known. Similarly in conceptions, there are things that are principles for conception - they are self-conceived. And when one wants to indicate them, that is not in reality a definition of the unknown, but a reminder and an occurrence to the mind by the name of the sign. And perhaps they are in themselves more hidden than it, but due to some cause and some state become more known.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological argument from authority: Ibn Taymiyya quotes Ibn Sina to show that even the leading logician admits that some concepts are self-evident and cannot be strictly defined. The passage begins by stating that there are things that are self-conceived (mutasawwar bi-dhātihi). The author notes that the acute among the logicians say that some things cannot be defined; their 'definition' is really just a reminder (tanbīh) and distinction (tamyīz). Then follows the quote from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. Ibn Sina says that the meanings of 'existent' (al-mawjūd), 'thing' (al-shay'), and 'necessary' (al-ḍarūrī) are imprinted in the soul primarily—they do not need to be brought by something more known. He draws an analogy to assent (taṣdīq): just as there are primary principles that are assented to themselves and enable assent to others, so in conception (taṣawwur) there are primary concepts that are self-conceived. If one does not already have these concepts in mind, no definition can produce them; one can only remind or indicate them by a sign. Sometimes the indicator is in itself more hidden than what is indicated, but due to a particular circumstance becomes more known. The upshot is that Ibn Sina himself concedes that there are foundational concepts that are not acquired through definition, supporting Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the universal applicability of ḥadd. The passage is important for understanding the limits of logical definition and the role of direct intuition.

التصديق
الموجود
الشيء
الضروري

Ijtihad in Applying Legal Terms to Particulars
Ibn Taymiyya discusses the need for ijtihad to apply legal terms to particular cases and types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why applying a general Sharia term to a specific case requires ijtihad, using the concept of tahqiq al-manat.

Core Idea
Ijtihad bridges the gap between general Sharia terms and specific instances.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya highlights a crucial point: knowing the definition of a legal term is not enough to apply it to real cases. The process of determining whether a particular person, action, or object falls under that term is called tahqiq al-manat, and it often requires ijtihad. This lesson will guide you through this process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Term and Its Description
Find the Sharia term (e.g., 'adl for testimony) and the description attached to it.
2
Recognize the Need for Verification
Understand that the term is general; applying it to a specific case requires checking if that case possesses the description.
3
Apply the Concept of Tahqiq al-Manat
Verify whether the particular instance matches the description (e.g., is this person just?).
4
Conclude the Role of Ijtihad
If the match is not obvious, ijtihad is needed to make the determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معرفة دخول الأعيان الموجودة في هذه الأسماء والألفاظ، فهذا قد يكون ظاهراً، وقد يكون خفياً يحتاج إلى «اجتهاد». وهذا هو التأويل في لفظ الشارع، الذي يتفاضل الفقهاء وغيرهم فيه فإنهم قد اشتركوا في حفظ الألفاظ الشرعية بما فيها من الأسماء، أو حفظ كلام الفقهاء، أو النحاة، أو الأطباء، وغيرهم. ثم يتفاضلون بأن يَسْبِق أحدهم إلى أن يعرف أن هذا المعيَّن الموجود هو المراد، أو مراد بهذا الاسم، كما يسبق الفقيه الفاضل إلى حادثة فيُنزل عليها كلام الشارع أو كلام الفقهاء. وكذلك الطبيب يسبق إلى مرض لشخص معين فيُنزل عليه كلام الأطباء إذ الكتب والكلام المنقول عن الأنبياء والعلماء إنما هو مطلق بذكر الأشياء بصفاتها وعلاماتها فلا بد أن يعرف أن هذا المعيَّن هو ذاك. وإذا كان خفياً فقد يسميه بعض الناس تحقيق المناط»، فإن الشارع قد ناط الحكم بوصف. كما ناط قبول الشهادة بكونه ذا عَدْل، وكما ناط العشرة المأمور بها بكونها بالمعروف، وكما ناط «النفقة الواجبة بالمعروف، وكما ناط «الاستقبال في الصلوة» بالتوجه شَطْرَ المسجد الحرام. يبقى النظر في هذا المعيَّن هل هو «شطر المسجد الحرام»، وهل هذا الشخص ذو عدل»، وهل هذه النفقة «نفقة بالمعروف»، وأمثال ذلك، لا بدّ فيه من نظر خاص، لا يعلم ذلك بمجرد الاسم. وقد يكون الاجتهاد في دخول بعض الأنواع في مسمى ذلك الاسم. كدخول الأشربة المسكرة من غير العنب والنخل في مسمى «الخمر، ودخول «الشطرنج» و«النَّرْد» ونحوهما في مسمى «المَيْسِر».
And as for knowing the inclusion of existing individuals in these names and words, this may be clear or may be hidden requiring 'ijtihad'. And this is 'ta'wil' in the words of the Lawgiver, in which the jurists and others excel, for they share in memorizing the legal words including the names, or memorizing the speech of jurists, grammarians, physicians, and others. Then they excel by one of them preceding to know that this particular existing [thing] is intended, or intended by this name, just as the excellent jurist precedes to an incident and applies to it the words of the Lawgiver or the words of the jurists. And likewise the physician precedes to a disease of a particular person and applies to it the words of the physicians, for books and speech transmitted from the prophets and scholars are only absolute by mentioning things with their descriptions and signs, so one must know that this particular is that. And if it is hidden, some people call it 'tahqiq al-manat', for the Lawgiver has tied the ruling to a description. Just as He tied the acceptance of testimony to being just, and as He tied the commanded companionship to being with kindness, and as He tied the obligatory expenditure to kindness, and as He tied facing the qibla in prayer to directing towards the Sacred Mosque. What remains is consideration of this particular: is it 'the direction of the Sacred Mosque', and is this person 'just', and is this expenditure 'expenditure with kindness', and the like; this requires a special consideration, not known by mere name. And ijtihad may be regarding the inclusion of some types under the meaning of that name. Like the inclusion of intoxicating drinks from other than grapes and dates under the name 'khamr', and the inclusion of 'chess' and 'backgammon' and the like under the name 'maysir'.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion on the role of ijtihad and ta'wil in applying general legal terms to specific instances. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that knowing whether a particular existing entity falls under a given name or word may be obvious or hidden. When hidden, it requires ijtihad, and this is what is meant by ta'wil in the words of the Lawgiver. Jurists and others excel in this by memorizing legal texts and then applying them to new situations. He gives the example of a skilled jurist who encounters a new incident and applies the revealed texts or previous juristic rulings to it. Similarly, a physician applies medical texts to a specific patient's disease. The key point is that revealed texts are stated in absolute terms with descriptions and signs; one must determine whether the particular case matches that description. This process is sometimes called 'tahqiq al-manat' (verification of the effective cause). The Lawgiver has tied rulings to descriptions: e.g., accepting testimony tied to justice, marital companionship to kindness, obligatory expenditure to kindness, and prayer direction to facing the Sacred Mosque. The question then becomes: Is this particular person just? Is this particular act expenditure with kindness? This requires a specific judgment, not known merely by the name. Additionally, ijtihad may involve determining whether a new species falls under a general name, such as whether intoxicating drinks from sources other than grapes and dates are included under the term 'khamr' (wine), or whether chess and backgammon are included under 'maysir' (gambling). This shows that logical definitions alone are insufficient; one must have deep knowledge of the Sharia and its objectives to correctly apply terms. The examples are all from Islamic law: testimony, marriage, expenditure, prayer direction, wine, gambling.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Here, ijtihad means the effort to determine whether a particular case falls under a general legal term or ruling, requiring expertise in the Sharia. [Note: Ijtihad is a standard term in usul al-fiqh; here it is used specifically for applying definitions to particulars.]
التأويل (al-ta'wil): Ta'wil is the interpretation or application of the Lawgiver's words to specific instances, especially when not obvious.
تحقيق المناط (tahqiq al-manat): The verification of the effective cause or description to which the ruling is attached; determining whether the particular case possesses that description. [Note: This term is central in usul al-fiqh for applying rulings to new cases; here Ibn Taymiyya uses it in the context of logical definition and ijtihad.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Ibn Taymiyya mentions 'tahqiq al-manat' in the context of which Sharia example?
A) Determining the time of prayer
B) Verifying whether a person is 'adl (just) for testimony
C) Calculating zakat on wealth
D) Distinguishing between Quranic verses

Key Takeaway
Applying general Sharia terms to specific cases often requires ijtihad through tahqiq al-manat, not just definitional knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Sharia ruling requiring 'expenditure with kindness' in marriage (Quran 4:19). Describe the process of tahqiq al-manat to determine whether a specific husband's spending qualifies as 'kindness'.
?
Identify a modern product that could fall under the term 'khamr' (intoxicating drink) and explain the ijtihad needed to include it.

Extending Legal Names to New Types Through Ijtihad
Ibn Taymiyya discusses the need for ijtihad to apply legal terms to particular cases and types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a new type of object or activity may be included under an existing legal term through ijtihad, using the examples of khamr and maysir.

Core Idea
Ijtihad determines whether new types fall under general legal names.

Ibn Taymiyya gives two key examples: intoxicating drinks from sources other than grapes and dates may be included under 'khamr', and games like chess and backgammon under 'maysir'. This shows that the scope of a legal term is not limited to the original instances; ijtihad is needed to extend it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Instances and Their Description
For khamr, the original instances are dates and grapes, and the description is intoxication.
2
Consider the New Type
e.g., an intoxicating drink from honey or barley.
3
Find the Shared Effective Cause ('Illah)
Both cause intoxication.
4
Apply Ijtihad to Determine Inclusion
Based on the shared 'illah, the new type may fall under the same legal name.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

The Impossibility of Demanding Simple Concepts and the Equivalence of Definition and Name
Author argues demanding simple concepts is impossible and definition equals name in benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reductio argument that simple concepts cannot be demanded and explain why definition and name are equivalent in benefit.

Core Idea
Definition cannot produce new simple concepts; it only clarifies what is already known.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya delivers a devastating critique of the logicians' claim that definitions are necessary for obtaining simple conceptualizations (tasawwurat mufrada). He employs a reductio ad absurdum to show that demanding simple concepts is logically impossible, and then argues that even if definitions were possible, they offer no more benefit than a mere name. This challenges the very foundation of the logician's theory of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians claim that simple concepts (tasawwurat mufrada) are obtained through definitions, making definitions essential for conceptualization. Locate this claim in the broader context of the passage.
The passage begins by addressing a hypothetical objection about impossibility.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's reductio argument
He argues that the impossibility of demanding simple concepts is necessary on either contradictory assumption; if its non-existence entailed contradictions, the impossibility must be established. Trace the logical structure: assuming the impossibility of demanding simple concepts leads to no contradiction, so it stands.
This is a meta-logical argument about the impossibility itself.
3
Analyze the dichotomy after the reductio
Once the impossibility is established, Ibn Taymiyya presents two cases: either simple concepts are already present in man or they are not. For each, definition provides no new cognitive benefit—it cannot create the concept if unknown, and if known, it is redundant.
This dichotomy is exhaustive and shows definition is either useless or insufficient.
4
Conclude the equivalence of definition and name
Since definition cannot produce new simple concepts, its benefit is no different from a name: both merely point to something already known or fail to teach the unknown. The intended point is that definition and name are equivalent in benefit.
This undermines the logicians' prioritization of definition over name for conceptualization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : هذا الامتناع لازم على تقدير هذا النقيض وتقدير هذا النقيض، فأيهما كان ثابتاً في نفس الأمر كان الامتناع لازماً له يلزم به في نفس الأمر . وليس في هذا جمع بين النقيضين ولا رفع للنقيضين. وهذا يقوى المقصود. فإن هذا الامتناع إذا لَزِم انتفائه المُحالُ لم يكن منتفياً، بل يكون هذا الامتناع ثابتاً، وهو المطلوب. وإذا من كان عدمُ هذا الامتناع مستلزماً للنقيضين كان مُحالاً، فيكون نقيضه ـ وهو ثبوت هذا الامتناع ثابتاً، وهو المراد. وإذا لم تكن التصورات المفردة مطلوبة، فإما أن تكون حاصلة للإنسان، فلا فائدة للحدود كفائدة الاسم تحصل بالحد، فلا يفيد الحدُّ التصوير؛ وإما أن لا تكون حاصلة، فمجرد حصول الحد لا يوجب تصور المسميات لمن لا يعرفها. ومتى كان له شعور بها لم يحتج إلى الحد في ذلك الشعور، إلا من جنس ما يحتاج إلى الاسم. والمقصود هو التسوية بين فائدة الحد وفائدة الاسم.
It is said: This impossibility is necessary on the assumption of this contradictory and on the assumption of this contradictory; whichever is established in reality, the impossibility is necessary for it, necessitating it in reality. There is no combining the two contradictories nor lifting them. This strengthens the intended point. For if this impossibility entails its non-existence being impossible, then it is not non-existent; rather this impossibility is established, and that is required. And if the non-existence of this impossibility entails the two contradictories, then it is impossible, so its opposite—namely the establishment of this impossibility—is established, and that is intended. And if simple conceptualizations are not demanded, then either they are already present in man, in which case definitions have no benefit just as the benefit of the name is obtained by definition, so definition does not provide conceptualization; or they are not present, then the mere occurrence of the definition does not necessitate the conceptualization of the named things for one who does not know them. And whenever he has awareness of them, he does not need the definition for that awareness, except in the same way he needs the name. The intended point is the equivalence between the benefit of definition and the benefit of the name.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This is a critical passage in which Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that definitions are necessary for obtaining simple conceptualizations (tasawwurat mufrada). He argues that the impossibility of demanding such conceptualizations is established by a reductio ad absurdum: if the non-existence of that impossibility entailed both a thing and its contradictory, then that non-existence is impossible, so the impossibility stands. Thus, simple conceptualizations are not subject to demand. Then he presents a dichotomy: either these concepts are already known, in which case a definition adds nothing beyond a name; or they are unknown, in which case a definition, being composed of words, cannot convey the unknown concepts any more than a name can. Therefore, definition and name are equivalent in benefit—neither can produce new simple conceptualizations. This undermines the logicians' foundational distinction between definition and name.

A) It provides new knowledge that was previously unknown.
B) It is equivalent to a name in benefit.
C) It clarifies the name by adding detail.
D) It is necessary for conceptualization.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that definitions produce new simple concepts, arguing that definition and name are equivalent in benefit, and that the demand for simple concepts is logically impossible.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Philosophers' Distinction
Critique of the philosophers' existence/quiddity distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the philosophers' claim that existence is additional to quiddity externally, and explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers it a corrupt principle built on historical errors.

Core Idea
The philosophers' extra-mental existence/quiddity distinction is built on a corrupt foundation.

Ibn Taymiyya dismantles a cornerstone of Aristotelian metaphysics: the idea that existence is an attribute added to quiddity in external reality. He argues that this distinction is not only false but also derives from earlier philosophical errors that even Aristotle himself refuted. In this lesson, we will trace the historical roots of the distinction and understand Ibn Taymiyya's charge that it is fundamentally corrupt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate in the passage the statement that philosophers claim 'existence of quiddities in the external is additional to them' and that quiddities have 'subsistence in the external other than their existence'. This is the target of the critique.
The claim is in the first few lines of the Arabic.
2
Trace the historical origins of the error
Ibn Taymiyya shows that this error is not new: Pythagoreans wrongly posited numbers externally, Platonists posited separate forms, and even Aristotle's own hyle and separate specific realities are false. The philosophers' current distinction is built on this corrupted history.
Notice how he moves from early philosophers to Aristotle.
3
Recognize the error in the philosophers' own alternative
Even after Aristotle refuted the ancients, his own claims about prime matter (hyle) and specific realities existing externally are equally false. Ibn Taymiyya equates this error to atomism and notes most Islamic theological schools reject both.
He mentions schools like Kullabiyya, Najjariyya, etc.
4
Understand the foundational corruption
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the distinction between quiddity and existence in logic is 'built upon this corrupt principle'. The entire edifice is unsound because it posits extra-mental realities that are neither mental nor actual existents.
This is the key point: the distinction is not epistemologically sound.

The statements of the philosophers concerning existence, subsistence, duration, and place. It requires that the existence of quiddities that are externally is additional to them in the external, and that quiddities have a subsistence in the external other than their existence in the external. And among what clarifies the discussion concerning the first principle is that the philosophers, the followers of Aristotle like Ibn Sina and those below him, do not say that every non-existent among individuals and others is subsistent in the external. Only those who say that among the Mu'tazila and Shi'a say it—and they are in a better state than the philosophers, for the philosophers are of two opinions. As for their ancients, like Pythagoras and his followers, and Plato and his followers, they were in that manifest error; Aristotle and his followers refuted them, as Ibn Sina mentioned in al-Shifa' and elsewhere. The followers of Pythagoras thought that numbers and magnitudes are things existing in the external, separate from the numbered and measured things. Then the followers of Plato perceived the corruption of this, and thought that specific realities, like the reality of man and horse and the like, are subsistent in the external, separate from the individual entities existing in the external, and that they are eternal, not subject to change—these are called 'Platonic forms' and 'suspended forms'. And they did not restrict themselves to that, but also affirmed that in 'matter', 'duration', and 'place'. So they affirmed a matter abstracted from forms, subsistent in the external, which is the primary hyle, concerning which the majority of rational people among their brethren and others corrected them. And they affirmed a duration existing external to bodies and their attributes. And they affirmed a void existing external to bodies and their attributes, like number. And Aristotle and his followers perceived that all these are things estimated in minds, not subsistent in external entities. Then Aristotle and his followers claimed that 'matter exists in the external separate from sensible forms', and that specific realities are subsistent in the external separate from particular individuals. And this is also false, as has been explained elsewhere, and it was shown that the statement of those who say 'the body is composed of hyle and form' is false, just as the statement of those who say 'it is composed of indivisible atoms' is false; and that most sects of the mutakallimun among Muslims and others, like the Kullabiyya, Najjariyya, Dirariyya, Hishamiyya, and many of the Karramiyya, do not affirm this nor that, as is the case with the majority of jurists and others. The discussion concerning those who distinguish between 'existence' and 'quiddity' is elaborated elsewhere. The purpose here is to point out that what they mentioned in logic concerning the distinction between 'quiddity' and 'existence of quiddity in the external' is built upon this corrupt principle. And the reality of the correct distinction is that 'quiddity' is what is imprinted in the soul of the thing, and 'existence' is the very thing that is in the external from it. And this is a correct distinction. For the distinction between what is in the soul and what is in the external is established, known, and indubitable. But to posit a reality that is neither established in knowledge nor in existence is false. And it is known that the term 'quiddity' is intended for what is in the soul and the existent in the external. And the term 'existence' is intended for the explanation of what is in the soul and the existent in the external. So if both are intended for what is in the soul, then 'quiddity' is 'existence', and if both are intended for what is in the external, then 'quiddity' is also 'existence'. But if one is intended for what is in the soul and the other for what is in external existence, then 'quiddity' is other than 'existence'. And their statement only entails the affirmation of a 'quiddity' in the mind, not in external existence. And this is not disputed and has no benefit, for this is a report about mere positing and invention, since every human can invent a quiddity in himself other than what another has invented.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya on the philosophers' distinction between existence (wujud) and quiddity (mahiyya). He begins by summarizing the philosophers' claim: that the existence of external quiddities is additional to them, and that quiddities have a subsistence (thubut) in the external distinct from their existence. He then clarifies that the philosophers (Aristotelians like Ibn Sina) do not go as far as the Mu'tazila and Shi'a in affirming the subsistence of non-existent things. The philosophers themselves are divided: the ancient philosophers (Pythagoreans and Platonists) held erroneous views about numbers, magnitudes, and Platonic forms existing externally. Aristotle refuted them. However, Aristotle and his followers themselves fell into error by claiming that prime matter (hyle) and specific realities exist externally separate from sensible forms. Ibn Taymiyya declares this false, just as the atomist view is false. He notes that many Islamic theological schools do not affirm either view. Then he states the correct distinction: quiddity is the mental imprint of a thing, and existence is the actual external thing. The distinction between mental and external is sound; but positing a quiddity that is neither known nor existent is false. He further explains that the terms 'quiddity' and 'existence' can refer to mental or external things: if both refer to the same domain (both mental or both external), they are identical; only when one is mental and the other external is there a difference. The philosophers' distinction amounts to merely inventing quiddities in the mind, which is trivial and leads to relativism.

الوجود
الهيولى
المثل الأفلاطونية

According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the 'corrupt principle' upon which the philosophers' distinction between quiddity and existence is built?
A) The belief that numbers exist externally separate from numbered things.
B) The belief that quiddities have a subsistence in the external other than their existence, which itself rests on earlier erroneous views about hyle, Platonic forms, etc.
C) The belief that the world is eternal.
D) The belief that concepts are innate in the human mind.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique demonstrates that the philosophers' distinction between existence and quiddity is not a neutral logical analysis but rests on a corrupt metaphysical foundation derived from ancient errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

intermediate
8 min
The Correct Distinction: Mental Quiddity and External Existence
Ibn Taymiyya's positive statement of the correct distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate Ibn Taymiyya's correct distinction between mahiyya as the mental imprint and wujud as external reality, and apply it to clarify ambiguous terminology in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
The only sound distinction is between what is in the mind and what is in the external world.

After dismantling the philosophers' erroneous distinction, Ibn Taymiyya presents the correct one: quiddity (mahiyya) is the mental imprint of a thing, and existence (wujud) is the external reality. This simple clarification resolves many confusions, especially when terms are used ambiguously. In this lesson, we will unpack his precise definitions and see how they apply to standard discussions in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's definitions
Find where he says: 'quiddity is what is imprinted in the soul of the thing, and existence is the very thing that is in the external from it.' These are his operational definitions.
This appears later in the passage, after the critique.
2
Understand the two domains: mental and external
He emphasizes that the distinction between mental and external is 'established, known, and indubitable'. The key is that these are two distinct domains, not two things within the same domain.
No one disputes the difference between a concept in the mind and an actual object outside.
3
Analyze how the terms can be used in both domains
Ibn Taymiyya explains that both 'quiddity' and 'existence' can refer to either mental or external things. If both refer to the same domain, they are identical; if one is mental and the other external, they differ.
This resolves the confusion of whether they are the same or different.
4
Recognize the triviality of mere mental invention
He points out that the philosophers' distinction only amounts to inventing a quiddity in the mind, which anyone can do arbitrarily. This has no epistemological value.
Contrast: his distinction is based on real, known difference.

الصحيح
بينهما
وحقيقة الفَرْق الصحيح: أن «الماهية» هي ما يَرْتَسِمُ في النفس من الشيء»، الفرق و «الوجود هو نفس ما يكون في الخارج منه . وهذا فرق صحيح. فإن الفرق بين ما في النفس وما في الخارج ثابت معلوم لا ريب فيه . وأما تقدير حقيقة لا تكون ثابتة في العلم ولا في الوجود فهذا باطل .
ومعلوم أن لفظ «الماهية يُراد به ما في النفس والموجود في الخارج . (۱) ولفظ «الوجود» يُراد به بيان ما في النفس، والموجود في الخارج (۱). فمتى أريد بهما ما في النفس فَـ «الماهية هي الوجود»، وإن أريد بهما ما في الخارج فـ «الماهية» هي «الوجود» أيضاً. وأما إذا أريد بأحدهما ما في النفس وبالآخر ما في الوجود الخارج ف «الماهية) غيرُ الوجود» .
(۲)
وكلامهم إنما يستلزم ثبوت ماهية» في الذهن لا في الوجود الخارجي. وهذا لا الماهيات اختراعات نزاع فيه ولا فائدة فيه، إذ هذا خبر عن مجرد وضع واختراع، إذ يقدر كلُّ [٦٠] إنسان أن يخترع ماهية في نفسه غير ما اخترعها الآخر.
The statements of the philosophers concerning existence, subsistence, duration, and place. It requires that the existence of quiddities that are externally is additional to them in the external, and that quiddities have a subsistence in the external other than their existence in the external. And among what clarifies the discussion concerning the first principle is that the philosophers, the followers of Aristotle like Ibn Sina and those below him, do not say that every non-existent among individuals and others is subsistent in the external. Only those who say that among the Mu'tazila and Shi'a say it—and they are in a better state than the philosophers, for the philosophers are of two opinions. As for their ancients, like Pythagoras and his followers, and Plato and his followers, they were in that manifest error; Aristotle and his followers refuted them, as Ibn Sina mentioned in al-Shifa' and elsewhere. The followers of Pythagoras thought that numbers and magnitudes are things existing in the external, separate from the numbered and measured things. Then the followers of Plato perceived the corruption of this, and thought that specific realities, like the reality of man and horse and the like, are subsistent in the external, separate from the individual entities existing in the external, and that they are eternal, not subject to change—these are called 'Platonic forms' and 'suspended forms'. And they did not restrict themselves to that, but also affirmed that in 'matter', 'duration', and 'place'. So they affirmed a matter abstracted from forms, subsistent in the external, which is the primary hyle, concerning which the majority of rational people among their brethren and others corrected them. And they affirmed a duration existing external to bodies and their attributes. And they affirmed a void existing external to bodies and their attributes, like number. And Aristotle and his followers perceived that all these are things estimated in minds, not subsistent in external entities. Then Aristotle and his followers claimed that 'matter exists in the external separate from sensible forms', and that specific realities are subsistent in the external separate from particular individuals. And this is also false, as has been explained elsewhere, and it was shown that the statement of those who say 'the body is composed of hyle and form' is false, just as the statement of those who say 'it is composed of indivisible atoms' is false; and that most sects of the mutakallimun among Muslims and others, like the Kullabiyya, Najjariyya, Dirariyya, Hishamiyya, and many of the Karramiyya, do not affirm this nor that, as is the case with the majority of jurists and others. The discussion concerning those who distinguish between 'existence' and 'quiddity' is elaborated elsewhere. The purpose here is to point out that what they mentioned in logic concerning the distinction between 'quiddity' and 'existence of quiddity in the external' is built upon this corrupt principle. And the reality of the correct distinction is that 'quiddity' is what is imprinted in the soul of the thing, and 'existence' is the very thing that is in the external from it. And this is a correct distinction. For the distinction between what is in the soul and what is in the external is established, known, and indubitable. But to posit a reality that is neither established in knowledge nor in existence is false. And it is known that the term 'quiddity' is intended for what is in the soul and the existent in the external. And the term 'existence' is intended for the explanation of what is in the soul and the existent in the external. So if both are intended for what is in the soul, then 'quiddity' is 'existence', and if both are intended for what is in the external, then 'quiddity' is also 'existence'. But if one is intended for what is in the soul and the other for what is in external existence, then 'quiddity' is other than 'existence'. And their statement only entails the affirmation of a 'quiddity' in the mind, not in external existence. And this is not disputed and has no benefit, for this is a report about mere positing and invention, since every human can invent a quiddity in himself other than what another has invented.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya on the philosophers' distinction between existence (wujud) and quiddity (mahiyya). He begins by summarizing the philosophers' claim: that the existence of external quiddities is additional to them, and that quiddities have a subsistence (thubut) in the external distinct from their existence. He then clarifies that the philosophers (Aristotelians like Ibn Sina) do not go as far as the Mu'tazila and Shi'a in affirming the subsistence of non-existent things. The philosophers themselves are divided: the ancient philosophers (Pythagoreans and Platonists) held erroneous views about numbers, magnitudes, and Platonic forms existing externally. Aristotle refuted them. However, Aristotle and his followers themselves fell into error by claiming that prime matter (hyle) and specific realities exist externally separate from sensible forms. Ibn Taymiyya declares this false, just as the atomist view is false. He notes that many Islamic theological schools do not affirm either view. Then he states the correct distinction: quiddity is the mental imprint of a thing, and existence is the actual external thing. The distinction between mental and external is sound; but positing a quiddity that is neither known nor existent is false. He further explains that the terms 'quiddity' and 'existence' can refer to mental or external things: if both refer to the same domain (both mental or both external), they are identical; only when one is mental and the other external is there a difference. The philosophers' distinction amounts to merely inventing quiddities in the mind, which is trivial and leads to relativism.

B) When both refer to the same domain (both mental or both external).
C) When one refers to mental and the other to external.
D) They are never identical.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's correct distinction is between mental concept and external reality, not between two extra-mental entities; when terms refer to the same domain, they denote the same thing.

Badihi and Nazari are Relative: What is Self-evident Varies
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the distinction between intuitive and theoretical knowledge is purely relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the badihi/nazari distinction is relative and apply it to evaluate claims of self-evidence in Islamic theological arguments.

Core Idea
The 'self-evident' is not universal—what is obvious to one scholar may require lengthy reasoning for another.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: the division of knowledge into the self-evident (badihi) and the theoretical (nazari). While logicians treat this division as fixed and universal, Ibn Taymiyya argues it is purely relative. He builds on the earlier discussion of concepts and provides a definition of self-evident affirmation, then shows how its application varies by person. This reframing has profound implications for how we assess the foundations of arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
What exact claim of the logicians is Ibn Taymiyya attacking? He attacks the fixed, universal distinction between badihi and nazari, and the assumption that what is self-evident is the same for all rational beings.
2
Trace his argument structure
Ibn Taymiyya notes that logicians agree both badihi and nazari exist, and that nazari depends on badihi to avoid infinite regress. He then argues that the difference is 'only by relation and addition' (nisba wa idafa), meaning it is relative to each person.
He uses the logicians' own premises to undermine their conclusion.
3
Examine his definition of badihi
He defines badihi as that for which merely conceiving the subject and predicate suffices to yield the judgment, without need for a middle term. This definition is standard, but he emphasizes that whether this holds depends on the individual's cognitive capacity.
The definition itself is not contested; its application is.
4
Draw the epistemological implication
If badihi is relative, then what is foundational for one person may require proof for another. This has consequences for how we treat first principles in Islamic theology and jurisprudence—they cannot be assumed universally self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: الفرق بين البديهي والنظري أمر نسبي محض. أن يقال : هم معترفون بما لا بد منه من أن التصديقات منها بديهي ومنها نظري، وأنه يمتنع أن تكون كلها نظرية لافتقار النظري إلى البديهي. وإذا كان كذلك فالفرق بين البديهي والنظري إنما هو بالنسبة والإضافة. فقد يَبْدَه هذا من العلم ويبتدىء في نفسه ما يكون بديهياً له، وإن كان غيره لا يناله إلا بنظر قصير أو طويل، بل قد يكون غيره يتعسّر عليه حصوله بالنظر. وقد تقدم التنبيه على هذا في التصورات. لكن نزيده هنا دليلاً يختص هذا، فنقول : البديهي من التصديقات : هو ما يكفي تصور طرفيه – موضوعه ومحموله – في حصول تصديقه، فلا يتوقف على وسط يكون بينهما أو ما يكون كل من تصوراته وتصديقاته بديهياً لا يفتقر إلى وسط يكون بين الموضوع والمحمول.
The second: The difference between the intuitive and the theoretical is a purely relative matter. It is said: They acknowledge the necessity that among affirmations some are intuitive and some are theoretical, and that it is impossible for all to be theoretical because the theoretical depends on the intuitive. And if that is so, then the difference between the intuitive and the theoretical is only by relation and addition. For something may become intuitive in knowledge and begin in oneself as being intuitive for him, even if another cannot obtain it except by short or long reasoning; nay, another may find it difficult to obtain through reasoning. And the warning on this has already preceded in the conceptions. But we increase here a proof specific to this, and we say: The intuitive among affirmations is that for which the conception of its two extremes – its subject and predicate – suffices for obtaining its affirmation, so it does not depend on a middle term between them; or one whose every conception and affirmation is intuitive does not need a middle term between subject and predicate.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the relativity of the distinction between the self-evident (badīhī) and the theoretical (naẓarī). Ibn Taymiyya first notes that both logicians agree that some affirmations are self-evident and some require reasoning, and that not all can be theoretical because that would cause infinite regress. The key point: the distinction is relative (nisbī) and relational (iḍāfī). What is self-evident for one person may require long reasoning for another, or may even be impossible for another to obtain through reasoning. He references an earlier discussion on conceptions. He then gives a definition: a self-evident affirmation is one where merely conceiving the subject and predicate yields the judgment, without need for a middle term. This definition is standard, but he emphasizes that the application varies by person. This guards against the mistaken view that logical categories are fixed and universal—they depend on individual intellectual capacity.

Key Terms
بديهي
نظري
نسبة وإضافة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's key point is that the division between self-evident and theoretical knowledge is not fixed but relative to the individual knower, which challenges the universal claims of classical logic.

The Relativity of Intuitive and Theoretical Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the distinction between intuitive and theoretical knowledge is relative and defines intuitive affirmations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the distinction between intuitive and theoretical knowledge is relative and apply his definition of intuitive knowledge to Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Intuitive knowledge is relative to the knower; what is self-evident to one may need proof for another.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' fixed division of knowledge into intuitive (badihi) and theoretical (nazari). He argues that this division is relative, varying from person to person. He then provides a precise definition of intuitive in affirmations: it is knowledge obtained simply by understanding the subject and predicate, without requiring a middle term. This lesson will help you grasp the epistemological flexibility Ibn Taymiyya introduces and its implications for how we evaluate arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doctrine being criticized
Recognize that Ibn Taymiyya is responding to the logicians who claim that some propositions are inherently intuitive and others inherently theoretical, with no overlap.
The logicians treat badihi as fixed and universal.
2
Extract the key principle: relativity
Ibn Taymiyya states that the difference between badihi and nazari is 'only in terms of relation and addition' (بالنسبة والإضافة). This means what is intuitive for one person may be theoretical for another.
He gives the example that one person may grasp something immediately while another needs reasoning.
3
Analyze the definition of badihi
In the context of affirmations (tasdiqat), badihi is defined as that for which conceptualizing its subject and predicate is sufficient for affirmation, without needing a middle term (wasat).
This definition emphasizes the immediacy of intuitive knowledge.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'Wine (khamr) is haram.' A trained jurist may intuitively know this because the connection intoxicant-haram is immediate for him. A layperson may need a syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram, wine is an intoxicant, therefore wine is haram.'
This shows relativity: the same proposition can be badihi for one, nazari for another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني
الفرق بين البديهي و النظري أمر نسبي محض
أن يقال : هم معترفون بما لا بد منه من أن التصديقات منها بديهي ومنها نظري، وأنه يمتنع أن تكون كلها نظرية لافتقار النظري إلى البديهي
وإذا كان كذلك فالفرق بين البديهي والنظري إنما هو بالنسبة والإضافة.
فقد يبده هذا من العلم ويبتدىء في نفسه ما يكون بديهياً له، وإن كان غيره لا يناله إلا بنظر قصير أو طويل، بل قد يكون غيره يتعسر عليه حصوله بالنظر. وقد تقدم التنبيه على هذا في التصورات. لكن نزيده هنا دليلاً يختص هذا، فنقول :
البديهي من التصديقات : هو ما يكفي تصور طرفيه - موضوعه ومحموله - في حصول تصديقه، فلا يتوقف على وسط يكون بينهما أو ما يكون كل من تصوراته وتصديقاته بديهياً لا يفتقر إلى وسط يكون بين الموضوع والمحمول
The second [point]: The difference between the intuitive and the theoretical is purely relative. It should be said: They acknowledge what is necessary, that affirmations include both intuitive and theoretical, and that it is impossible for all to be theoretical because the theoretical depends on the intuitive. If so, the difference between intuitive and theoretical is only in terms of relation and addition. For something may be intuitive to a person and begun in itself, while another person cannot attain it except through short or lengthy reasoning, indeed another may find it difficult to attain even through reasoning. This has been indicated earlier in the concepts. But here we add a proof specific to this, and we say: The intuitive among affirmations is that whose two ends – its subject and predicate – are sufficient in their conceptualization for attaining its affirmation, so it does not depend on a middle term between them, or that all its conceptualizations and affirmations are intuitive and do not require a middle term between subject and predicate.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion. Ibn Taymiyya explains that the division of knowledge into badihi (intuitive) and nazari (theoretical) is relative, not absolute. He gives the analogy that what is intuitive for one person may require reasoning for another. He then defines badihi in affirmations: it is that which is known simply by conceptualizing the subject and predicate, without needing a middle term. This definition emphasizes that intuitive knowledge is direct and immediate. The relativity is important because it undermines the logicians' rigid classification. For example, the jurist may intuitively know that wine is haram due to his training, while a layman needs a syllogism.

Key Takeaway
The division into intuitive and theoretical knowledge is relative, not absolute, because what is self-evident to one person may require reasoning for another.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Intuitive Universal Knowledge
Critique of logicians' claim about intuitive universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that particular knowledge is epistemologically prior to universal knowledge and identify the role of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) in deriving universals.

Core Idea
Universal knowledge is not purely intuitive; it arises from certainty about particulars and is extended by analogy.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that universal judgments are known by pure intuition (badiha) and necessity (darura). He argues that careful observation shows that certainty about particular sensible objects is stronger and more immediate to human instinct. Universals are derived later through analogical and causal reasoning, much as jurists derive legal rulings. This lesson will guide you through his epistemological critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being critiqued.
The logicians assert that universal judgments are known by intuition and necessity, without dependence on particulars. This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's criticism.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-assertion.
He says knowledge of particulars is more certain and precedes universals in human instinct. The certainty of rational people about particular sensibles is greater than their certainty about universals.
3
Analyze his example.
A person knows he is sentient and capable of voluntary movement before knowing the universal 'every human is sentient', and that before knowing 'every animal is sentient'. This shows particular knowledge is prior.
4
Understand the reasoning mechanism.
The soul judges absent cases by analogy (tamthil) and causal reasoning (ta'lil), not by formal demonstration (burhan). This is how universals are derived from particulars.
5
Connect to Islamic jurisprudence.
Jurists use qiyas al-tamthil to derive legal rulings from original cases, showing that this method is epistemically valid and natural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - استنباط الكليات من الجزئيات بالتمثيل. الكلي) من واهب «العقل - أو قالوا من العقل الفعّال عندهم أو نحو ذلك، قيل لهم: جهل. الكلام في ما به يُعلم أن ذلك الحكم الكلي الذي في النفس «علم» - لا ظن ولا والضرورة فإن قالوا: هذا يُعلم بالبديهة والضرورة كان هذا قولاً بأن هذه القضايا الكلية العلم بالبديهة معلومة بالبديهة والضرورة، وأن النفس مضطرة إلى هذا العلم. وهذا إن كان حقاً فالعلم بالأعيان المعينة وبأنواع الكليات يحصل أيضاً في النفس بالبديهة والضرورة، كما هو الواقع . فإن جزم العقلاء بـ «الشخصيات من الحسيات أعظم من جَزْمهم بـ « الكليات»، جزمهم بكلية الأنواع أعظم من جزمهم بكلية الأجناس». والعلم بـ «الجزئيات» / أسبق إلى الفطرة، فجزم الفطرة بها أقوى ؛ ثم كلما قوي العقل اتسعت الكليات». وحينئذ فلا يجوز أن يقال: إن العلم بـ «الأشخاص موقوف على العلم بـ الأنواع والأجناس ، ولا أن العلم بـ الأنواع ) موقوف على العلم بـ «الأجناس». بل قد يعلم الإنسان أنه حساس متحرك بالإرادة قبل أن يعلم أن «كل إنسان كذلك»، ويعلم أن الإنسان كذلك قبل أن يعلم أن كلَّ حيوان كذلك». فلم يبق علمه بـ «أنه» أو بـ «أن غيره من الحيوان حساس متحرك بالإرادة» موقوفاً على «البرهان» . وإذا علم حكم سائر الناس وسائر الحيوان، فالنفس تحكم بذلك بواسطة علمها أن ذاك الغائب مثل هذا الشاهد أو أنه يساويه في السبب الموجب لكونه حساساً، متحركاً بالإرادة»، ونحو ذلك من قياس التمثيل والتعليل الذي يحتج به الفقهاء في إثبات الأحكام الشرعية.
The Third Station - Deriving universals from particulars by analogical reasoning. As for the universal from the donator of intellect - or they say from the Active Intellect according to them or similar - it was said to them: ignorance. The discussion concerns that by which it is known that that universal judgment which is in the soul is knowledge - not opinion nor necessity. For if they say: this is known by intuition and necessity, this would be a saying that these universal propositions are known by intuition and necessity, and that the soul is compelled to this knowledge. If this is true, then knowledge of specific individuals and of types of universals also occurs in the soul by intuition and necessity, as is reality. The certainty of rational people about particular sensibles is greater than their certainty about universals; their certainty about the universality of species is greater than their certainty about the universality of genera. And knowledge of particulars precedes instinct, so the instinct's certainty about them is stronger; then as the intellect strengthens, universals expand. Thus it is not permissible to say that knowledge of individuals depends on knowledge of species and genera, nor that knowledge of species depends on knowledge of genera. Rather, a person may know that he is sentient and capable of voluntary movement before he knows that every human is like that, and he knows that man is like that before he knows that every animal is like that. So his knowledge about himself or about other animals being sentient and capable of voluntary movement is not dependent on demonstration. And when he knows the ruling of other people and other animals, the soul judges that by virtue of its knowledge that that absent one is similar to this present one or that it equals it in the cause necessitating its being sentient and capable of voluntary movement, and similar to that from analogical reasoning and causal reasoning which the jurists use in establishing legal rulings.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a critical epistemological argument against the logicians' claim that universal judgments are known by pure intuition (badiha) and necessity (darura). The author, Ibn Taymiyya, begins by stating the topic: deriving universals from particulars through analogical reasoning (tamthil). He then addresses the logicians' assertion that universal knowledge is intuitive, arguing that if that were true, then knowledge of specific individuals and types of universals would also be intuitive, which is the case. However, he argues that certainty about particular sensible objects is actually stronger and more immediate to human instinct than certainty about universals. As the intellect grows, universals expand, but they are not prior. He provides an example: a person knows he is sentient and has voluntary movement before knowing the universal proposition 'every human is sentient,' and that before knowing 'every animal is sentient.' Therefore, knowledge of particular cases does not require a formal demonstration (burhan). Instead, the soul judges absent cases by analogy and causal reasoning (ta'lil), as jurists do in deriving legal rulings. The commentary should follow the author's order: first the critique of intuition, then the priority of particular knowledge, then the role of analogical reasoning in deriving universals.

Checkpoint

Critical History of Philosophical Errors on Universals
Author narrates and refutes the history of philosophical error on universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the sequence of philosophical errors on universals and apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique to distinguish mental universals from external particulars.

Core Idea
Every attempt to place universals outside the mind fails; only the Islamic critique restores the truth.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya presents a concise history of philosophical thought on universals, from Pythagoras to Aristotle, and then delivers his own definitive critique. You will learn how each school erred and why Ibn Taymiyya's position—that universals exist only in the mind—is the correct one. This historical critique is essential for understanding the Islamic epistemological stance that grounds knowledge in the mind's correspondence to reality, not in the existence of abstract entities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three philosophical positions on universals
Find Pythagoras' view (abstract numbers externally exist), Plato's view (abstract essences externally exist), Aristotle's view (universals exist in external particulars).
Ibn Taymiyya presents these as a chain of corrections, each subsequent philosopher recognizing the error of his predecessor.
2
Pinpoint Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of each
He refutes Pythagoras and Plato by stating their errors were later recognized; he refutes Aristotle by asserting that nothing in the external world is universal; all external existents are particular.
Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of Aristotle is key: even the 'natural universal' is not external.
3
Understand the 'natural universal' distinction
Ibn Taymiyya explains that when people speak of the natural universal in the external world, they mean the mental universal corresponds to external individuals, not that universality is external.
This correspondence is like the general to its individuals.
4
Analyze the linguistic evidence
He supports his view by citing Quranic verse (Surah Yusuf 68) and Umar's saying to show that mental content is often described as 'in the mind' and can be said to exist externally metaphorically.
This shows that universality is a mental attribute, not an external one.
5
Grasp the self-refutation of essences neither in mind nor external
Ibn Taymiyya dismisses such claims as conceptually incoherent: a complete conceptualization reveals their falsity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استطراد. فيثاغورس مبتدأ فلسفة وهذا كان مبتدأ فلسفتهم التي وضعها فيثاغورس، وكانوا يسمون أصحابه «أصحاب العدد»، وكانوا يظنون أن الأعداد المجردة موجودة خارج الذهن. ثم تبين لأفلاطون وأصحابه غلط ذلك، وظنوا أن الماهيات المجردة كـ «الإنسان المطلق» و «الفرس المطلق» موجودات خارج الذهن، وأنها أزلية أبدية. ثم تبين لأرسطو وأصحابه غلط ذلك، فقالوا: بل هذه الماهيات المطلقة موجودة في الخارج مقارنة لوجود الأشخاص. ومشى مَنْ مشى من أتباع أرسطو من المتأخرين على هذا وهو أيضاً غلط فإن ما في الخارج ليس بكلي أصلاً، وليس في الخارج إلا ما هو معيّن مخصوص. وإذا قيل الكلي الطبيعي في الخارج فمعناه أن ما هو كلي في الذهن هو مطابق للأفراد الموجودة في الخارج مطابقة العام لأفراده. والموجود في الخارج معيناً مختص، ليس بكلّي أصلاً: ولكن فيه حصّتُه من الكلي. المقام الثالث - الوجه الرابع: مبتدأ فلسفة فيثاغورس هو «الأعداد المجردة». وما في الذهن يطلق عليه أنه قد يوجد في الخارج، كما يقال: «فعلتُ ما في نفسي»، و «في نفسي أمور أريد فعلها»، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا حَاجَةً فِي نَفْسِ يَعْقُوبَ قضَهَا ﴾ [سورة يوسف: ٦٨]، وقول عمر: كنت زَوَّرْتُ في نفسي مقالة أحببتُ أن أقولها(١). ونظائره كثيرة. والكلي إذا وجد في الخارج لا يكون إلا معيناً، لا يكون كلياً. فكونه كلياً مشروط بكونه في الذهن. ومن أثبت ماهية لا في الذهن ولا في الخارج، فتصور قوله تصوراً تاماً يكفي في العلم بفساد قوله. وهذه الأمور مبسوطة في غير هذا الموضع.
Digression. Pythagoras is the founder of philosophy, and this was the beginning of their philosophy which Pythagoras established. They called his companions 'the people of number', and they thought that abstract numbers exist outside the mind. Then it became clear to Plato and his companions the error of that, and they thought that abstract essences like 'absolute man' and 'absolute horse' exist outside the mind, and that they are eternal and everlasting. Then it became clear to Aristotle and his companions the error of that, so they said: Rather these absolute essences exist in the external world in conjunction with the existence of individuals. And some of the later followers of Aristotle followed this, and it is also an error, because what is in the external world is not universal at all; there is nothing in the external world except what is determinate and particular. And if it is said that the natural universal is in the external world, its meaning is that what is universal in the mind corresponds to the individuals existing in the external world in the correspondence of the general to its individuals. And what exists in the external world is determinate and particular, not universal at all, but it has its share of the universal. The third station - the fourth aspect: The foundation of the philosophy of Pythagoras is 'abstract numbers'. And what is in the mind is said to exist in the external world, as one says: 'I did what was in my mind', and 'there are matters in my mind that I want to do', and from that is the saying of the Most High: 'Except a need in the soul of Ya'qub which he fulfilled' [Surah Yusuf: 68], and the saying of Umar: 'I had prepared in my mind a speech that I wanted to deliver' (1). And many similar examples. And the universal, if it exists in the external world, it is only particular, not universal. So its being universal is conditional upon its being in the mind. And whoever posits an essence neither in the mind nor in the external world, a complete conceptualization of his statement suffices for knowing the falsity of his statement. These matters are elaborated elsewhere.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a critical historical narrative in which Ibn Taymiyya traces the errors of philosophers regarding the existence of universals. He begins with Pythagoras and his followers ('people of number') who believed abstract numbers exist externally. Then Plato and his followers corrected this but fell into a different error: they held that abstract essences like 'absolute man' exist externally as eternal entities. Next, Aristotle and his followers corrected Plato's view, asserting that universal essences exist externally only in conjunction with particular individuals. Ibn Taymiyya then refutes even Aristotle's position: what exists externally is always particular, not universal. He explains that when people speak of the 'natural universal' in the external world, they mean the mental universal corresponds to external individuals, not that universality exists externally. He reinforces this by noting that mental content is often described as 'in the mind' and may be said to 'exist externally' in a metaphorical sense, citing Qur'anic and historical examples. The universal's universality is conditional on being in the mind. Finally, he dismisses any claim of an essence neither in mind nor external as self-refuting. This critique establishes Ibn Taymiyya's nominalist-leaning position that universals are mental constructs, not external realities.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
الماهيات المجردة
الأعداد المجردة

B) It exists externally only as a particular individual
C) It exists as a mental concept that corresponds to external particulars
D) It does not exist at all

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique establishes that universals are mental constructs; external reality is composed only of particular individuals.
