Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Soul Perfection via Metaphysics
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the soul's perfection via abstract metaphysics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the philosophers' highest science does not perfect the soul, and contrast it with his positive account of perfection through knowledge of God and righteous deeds.

Core Idea
Soul perfection requires knowledge of God with righteous deeds, not abstract metaphysics.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the philosophers' claim that the soul is perfected through abstract universals. Even if we grant that, he argues, their highest science—the study of existence and its divisions—fails to deliver true perfection. This lesson dissects his critique and clarifies his alternative vision of human perfection rooted in Islamic teachings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya states the view he is refuting: that the soul is perfected by abstract universals (al-kulliyāt al-mujarrada).
This is the target of his critique.
2
Note his concessive move
He says 'Suppose that the soul is perfected by abstract universals as they claimed' — this grants their premise for the sake of argument.
He then shows that even on their own terms, their science fails.
3
Extract his counter-argument
He argues that the concept of existence (al-wujūd) is self-evident and not the ultimate goal. What they study are divisions of existence (necessary/possible, etc.), which are more specific but still only abstract categorizations.
These divisions do not constitute knowledge of God or righteous action.
4
Contrast with his positive thesis
The perfection of the soul is through knowledge of God with righteous deeds (ma‘rifat Allāh ma‘a al-‘amal al-ṣāliḥ), not mere metaphysical study.
This aligns with Islamic teachings on tazkiyah (purification).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس

كمال النفس بمعرفة الله مع العمل الصالح، لا بمجرد معرفة الله فضلاً عن كونه يحصل بمجرد علم الفلسفة

الوجه الخامس أن يقال : هب أن النفس تكمل بالكليات المجردة كما زعموه، فما يذكرونه في العلم الأعلى عندهم الناظر في الوجود ولواحقه ليس كذلك . فإن تصور معنى الوجود فقط أمر ظاهر حتى يستغني عن الحد عندهم لظهوره. فليس هو المطلوب. وإنما المطلوب أقسامه .

ونفس انقسامه إلى واجب وممكن وجوهر وعرض، وعلة ومعلول، وقديم، وحادث، هو أخص من مسمى الوجود. وليس في مجرد معرفة انقسام الأمر العام في
The Fifth Aspect: The perfection of the soul is through knowledge of God with righteous action, not merely through knowledge of God, let alone it being attained merely through the science of philosophy. The Fifth Aspect is to say: Suppose that the soul is perfected by abstract universals as they claimed, yet what they mention in their highest science, which examines existence and its concomitants, is not so. For the conception of the meaning of existence alone is something self-evident that dispenses with definition according to them due to its obviousness. It is not what is sought. Rather, what is sought are its divisions. The very division of existence into Necessary and possible, substance and accident, cause and effect, eternal and originated, is more specific than the name 'existence'. And mere knowledge of the division of the universal matter in...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique. He states the central claim: the perfection of the soul is attained through knowledge of God accompanied by righteous deeds, not merely through knowledge of God, and certainly not through the mere science of philosophy. He then presents an argument: Even if we grant that the soul is perfected by abstract universals as the philosophers claim, what they present in their 'highest science'—the study of existence and its concomitants—does not actually achieve this. The concept of existence is self-evident and does not require definition, so it is not the sought-after knowledge. What is sought is the division of existence into its categories: necessary and possible, substance and accident, cause and effect, eternal and originated. These divisions are more specific than the mere term 'existence'. However, mere knowledge of the division of the general matter—the passage cuts off, but the argument is that such abstract divisions do not constitute true knowledge of the particular reality of God or the soul's perfection.

Key Takeaway
Even if abstract universals could perfect the soul, the philosophers' highest science only studies self-evident existence and its divisions, which do not constitute true perfection; true perfection is knowledge of God with righteous deeds.

Soul Perfection: Beyond Mere Knowledge
Author refutes the claim that soul perfection comes from mere knowledge by highlighting its two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya’s argument against the sufficiency of mere knowledge for soul perfection, and identify the two faculties of the soul.

Core Idea
Soul perfection requires both theoretical knowledge and practical worship.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya refutes the philosophers’ claim that the soul can be perfected by theoretical knowledge alone. He introduces the soul’s two fundamental powers—cognitive and volitional—and argues that true perfection comes only through worship that integrates knowledge, love, and humility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the claim that Ibn Taymiyya refutes: that the soul is perfected by mere knowledge. Note that he calls this a false statement (qawl batil).
2
Extract the counter-argument’s premise
Locate the premise that the soul has two powers: theoretical-cognitive and volitional-practical. This is the foundation of his argument.
3
Understand the necessity of both powers
Why does having two powers imply that mere knowledge is insufficient? Trace the reasoning: each power must attain its own perfection.
Theoretical power is perfected by knowledge; practical power by action and worship.
4
Reconstruct the full argument
Combine the steps: (1) The soul has two powers. (2) Each power must be perfected. (3) Knowledge perfects only the theoretical power. (4) Therefore, worship (which includes action and love) is needed to perfect the practical power. (5) Hence, perfection requires both knowledge and worship.
This is a practical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا التنبيه على أنه لو قدّر أن النفس تكمل بمجرد العلم كما زعموه؛ فإنه قول باطل. فإن النفس لها قوتان: قوة علمية نظرية وقوة إرادية عملية. فلا بد لها من كمال القوتين بمعرفة الله وعبادته. ولا تكمل نفس قط إلا بعبادة الله وحده لا شريك له. والعبادة تجمع معرفته ومحبته والذل له. وبهذا بعث الله الرسل وأنزل الكتب الإلهية؛ كلها تدعو إلى عبادة الله وحده لا شريك له.
And the intended point here is to draw attention to the fact that if it were assumed that the soul attains perfection through mere knowledge as they claim, then that is a false statement. For the soul has two powers: a theoretical cognitive power and a volitional practical power. Thus it must have perfection of both powers through knowledge of God and worship of Him. And no soul ever attains perfection except by worship of God alone, with no partner. And worship combines knowledge of Him, love of Him, and humility before Him. And with this, God sent the messengers and revealed the divine scriptures; all of them call to worship of God alone, with no partner.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophers' view that the soul is perfected solely by theoretical knowledge ('ilm). Ibn Taymiyya argues that the soul has two essential faculties: a theoretical-cognitive power (quwwa 'ilmiyya nazariyya) and a volitional-practical power (quwwa iradiyya 'amaliyya). Therefore, perfecting the soul requires both correct knowledge and righteous action, which is worship (ibada). Worship, in turn, encompasses knowledge, love, and humility. This comprehensive purpose is the essence of all prophecies and scriptures.

Key Terms
قوة علمية نظرية (quwwa 'ilmiyya nazariyya): The soul's theoretical power that grasps universals and intellectual knowledge. [Note: Standard usage in Islamic psychology; consistent with al-Ghazali and others.]
قوة إرادية عملية (quwwa iradiyya 'amaliyya): The soul's volitional power that drives action and moral choices. [Note: No significant shift.]
عبادة ('ibada): Worship that integrates knowledge, love, and humility toward God. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya's definition is broader than ritual acts; it encompasses submission and devotion.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only theoretical knowledge.
B) Only practical action.
C) Both knowledge and worship.
D) Rejecting all knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The soul is perfected only when both its theoretical and practical powers are fulfilled through knowledge of God and worship (which combines knowledge, love, and humility).

Ibn Taymiyya: Particular Conceptions Before Universal Propositions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that particular conceptions precede and are more basic than universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Ibn Taymiyya uses examples of particular knowledge to argue against the priority of universal propositions in epistemology.

Core Idea
Particular knowledge is more immediate than universal rules; experience grounds abstraction.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a foundational assumption of Aristotelian logic: that universal propositions are the primary building blocks of knowledge. He insists, instead, that our first grasp of reality is always through particular, concrete instances—like knowing your own body is bigger than your hand before ever formulating the rule 'every whole is greater than its part.' This lesson walks through his argument and his rebuttal to the rationalist claim that universals are innate gifts from the 'Bestower of intellect.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal propositions
Notice the universal statements Ibn Taymiyya mentions: 'every whole is greater than its part,' 'two contradictories cannot coexist,' 'two opposites cannot coexist.' These are al-qadiyya al-kulliyya (universal propositions).
These are the kind of statements the philosophers treat as self-evident or divinely bestowed.
2
Observe the particular examples
Ibn Taymiyya gives concrete examples: a person's body is greater than their hand; the sky is greater than its planets; a particular thing cannot be both existent and non-existent; a specific black cannot also be white. These are particular conceptions (tasawwur mu'ayyan).
He is using everyday experiences that anyone can verify.
3
Understand the priority argument
He claims that the particular conception comes first in the mind (asbaq). For instance, you first imagine your body > hand, then later you may formulate the universal. The particular is directly experienced; the universal is abstracted.
This challenges the Aristotelian ordering where universals are known before particulars.
4
Examine the response to the 'Bestower of intellect' objection
An opponent might say: 'These universals are innate from wahib al-'aql.' Ibn Taymiyya counters: if so, then the particular propositions are even closer to the Bestower—more immediate and direct.
This turns the rationalist argument back on itself, suggesting particulars are more fundamental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، ونحو ذلك. فإن هذه قضايا كلية. تصور المعين العقل. ومعلوم أن الإنسان إذا تصور ما يتصوره من شيء معين وجزئه، فإن تصوره أسبق - لكون هذا الكل المعين أعظم من جزئه - أسبق إلى عقله من أن يتخيل أن كل كل أعظم من جزئه. فهو يتصور أن بدنه أعظم من يده ورجله، وأن السماء أعظم من كواكبها، والجبل أعظم من بعضه، والمدينة أعظم من بعضها، ونحو ذلك، قبل أن يتصور القضية الكلية الشاملة لجميع هذه الأفراد. وكذلك إذا تصور شيئاً معيناً يعلم أنه لا يكون موجوداً معدوماً في حال واحدة، يتصور أن كل نقيضين لا يجتمعان. وكذلك إذا تصور سواداً معيناً علم أنه لا يكون اللون الواحد سواداً بياضاً قبل أن يتصور أن كل ضدين لا يجتمعان. وأمثال ذلك كثيرة. وإذا قيل: تلك القضية الكلية تحصل في الذهن ضرورة أو بديهة من واهب العقل، قيل: فحصول تلك القضية المعينة في الذهن من واهب العقل أقرب.
Two opposites cannot coexist and cannot both be absent, and the like. These are universal propositions. The conception of the particular intellect. And it is known that when a man conceives what he conceives of a particular thing and its part, his conception is prior—since this particular whole is greater than its part—it is prior in his mind to imagining that 'every whole is greater than its part.' For he conceives that his body is greater than his hand and his leg, and that the sky is greater than its planets, and the mountain is greater than part of it, and the city is greater than part of it, and the like, before he conceives the universal proposition encompassing all these individuals. Likewise, when he conceives a particular thing and knows that it cannot be existent and non-existent in a single state, he conceives that 'two contradictories cannot coexist.' And likewise, when he conceives a particular blackness, he knows that a single color cannot be both black and white, before he conceives that 'two opposites cannot coexist.' And many similar examples. And if it is said: that universal proposition occurs in the mind necessarily or as a primary intuition from the Bestower of intellect, it is said: the occurrence of that particular proposition in the mind from the Bestower of intellect is closer.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological argument that particular conceptions (tasawwur mu'ayyan) are more immediate and prior to universal propositions. He begins by giving everyday examples: a person knows that his own body is greater than his hand, that the sky is greater than its planets, etc., before formulating the universal rule 'every whole is greater than its part.' Similarly, he knows that a particular thing cannot both exist and not exist at the same time before grasping the principle of non-contradiction. He then addresses a potential objection: perhaps these universal propositions are innate from the Bestower of intellect (wahib al-'aql). Ibn Taymiyya responds that if that were the case, then the particular propositions are even closer to the Bestower of intellect, implying they are more directly given. This undercuts the rationalist priority of universals and supports his empiricist-like epistemology rooted in particular experience.

Key Terms
تصور المعين (tasawwur al-mu'ayyan): Conception of a particular, individual thing as opposed to a universal concept.
القضية الكلية (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya): A universal proposition that applies to all individuals of a kind, such as 'every whole is greater than its part.'
واهب العقل (wahib al-'aql): The Bestower of intellect, a term used by philosophers to refer to the source of innate rational knowledge. [Note: In Ibn Taymiyya's critique, this term is used polemically; he questions the philosophical claim that universals are directly bestowed.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, which does a person conceive first?
A) The universal proposition that every whole is greater than its part.
B) The particular fact that his body is greater than his hand.
C) Both simultaneously.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that concrete, particular knowledge forms the foundation for abstract universal principles, challenging the rationalist priority of innatism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' 8 min
The Salaf's knowledge refutes Greek logic's necessity
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the necessity of Greek logic using the Salaf and al-mizan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the Salaf's knowledge refutes the necessity of Greek logic, and identify the Qur'anic concept of al-mizan as an alternative epistemic standard.

Core Idea
The Salaf's perfect knowledge before Greek logic proves that divine guidance suffices; the 'Balance' is any sound reasoning, not Greek formalism.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the core claim that Greek logic is necessary for reliable knowledge. He points to the Salaf, who attained the highest levels of knowledge and faith without ever encountering the logic of the Greeks. Then, he introduces the Qur'anic concept of al-mizan—the Balance—as the God-given capacity for reasoning that is universal and sufficient. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it undermines the logicians' pretensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the targeted claim
Find the claim that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking. It is the assertion that knowledge cannot be trusted unless it is weighed by Greek logical method, or that human minds cannot be sound without it.
2
Present the Salaf as counterevidence
Ibn Taymiyya points to the Companions, Followers, and Imams of the Muslims. They had perfect knowledge and faith before any contact with Greek logic. This is an empirical refutation: if the claim were true, they would have been in error, but we know they were the best of the Ummah.
3
Understand the logicians' defense
The logicians might respond that they do not insist on their own terminology, but on the universal meanings behind it. Ibn Taymiyya concedes that unknowns are only known through knowns, but he denies that this requires their specific system.
4
Introduce al-mizan as alternative
Ibn Taymiyya quotes Qur'anic verses where Allah mentions sending down the Book and the Balance (al-mizan). He interprets this as the innate ability to weigh the unknown against the known, which is present in all nations, even those ignorant of Greek logic. Thus, the need for Greek logic is negated.
5
Assess the strength of the critique
Evaluate whether Ibn Taymiyya's argument successfully refutes the logicians' claim. Note that he does not reject reasoning itself, but rather the exclusivity of the Greek formal method. The critique uses historical evidence and revelation, which in his framework are superior to purely rationalist claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعلوم أن أفضل هذه الأمة من الصحابة والتابعين لهم بإحسان، وأئمة المسلمين - عَرَفوا ما يجب عليهم ، وكَمُل علمهم وإيمانهم، قبل أن يُعرف منطق اليونان .. فكيف يقال : إنه لا يوثق بالعلم إن لم يوزَن به»، أو يقال : إن فطر بني آدم في الغالب لا تستقيم إلا به .
فإن قالوا : نحن لا نقول إن الناس يحتاجون إلى اصطلاح المنطقيين بل إلى المعاني التي توزن بها العلوم، قيل: لا ريب أن المجهولات لا تعرف إلا بالمعلومات، والناس محتاجون إلى أن يَزِنوا ما جهلوه بما علموه . وهذا من الميزان التي أنزلها الله حيث قال : هو اللَّهُ الَّذِى أَنزَلَ الْكِتَبَ بِالْحَقِّ وَالْمِيزَانُ، وقال: لَقَدْ أَرْسَلْنَا رُسُلَنَا بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ وَأَنزَلْنَا مَعَهُمُ الْكِتَابَ وَالْمِيزَانَ. وهذا موجود عند أمتنا وغير أمتنا ممن لم يسمع قطُّ بمنطق اليونان. فعلم أن الأمم غير محتاجة إليه .
ملخص دعاوي أهل المنطق وكذبها
وهم لا يدَّعون احتياج الناس إلى نفس ألفاظ اليونان، بل يدعون الحاجة إلى المعقولات المعاني المنطقية التي عبروا عنها بلسانهم، وهو كلامهم في المعقولات الثانية»، فإن موضوع المنطق هو المعقولات الثانية من حيث يتوصل بها إلى علم ما لم يعلم. فإنه ينظر في أحوال المعقولات الثانية - وهي النِّسَب الثانية - للماهيات من حيث مطلقة عَرَض لها إن كانت موصلة إلى تحصيل ما ليس بحاصل، أو معينة في ذلك، لا على وجه جزئي، بل على قانون كلي.
ويدعون أن صاحب المنطق ينظر في جنس الدليل، كما أن صاحب أصول الفقه ينظر في الدليل الشرعي ومرتبته فيميّز بين ما هو دليل شرعي وما ليس بدليل شرعي، الدليل المطلق وينظر في مراتب الأدلة حتى يقدّم الراجح على المرجوح عند التعارض. وهم يزعمون أن صاحب المنطق ينظر في الدليل المطلق الذي هو أعم من الشرعي، ويميز بين ما هو دليل وما ليس بدليل .
And it is known that the best of this Ummah from the Companions, the Followers in goodness, and the Imams of the Muslims – they knew what was obligatory upon them, and their knowledge and faith were perfected, before the logic of the Greeks was known. So how can it be said that knowledge is not reliable unless weighed with it, or that the natural dispositions of the children of Adam are generally not correct except through it? If they say: We do not say that people need the technical term of the logicians, but rather the meanings by which sciences are weighed, it is said: There is no doubt that unknowns are only known through knowns, and people need to weigh what they do not know by what they know. This is part of the balance that Allah sent down when He said: 'It is Allah who has sent down the Book with truth and the Balance' and 'We have sent Our messengers with clear proofs and sent down with them the Book and the Balance.' And this exists in our Ummah and other nations who have never heard of the logic of the Greeks. So it is known that nations are not in need of it. Summary of the claims of the logicians and their falsehood: They do not claim that people need the very words of the Greeks, but rather claim the need for the intelligibles, the logical meanings that they expressed in their language, which is their speech about the 'secondary intelligibles.' For the subject matter of logic is the secondary intelligibles insofar as they lead to knowledge of what is not known. For it examines the states of the secondary intelligibles – which are the secondary relations – of essences insofar as they are absolute, an accident occurs to them as to whether they lead to acquiring what is not obtained, or assist in that, not in a particular manner but according to a universal law. And they claim that the logician examines the genus of proof, just as the jurist of usul al-fiqh examines the legal proof and its rank, distinguishing between what is a legal proof and what is not, the absolute proof, and examines the ranks of proofs to give precedence to the preponderant over the weaker when they conflict. And they claim that the logician examines the absolute proof which is more general than the legal proof, and distinguishes between what is a proof and what is not a proof.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the claim that Greek logic is necessary for knowledge. He begins by pointing to the historical fact that the best of the Ummah, the Companions and the Imams, had complete knowledge and faith before any contact with Greek logic. This serves as an empirical refutation of the claim that knowledge cannot be relied upon unless verified by the logical method. Ibn Taymiyya then anticipates an objection: the logicians might say that they do not insist on their specific terminology, but rather on the underlying universal meanings and principles of reasoning. In response, he concedes that unknowns are indeed only known through knowns, but this is the natural process of reasoning that Allah has placed in people, which He calls 'the Balance' (al-mizan) in the Qur'an. This mizan is universal and was present in all nations, including those who never heard of Greek logic. Thus, the claim of necessity for Greek logic is false. Ibn Taymiyya then summarizes the logicians' claims: they distinguish between the literal words and the concepts, and they define logic as dealing with 'secondary intelligibles' and 'secondary relations' of essences. They analogize the logician's examination of proof to the jurist's examination of legal proof, but they claim the logician examines proof in an absolute sense, more general than legal proof. This summary sets up his refutation of their claims.

الميزان

B) Because the Salaf had perfect knowledge and faith without ever using Greek logic.
C) Because the Salaf used Indian logic instead.
D) Because Greek logic was invented after the Salaf.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Salaf's perfect knowledge before Greek logic disproves its necessity, and that the Qur'anic 'Balance' provides a sufficient epistemic standard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about the role of kalam in Islamic theology, a theologian claims that dialectical reasoning is indispensable for defending the faith. Using Ibn Taymiyya's method, explain how the practice of the Salaf can be used to argue that kalam is not necessary, even if it might be useful.
?
Analyze the claim that 'the Balance (al-mizan) is the innate ability to weigh arguments.' Give one concrete example from the life of a Companion where this ability was exercised without any formal logical training.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

The Logicians' Four Claims and Their General Refutation
Author enumerates the logicians' four claims about tasawwur and tasdiq and refutes them as false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four claims that logicians make about tasawwur and tasdiq and explain Ibn Taymiyya's general refutation of each.

Core Idea
The logicians' four claims are all false: they claim exclusivity, but truth is found in many paths.

The logicians assert that conceptualizations (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq) can only be attained through their specific methods of definition and syllogism. They also claim their method yields correct concepts and certain knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya refutes all four claims, arguing that truth is not monopolised by any single method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two negative claims
Find in the Arabic where the logicians state that conceptualizations cannot be attained except through their method, and similarly for assents. Note that these are claims of exclusivity: no other path works.
The text says: لا تُنال التصورات بغير ما ذكروه... و التصديقات لا تنال بغير ما ذكروه
2
Identify the two positive claims
Next, locate the positive claims: that their method yields the conceptualization of previously unknown realities, and that their demonstration yields certifying knowledge. Note the author's remark that the fourth is 'more plausible'.
The fourth claim is about burhan yielding yaqin.
3
Understand the logicians' overall position
The logicians claim that all rational knowledge of humans must go through their two paths (definition and syllogism) and that logic is the legal tool to weigh all scientific paths.
They say: طرق العلم على عقلاء بني آدم مسدودة إلا من الطريقين اللذين ذكروهما
4
Analyse Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
Observe that he labels all four claims as false in both negation and affirmation. He denies that other paths are entirely false, and denies that the logicians' paths are entirely true as claimed. He asserts that every speaker inevitably includes some truth.
Key line: كل هذه الدعاوي كذب في النفي والإثبات

And the discussion with them is only about the concepts they have placed in logic and claimed that four claims [about] the required conceptualizations cannot be attained except through them, and the required assents cannot be attained except through them. So they mentioned for their logic four claims—two negative and two positive. They claimed that conceptualizations cannot be attained by other than the method they mentioned in it, and that assents cannot be attained by other than the method they mentioned in it. And these two claims are among the falsest of claims. And they claimed that the method they mentioned obtains the conceptualization of realities that were not conceptualized, and this is also false. The evidence for these three claims has preceded, and the discussion of their fourth claim—which is more plausible than the others—will come, which is their claim that their demonstration yields certifying knowledge. Then if they say: verily certifying knowledge or conceptual knowledge also cannot be attained without it, so they claimed that the paths to knowledge for rational sons of Adam are blocked except for the two paths they mentioned—what they mentioned of 'definition' and what they mentioned of 'syllogism'. And they claimed that the two paths they mentioned lead to the sciences that the sons of Adam attain by their intellects, meaning that what leads must be upon the path they mentioned, not upon others. So what they mentioned is a legal tool by which scientific paths are weighed, and by which the correct path is distinguished from the corrupt. So observance of this law safeguards the mind from slipping in the thought by which a conceptualization or an assent is attained. And this is the summary of their claims. And all of these claims are false in negation and affirmation. So neither are all the paths of others they negated entirely false, nor are all the paths they affirmed entirely true in the manner they claimed, although among their paths there is truth, just as among the paths of others there is falsehood. So there is no one among them or others who composes speech except that it necessarily contains some truth.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage enumerates and refutes the logicians' four core claims. The author first states the two negative claims: (1) conceptualizations (tasawwur) cannot be attained except through their method, (2) assents (tasdiq) cannot be attained except through their method. Then the two positive claims: (3) their method yields correct conceptualizations of previously unknown realities, and (4) their demonstration (burhan) yields certifying knowledge. The author notes that the first three are false, and the fourth is more plausible but will be discussed later. He then explains that the logicians assert their definitions and syllogisms are the only paths to knowledge, and that logic is a legal tool to weigh all scientific paths. Ibn Taymiyya refutes summarily: all these claims are false in both negation and affirmation—other paths are not entirely false, nor are their paths entirely true as claimed. Every speaker inevitably includes some truth. The refutation is general, not detailed.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the logicians' exclusivity claims: truth is not the monopoly of any single method, and even their own methods contain truth alongside falsehood.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
8 min
All Arguments Reduce to Implication
Author concludes that all argument forms reduce to implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's claim that all argument forms reduce to the principle of implication and identify its consequences for the Aristotelian taxonomy of syllogisms.

Core Idea
All arguments are just implication in disguise.

You have studied the various syllogistic forms — categorical, conditional, disjunctive — that logicians meticulously classify. Ibn Taymiyya now pulls the rug out: all these forms, he says, rest on a single, simple relation — the evidence implies the conclusion. This lesson unpacks that reduction and its radical implication for the science of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the Arabic verbatim: 'فهذه الأشكال وإن تكثرت فجميعها تعود إلى أن الدليل يستلزم المدلول.' Ibn Taymiyya asserts that despite multiplicity, all forms return to one fact: the evidence necessarily implies the evidenced. Note the universal quantifier 'جميعها' (all of them).
This is a sweeping epistemological claim, not a minor observation.
2
Understand the key terms: daleel and madlool
'الدليل' (al-daleel) is the evidence or premise; 'المدلول' (al-madlool) is the conclusion or thing evidenced. The relation is 'اللزوم' (al-luzum), necessary implication. Ibn Taymiyya reduces every argument to this core relation: if the daleel is true, the madlool must be true.
In usul al-fiqh, daleel often includes Qur'an and Sunnah; here it is logical evidence.
3
Contrast with the logicians' taxonomy
The logicians claimed demonstration is limited to specific forms (e.g., the four figures of the categorical syllogism, specific conditional and disjunctive types). Ibn Taymiyya denies this limitation. He says the same implication can be represented in many forms, both those they listed and those they did not.
This directly challenges the authority of Aristotle's Organon.
4
Assess the significance
If all arguments reduce to implication, then the logicians' elaborate classification is not the final word. There can be infinitely many valid argument forms, not just six. The criterion for validity is not conformity to a schema but the truth of the implication: if the evidence is sound, the conclusion follows.
This is a democratization of logical reasoning — any sound evidence can serve as a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الأشكال وإن تكثرت فجميعها تعود إلى أن الدليل يستلزم المدلول. ويمكن تصوير ذلك بصور متعددة مما ذكروه ومما لم يذكروه.
These forms, however numerous, all reduce to the fact that the evidence implies the evidenced. And that can be represented in multiple forms, both those they mentioned and those they did not mention.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This brief passage encapsulates Ibn Taymiyya's central critique of the logicians' elaborate taxonomy. He acknowledges that there are many forms of argument, but insists they all share one essential core: the evidence (daleel) must imply the conclusion (madlool). This is the principle of luzum (implication). The specific figures, conditional types, and divisions are merely different ways of expressing that implication. Therefore, the logicians' claim that demonstration is limited to six forms is arbitrary; there are infinitely many ways to represent a valid implication, many of which are not captured by the Aristotelian system. This reduces the entire art of logic to a simple epistemological principle: if the evidence is true, then the conclusion must be true.

Key Terms
الدليل
المدلول
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reduces all argument forms to the single epistemological principle of implication, thereby rejecting the logicians' claim that demonstration is limited to a fixed set of forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a categorical syllogism from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Show how it reduces to the single implication: the evidence (the two premises combined) implies the conclusion. Then construct a different form — e.g., a conditional — that expresses the same implication.
?

List two forms of argument from Islamic theology or jurisprudence that do not fit neatly into the six, yet are clearly valid examples of evidence implying conclusion.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Errors in Kalām and Falsafah
Ibn Taymiyya critiques errors of mutakallimun and falasifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the mutual errors of mutakallimun and falasifa regarding atomism, accidents, and divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Mutual errors between theologians and philosophers empowered each other's false claims.

Ibn Taymiyya begins by listing erroneous views held by some mutakallimun and falasifa. He then shows how the theologians' denial of divine purpose allowed philosophers to dismiss Islamic teachings. This lesson unpacks that dynamic and Ibn Taymiyya's comparison of the magnitude of errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the errors attributed to the mutakallimun
Find the specific claims Ibn Taymiyya lists: that accidents cannot persist, that bodies are similar, that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms.
These are central to their atomistic metaphysics.
2
Identify the errors attributed to the falasifa
Note their claim that bodies are composed of atoms, matter, and intellectual form—Ibn Taymiyya rejects this as equally erroneous.
3
Understand how the mutakallimun's denial of divine wisdom empowered the philosophers
Some mutakallimun claimed God creates without cause or wisdom; this made it easy for philosophers to portray Islamic theology as irrational.
Ibn Taymiyya says this error 'empowered' the philosophers.
4
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's comparison of errors
He states that the philosophers' errors in theology, prophethood, and resurrection are greater than those of the mutakallimun, but in natural sciences the philosophers may be more correct.
This is a nuanced assessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المتفلسفة إنها مركبة من الجواهر المنفردة والمادة والصورة العقليين أيضاً غلط، كما قد بسط هذا كله في غير هذا الموضع . وكذلك غلط من غلط من المتكلمين وادّعى أن الله لم يخلق شيئاً بسبب ولا لحكمة، ولا خص غَلَط هؤلاء مما سَلَّط أولئك المتفلسفة، وظنوا أن ما يقوله هؤلاء وأمثالهم هو دين المسلمين، أو قول الرسول وأصحابه . ولهذا كانت مناظرة ابن سينا وأمثاله في كتبهم لمبتدعة أهل الكلام فعامة مُناظرة ابن سينا هي للمعتزلة، وابن رُشد للكلابية. وكانوا إذا بينوا فساد بعض ما يقوله مبتدعة أهل الكلام يظنون أنه لم يبق حق إلا ما يقولونه هم، وذلك بالعكس. وليس الأمر كذلك، بل ما يقوله مبتدعة أهل الكلام فيه خطأ مخالف للشرع والعقل . والخطأ فيما تقوله المتفلسفة في الإلهيات والنبوات، والمعاد، والشرائع، أعظم من خطأ المتكلمين . وأما فيما يقولونه في العلوم الطبيعية والرياضية فقد يكون صواب المتفلسفة أكثر من صواب مَنْ رَدَّ عليهم من أهل الكلام فإن أكثر كلام أهل الكلام في هذه الأمور بلا علم، ولا عقل، ولا شرع. ونحن لم نقدح فيما عُلم من الأمور الطبيعية والرياضية، لكن ذكرنا أن ما يدعونه من «البرهان» الذي يفيد علوماً يقينية كلية بالأمور الطبيعية ليس كما يَدَّعونه، بل غالب عادات تقبل التغير، ولها شروط وموانع وهم لا يريدون بالقضايا البرهانية الواجب قبولها إلا ما يكون لزوم المحمول منها للموضوع لزوماً ذاتياً لا يقبل التغير بحال. فإذا قالوا كل ا ب لم يريدوا أن كل ما هو في الوجود ا فهوب، ولا ا: كل ما وُجد أو سيوجد ؛ وإنما يريدون أن كل ما يُفرض ويُقدَّر في العقل، بل في نفس الأمر، مع قطع النظر عن الوُجودَينِ الذهني والخارجي، فهو ب. كما إذا قالوا : «كل إنسان حيوان»، فالطبيعة الإنسانية من حيث هي هي تستلزم الحيوانية . وهم يدعون أن الماهية قد تنفك عن الوجودين الخارجي والذهني، وهو من أغاليطهم. ومعلوم أن هذا إن أريد به الإنسان المعروف فالإنسان المعروف لا يكون إلا حيواناً». وهذا أمر واضح ليس هو مما يُطلب علمه بالبراهين . فالصفات اللازمة للموصوف التي لا تكون له حقيقة إلا بها، لا توجد بدونها . وقد بسط الكلام على فَرْقهم بين اللازمة وبين الذاتية المقومة الداخلة في الماهية ، وبين اللازمة للماهية واللازمة لوجودها»، وبين أن هذا كله باطل، إلا إذا أريد بـ «الماهية» ما يُتصور في الذهن وبـ الوجود ما يكون في الخارج. فالفرق بين مصوّرات الأذهان وموجودات الأعيان فرق صحيح وأما أن يُدعى أن في الخارج جوهرين قائمين بأنفسهما، أحدهما الإنسان المحسوس، والآخر إنسان معقول ينطبق على كلّ واحدٍ واحدٍ من أفراد الإنسان ؛ ويُدعى أن الصفات اللازمة التي لا يمكن تحقُّقُ الموصوف إلا بها منها ما هو داخل مقوّم لماهيته الموجودة في الخارج، ومنها : ما هو خارج عارض لماهيته الموجودة في الخارج، فهذا كله باطل، كما قد بسط في غير هذا الموضع .(١). والمقصود أن ما يذكرونه من الأقيسة في العلوم الإلهية والطبيعية وما يتعلق بها فلا يفيد يقيناً إلا كما يفيد قياس التمثيل، إذ هي مبنية على قضية كلية لا يقين عندهم بأنها كلية إلا كاليقين الذي عندهم بـ قياس التمثيل، ولا سبيل لهم إلى ذلك .. مثل قولهم في العلم الإلهي : الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد
This is among the errors of some mutakallimun, like their error in saying: 'Accidents cannot persist, that bodies are similar, and that they are composed of indivisible atoms which cannot be divided, nor is one side distinguished from another.' For this is an error. And the philosophers' saying that they are composed of indivisible atoms and matter and intellectual form is also an error, as has been fully expounded elsewhere in this place. Likewise the error of those who erred among the mutakallimun and claimed that God did not create anything for a cause or for wisdom, nor did He distinguish any bodies with powers and natures. And they claimed that whenever something occurs, the free agent who distinguishes one of two similars without any particularization at all brings it about. And they denied what is in God's creations and in His Law of the wisdoms for which He created and commanded. For the error of these [mutakallimun] empowered those philosophers, and they thought that what these and their likes say is the religion of the Muslims, or the statement of the Messenger and his Companions. For this reason, the debate of Ibn Sina and his like in their books was against the innovators among the people of kalām; generally Ibn Sina's debate is against the Mu'tazilites, and Ibn Rushd's against the Kullabiyya. And when they show the corruption of some of what the innovators among the people of kalām say, they think that no truth remains except what they themselves say, and that is the opposite. The matter is not so; rather, what the innovators among the people of kalām say contains error contrary to the Law and reason. And the error in what the philosophers say concerning theology, prophethood, resurrection, and laws is greater than the error of the mutakallimun. As for what they say in the natural and mathematical sciences, the correctness of the philosophers may be greater than the correctness of those who refuted them from among the people of kalām, for most of the speech of the people of kalām on these matters is without knowledge, reason, or Law. And we have not criticized what is known of natural and mathematical matters, but we have mentioned that what they claim of 'demonstration' that yields certain universal sciences of natural matters is not as they claim; rather, most of it is habits that admit change, and they have conditions and obstacles, and they intend by demonstrative propositions that must be accepted only that the copulation of the predicate to the subject is essential copulation that does not admit change at all. So if they say 'Every A is B', they do not mean that every A that exists in external reality is B, nor that every A that has existed or will exist is B; rather, they mean that every A that is hypothesized and assumed in the mind, nay in the very matter itself, disregarding mental and external existence, is B. As when they say 'Every human is an animal', the human nature in itself entails animality. And they claim that quiddity may be free from both external and mental existence, which is among their fallacies. And it is known that if by this is meant the known human, then the known human is only an animal. This is an obvious matter, not something whose knowledge is sought by demonstrations. So the necessary attributes of the described thing, which it has no reality except through them, do not exist without them. And He has fully expounded on their distinction between the necessary and the essential constitutive that enters into the quiddity, and between what is necessary for the quiddity and what is necessary for its existence, and [on] that all this is false, unless by 'quiddity' is meant what is conceived in the mind and by 'existence' what is in the external. So the distinction between mental concepts and extramental existents is a correct distinction. As for claiming that in the external there are two substances subsisting by themselves, one being the sensible human and the other an intelligible human that applies to each individual of humans; and claiming that the necessary attributes without which the described thing cannot be realized, some of them are internal constitutive for its quiddity existing in the external, and some of them are external accidental for its quiddity existing in the external—all this is false, as has been fully expounded elsewhere in this place. (1) And the point is that what they mention of syllogisms in the divine and natural sciences and what pertains to them yields no certainty except as analogical reasoning yields certainty, since they are built on a universal proposition that they have no certainty regarding its universality except the certainty they have regarding analogical reasoning, and they have no means to that... such as their saying in theology: 'The One does not issue from it except one'.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of errors made both by mutakallimun (speculative theologians) and falasifa (philosophers). He begins by listing some errors of the mutakallimun: their claim that accidents cannot persist, that bodies are similar, and that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms. He calls this an error. Then he notes that the philosophers' claim that bodies are composed of atoms, matter, and intellectual form is also an error. Next he criticizes a group among the mutakallimun who denied that God creates for a purpose or wisdom, and who claimed that God creates by sheer will without specification. This error, he says, empowered the philosophers, who then thought that the mutakallimun's views represented Islam. He points out that Ibn Sina debated the Mu'tazila and Ibn Rushd debated the Kullabiyya; when they showed the corruption of some kalām positions, the philosophers assumed that their own views were the only truth, which is false. Ibn Taymiyya then states that the errors of the philosophers in theology, prophethood, and eschatology are greater than those of the mutakallimun, though in natural sciences the philosophers may be more correct. He clarifies that he is not criticizing established natural knowledge, but rather the claim that their demonstrations yield certain universal propositions; he argues that natural matters are based on habits that can change and have conditions. The philosophers' demonstrative propositions are meant to express essential copulation independent of existence, as when they say 'Every human is an animal'—the quiddity of human entails animality. They claim quiddity can be free from existence, which is a fallacy. If by 'human' they mean the known human, then that human is necessarily an animal; this is obvious. Ibn Taymiyya then discusses the distinction between essential and necessary attributes: they claim some attributes are constitutive of the quiddity and others are necessary but extra. He rejects this distinction unless by 'quiddity' one means mental conception and by 'existence' external reality; the only valid distinction is between mental concepts and extramental individuals. Any claim of two substances (sensible and intelligible) in the external world is false. Finally, he concludes that the syllogisms of the philosophers in divine and natural sciences yield no more certainty than analogical reasoning (qiyās al-tamthīl), because they are built on universal propositions that are themselves no more certain than analogical generalizations. An example is the principle 'The One does not issue from it except one'—a claim without certainty.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what consequence did the mutakallimun's denial of divine wisdom have?
A) It proved the philosophers correct.
B) It empowered the philosophers to dismiss Islamic teachings as irrational.
C) It led to the acceptance of atomism.
D) It caused the decline of kalam.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes how the errors of one group can be exploited by another, emphasizing the need to distinguish between correct Islamic doctrine and corrupted positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another example in Islamic intellectual history where an error in one school empowered a competing school's dismissal of the tradition. Explain how Ibn Taymiyya's critique would apply.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
advanced
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Philosophical Demonstration and Quiddity
Ibn Taymiyya critiques philosophical demonstration and quiddity doctrine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the philosophers' demonstrative syllogisms yield no more certainty than analogical reasoning, and his rejection of the quiddity-existence distinction.

Core Idea
Philosophical demonstration is built on universal premises that are no more certain than analogical generalizations.

In the second part of this passage, Ibn Taymiyya turns to the philosophers' claim that their syllogisms produce certain knowledge. He deconstructs the notion of essential predication and the independence of quiddity from existence, arguing that their 'demonstrations' are in fact as uncertain as analogical reasoning (qiyās al-tamthīl). This lesson unpacks his epistemological critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' claim about demonstrative propositions
They say 'Every A is B' meaning an essential copulation independent of existence—only considering the quiddity in itself.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' is said to hold for the quiddity of human, regardless of actual existence.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that if by 'human' they mean the known human, then it is obvious that a human is an animal; this does not require demonstration. The claim that quiddity can be free from existence is a fallacy.
3
Examine the distinction between essential and necessary attributes
Philosophers distinguish internal constitutive attributes and external necessary ones. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this unless by 'quiddity' one means mental concept and by 'existence' external reality.
The only valid distinction is between mental concepts and extramental existents.
4
Understand the final conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya states that syllogisms in divine and natural sciences yield no certainty except as analogical reasoning does, because they rest on universal premises that are themselves uncertain.
Example: 'The One does not issue from it except one' is such a principle.

المتفلسفة إنها مركبة من الجواهر المنفردة والمادة والصورة العقليين أيضاً غلط، كما قد بسط هذا كله في غير هذا الموضع . وكذلك غلط من غلط من المتكلمين وادّعى أن الله لم يخلق شيئاً بسبب ولا لحكمة، ولا خص This is among the errors of some mutakallimun, like their error in saying: 'Accidents cannot persist, that bodies are similar, and that they are composed of indivisible atoms which cannot be divided, nor is one side distinguished from another.' For this is an error. And the philosophers' saying that they are composed of indivisible atoms and matter and intellectual form is also an error, as has been fully expounded elsewhere in this place. Likewise the error of those who erred among the mutakallimun and claimed that God did not create anything for a cause or for wisdom, nor did He distinguish any bodies with powers and natures. And they claimed that whenever something occurs, the free agent who distinguishes one of two similars without any particularization at all brings it about. And they denied what is in God's creations and in His Law of the wisdoms for which He created and commanded. For the error of these [mutakallimun] empowered those philosophers, and they thought that what these and their likes say is the religion of the Muslims, or the statement of the Messenger and his Companions. For this reason, the debate of Ibn Sina and his like in their books was against the innovators among the people of kalām; generally Ibn Sina's debate is against the Mu'tazilites, and Ibn Rushd's against the Kullabiyya. The matter is not so; rather, what the innovators among the people of kalām say contains error contrary to the Law and reason. And the error in what the philosophers say concerning theology, prophethood, resurrection, and laws is greater than the error of the mutakallimun. As for what they say in the natural and mathematical sciences, the correctness of the philosophers may be greater than the correctness of those who refuted them from among the people of kalām, for most of the speech of the people of kalām on these matters is without knowledge, reason, or Law. And we have not criticized what is known of natural and mathematical matters, but we have mentioned that what they claim of 'demonstration' that yields certain universal sciences of natural matters is not as they claim; rather, most of it is habits that admit change, and they have conditions and obstacles, and they intend by demonstrative propositions that must be accepted only that the copulation of the predicate to the subject is essential copulation that does not admit change at all. So if they say 'Every A is B', they do not mean that every A that exists in external reality is B, nor that every A that has existed or will exist is B; rather, they mean that every A that is hypothesized and assumed in the mind, nay in the very matter itself, disregarding mental and external existence, is B. As when they say 'Every human is an animal', the human nature in itself entails animality. And they claim that quiddity may be free from both external and mental existence, which is among their fallacies. And it is known that if by this is meant the known human, then the known human is only an animal. This is an obvious matter, not something whose knowledge is sought by demonstrations. So the necessary attributes of the described thing, which it has no reality except through them, do not exist without them. And He has fully expounded on their distinction between the necessary and the essential constitutive that enters into the quiddity, and between what is necessary for the quiddity and what is necessary for its existence, and [on] that all this is false, unless by 'quiddity' is meant what is conceived in the mind and by 'existence' what is in the external. So the distinction between mental concepts and extramental existents is a correct distinction. As for claiming that in the external there are two substances subsisting by themselves, one being the sensible human and the other an intelligible human that applies to each individual of humans; and claiming that the necessary attributes without which the described thing cannot be realized, some of them are internal constitutive for its quiddity existing in the external, and some of them are external accidental for its quiddity existing in the external—all this is false, as has been fully expounded elsewhere in this place. Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of errors made both by mutakallimun (speculative theologians) and falasifa (philosophers). He begins by listing some errors of the mutakallimun: their claim that accidents cannot persist, that bodies are similar, and that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms. He calls this an error. Then he notes that the philosophers' claim that bodies are composed of atoms, matter, and intellectual form is also an error. Next he criticizes a group among the mutakallimun who denied that God creates for a purpose or wisdom, and who claimed that God creates by sheer will without specification. This error, he says, empowered the philosophers, who then thought that the mutakallimun's views represented Islam. He points out that Ibn Sina debated the Mu'tazila and Ibn Rushd debated the Kullabiyya; when they showed the corruption of some kalām positions, the philosophers assumed that their own views were the only truth, which is false. Ibn Taymiyya then states that the errors of the philosophers in theology, prophethood, and eschatology are greater than those of the mutakallimun, though in natural sciences the philosophers may be more correct. He clarifies that he is not criticizing established natural knowledge, but rather the claim that their demonstrations yield certain universal propositions; he argues that natural matters are based on habits that can change and have conditions. The philosophers' demonstrative propositions are meant to express essential copulation independent of existence, as when they say 'Every human is an animal'—the quiddity of human entails animality. They claim quiddity can be free from existence, which is a fallacy. If by 'human' they mean the known human, then that human is necessarily an animal; this is obvious. Ibn Taymiyya then discusses the distinction between essential and necessary attributes: they claim some attributes are constitutive of the quiddity and others are necessary but extra. He rejects this distinction unless by 'quiddity' one means mental conception and by 'existence' external reality; the only valid distinction is between mental concepts and extramental individuals. Any claim of two substances (sensible and intelligible) in the external world is false. Finally, he concludes that the syllogisms of the philosophers in divine and natural sciences yield no more certainty than analogical reasoning (qiyās al-tamthīl), because they are built on universal propositions that are themselves no more certain than analogical generalizations. An example is the principle 'The One does not issue from it except one'—a claim without certainty.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why do the philosophers' syllogisms in divine and natural sciences not yield certain knowledge?
A) Because they use the wrong logical form.
B) Because they are based on universal premises that are themselves as uncertain as analogical reasoning.
C) Because they rely on empirical observations that are fallible.
D) Because they contradict revelation.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that philosophical syllogisms are epistemologically equivalent to analogical reasoning, undermining the philosophers' claim to superior certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the philosophical principle 'The One does not issue from it except one' and evaluate its certainty according to Ibn Taymiyya's criteria. Then construct an analogical argument for a theological claim and compare their epistemic status.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Universal Knowledge and Divine Signs
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' claim that universal conceptions cannot indicate particular things, contrasting it with divine signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim that universals cannot indicate particulars, and contrast it with the Qur'anic concept of āyāt as direct indicators of specific realities.

Core Idea
Universals give generalities; divine signs give specificity – and that changes how we know God.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a core assumption of Aristotelian logic: that universal premises cannot yield knowledge of particular individuals. He not only acknowledges this limitation but turns it into a critique, arguing that the Qur'anic concept of 'āyāt' (signs) provides direct knowledge of specific realities, especially the Creator. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for epistemology in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
What do logicians say about universal concepts and particular knowledge? They claim that universal premises do not indicate specific individuals. Ibn Taymiyya quotes them as admitting this.
The logicians' position is that a universal conception can be shared by many and does not point to a unique individual.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's admission
Ibn Taymiyya concedes their point: the conclusion cannot be more comprehensive than the premises, and universal premises only give general knowledge.
He uses their own logic against them to show the limitation of syllogistic reasoning for particulars.
3
Contrast with divine signs
Qur'anic 'āyāt' (signs) indicate specific realities directly, like the sun as a sign of daytime. They are not universal but particular.
The sun is a specific created entity that directly points to the Creator.
4
Examine the broader concept of dalīl
Evidence (dalīl) is more general than qiyas (analogy). It can be specific-to-specific, e.g., using a star to locate the Kaaba.
This expands the notion of evidence beyond Aristotelian syllogism.
5
Understand the theological conclusion
Everything other than God is dependent on Him; thus contemplating any created thing directly necessitates the existence of the Creator Himself, not just a common attribute.
This is an argument from contingency framed as direct indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أمر مطلق كلي لا يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. وإنما يُعلم عينه بعلم آخر يجعله الله في القُلوب. وهم معترفون بهذا لأن النتيجة لا تكون أبلغ من المقدمات، والمقدمات فيها قضية كلية لا بد من ذلك والكلي لا يدل على معيَّن . دلالة الآيات وهذا بخلاف ما يذكره الله في كتابه من «الآيات»، كقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَاخْتِلَافِ الَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ - إلى قوله - لِقَوْمٍ يَعْقِلُونَ ﴾، وقوله : إن في ذلك لآيات لقوم يعقلون، لقوم يتفكرون، وغير ذلك. فإنه يدل على المعيَّن، كالشمس التي هي آية النهار . وقال تعالى : ﴿وَجَعَلْنَا الَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ ءَايَنَيْنِ فَمَحَوْنَا ءَايَةَ الَّيْلِ وَجَعَلْنَا ءَايَةَ النَّهَارِ مُبْصِرَةً ﴾ . والدليل أعم من القياس. فإن الدليل قد يكون بمعين على معين، كما يُستدل بالنجم وغيره من الكواكب على الكعبة . فالآيات تدل على نفس الخالق سبحانه لا على قدر مشترك بينه وبين غيره. فإن كل ما سواه مفتقر إليه نفسه، فيلزم من وجوده وجود عين الخالق نفسه.
About an absolute universal matter, the conception of which does not prevent participation in it. Its specific individual is only known by another knowledge that Allah places in hearts. And they admit this because the conclusion cannot be more comprehensive than the premises, and the premises contain a universal proposition necessarily, and the universal does not indicate a specific individual. The indication of the verses: And this is contrary to what Allah mentions in His Book of 'verses', such as His saying: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of night and day... for a people who use reason.' And His saying: 'Indeed in that are signs for a people who reflect, a people who ponder.' For it indicates the specific, like the sun which is the sign of daytime. And He said: 'And We made the night and day two signs, and We erased the sign of night and made the sign of daytime visible.' And 'evidence' is more general than 'analogy/qiyas'. For evidence may be from a specific to a specific, as one uses the star and other celestial bodies as evidence for the Kaaba. So the 'signs' indicate the Creator Himself, not a commonality between Him and other than Him. For everything other than Him is dependent on Him itself, so its existence necessitates the existence of the very Creator.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that universal knowledge cannot yield knowledge of particulars. Ibn Taymiyya first notes that a universal conception does not prevent multiple individuals from sharing it; the specific individual is known only by another knowledge from Allah. He then points out that the logicians themselves admit this because a conclusion cannot surpass the premises, and universal premises do not indicate particular existents. He contrasts this with the Qur'anic 'ayat' (signs), which directly point to specific realities, like the sun as a sign of daytime. These divine signs indicate the Creator Himself, not a generic concept. Ibn Taymiyya then distinguishes 'dalil' (evidence) from 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning), showing that evidence can be from one specific to another, like using a star to locate the Kaaba. The key point is that the universe's dependence on the Creator means that contemplating any created thing directly necessitates the existence of the Creator, not just a common attribute.

كلي
آيات
دليل

D) They only work for celestial bodies.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that while universal premises are limited to generalities, divine signs provide direct knowledge of specific realities, and this has profound implications for how we know God.

Two Types of al-Nazar: Talabi and Istidlali
Author distinguishes between nazar talabi and nazar istidlali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nazar talabi and nazar istidlali and explain why they are not contradictory.

Core Idea
Nazar that seeks knowledge cannot coexist with it; nazar that applies a proof can.

Ibn Taymiyya resolves a longstanding confusion in Islamic epistemology: how can nazar (contemplation) be both opposed to knowledge and productive of it? His answer is a radical distinction between two types of intellectual acts. This passage cuts through the ambiguity by showing that the conflict arises from conflating two different mental processes. Let us examine his precise analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of nazar
Find the two names Ibn Taymiyya gives for the types of contemplation. Note that one is 'nazar talabi' (inquiry into the proposition) and the other is 'nazar istidlali' (contemplation of the proof).
These terms are not standard in earlier mantiq; Ibn Taymiyya coins them for this distinction.
2
Analyze the wine example
Examine the syllogism: 'wine is forbidden, every intoxicant is wine, therefore every intoxicant is forbidden.' This is an example of nazar istidlali: the premises are known, and the conclusion follows necessarily. Confirm that this is a valid categorical syllogism (first figure, Barbara).
This example shows how nazar istidlali is a transition from principles to conclusions.
3
Understand the relationship to knowledge
For nazar talabi, Ibn Taymiyya says it opposes knowledge because if you already knew the judgment, seeking its proof would be redundant (tahsil al-hasil). For nazar istidlali, it produces knowledge without contradicting it. Notice the asymmetry.
The opposition is logical: one cannot simultaneously seek and have knowledge of the same thing.
4
Trace the critique of logicians
Ibn Taymiyya points out that many scholars claim nazar both opposes and entails knowledge—a clear contradiction. He resolves it by showing the two types are different. Identify the target of his critique and his logical argument against equivocation.
The critique is not against logic itself but against sloppy usage of the term 'nazar'.
5
Apply the analogy with the eye
Ibn Taymiyya compares nazar talabi to straining the eye (tahdeeq) to find an object, and nazar istidlali to actually seeing the object. Reflect on how this analogy clarifies the difference between seeking and having.
Just as tahdeeq does not guarantee sight, nazar talabi does not guarantee knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالنَّظَرُ نَوْعَانِ: أَحَدُهُمَا: النَّظَرُ فِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ الَّتِي هِيَ الْقَضِيَّةُ الْمَطْلُوبُ حُكْمُهَا لِيُطْلَبَ دَلِيلُهَا، الَّذِي هُوَ الْحَدُّ الْأَوْسَطُ مَثَلًا. وَهَذَا هُوَ النَّظَرُ الَّذِي لَا يُجَامِعُ الْعِلْمَ، بَلْ يُضَادُّهُ، لِأَنَّ هَذَا النَّاظِرَ طَالِبٌ لِلْعِلْمِ بِهَا، وَلَوْ كَانَ عَالِمًا بِهَا لَمْ يَطْلُبِ الْعِلْمَ، لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ تَحْصِيلُ الْحَاصِلِ.

وَالثَّانِي: النَّظَرُ فِي الدَّلِيلِ، وَهُوَ الْعِلْمُ بِالدَّلِيلِ الْمُسْتَلْزِمِ لِلْعِلْمِ بِالْمَدْلُولِ عَلَيْهِ. وَهُوَ تَصَوُّرُ الْحَدِّ الْأَوْسَطِ الْمُسْتَلْزِمِ لِثُبُوتِ الْأَكْبَرِ لِلْأَصْغَرِ، مِثْلُ مَنْ يَعْلَمُ أَنَّ الْخَمْرَ حَرَامٌ، وَأَنَّ كُلَّ مُسْكِرٍ خَمْرٌ فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَعْلَمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ مُسْكِرٍ حَرَامٌ. وَهَذَا النَّظَرُ هُوَ تَرْتِيبُ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ فِي النَّفْسِ، وَهَذَا النَّظَرُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُوجِبُ الْعِلْمَ، وَلَا يُنَافِي الْعِلْمَ.

وَلِلنَّاسِ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ اضْطِرَابٌ عَظِيمٌ - هَلِ النَّظَرُ مُفِيدٌ لِلْعِلْمِ أَوْ غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ، وَهَلِ النَّظَرُ هُوَ ضِدُّ الْعِلْمِ أَمْ لَا؟ وَأَمْثَالُ ذَلِكَ. وَكَثِيرٌ مِنَ النُّظَّارِ يَقُولُ فِي مُصَنَّفِهِ «إِنَّ النَّظَرَ يُضَادُّ الْعِلْمَ»، وَيَقُولُ أَيْضًا: «إِنَّهُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِلْعِلْمِ». وَهَذَا تَنَاقُضٌ بَيِّنٌ، فَإِنَّ مَلْزُومَ الشَّيْءِ لَا يَكُونُ مُضَادًّا لَهُ. لَكِنِ النَّظَرُ الَّذِي يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْعِلْمَ غَيْرُ النَّظَرِ الَّذِي يُضَادُّهُ. فَذَاكَ هُوَ «النَّظَرُ الِاسْتِدْلَالِيُّ»، وَهَذَا هُوَ النَّظَرُ الطَّلَبِيُّ. ذَاكَ نَظَرٌ فِي الدَّلِيلِ، فَإِذَا تَصَوَّرَهُ وَتَصَوَّرَ اسْتِلْزَامَهُ لِلْحُكْمِ عَلِمَ الْحُكْمَ، وَالنَّظَرُ الطَّلَبِيُّ نَظَرٌ فِي الْمَطْلُوبِ حُكْمُهُ، هَلْ يَظْفَرُ بِدَلِيلٍ يَدُلُّهُ عَلَى حُكْمِهِ أَوْ لَا يَظْفَرُ، كَطَالِبِ الضَّالَّةِ، وَالْمَقْصُودُ قَدْ يَجِدُهُ، وَقَدْ لَا يَجِدُهُ، وَقَدْ يُعْرِضُ عَنْهُمَا. فَإِنَّ الْأَوَّلَ انْتِقَالٌ مِنَ الْمُبَادِئِ إِلَى الْمَطَالِبِ، وَالْآخَرَ انْتِقَالٌ مِنَ الْمَطَالِبِ إِلَى الْمُبَادِئِ.

وَتَحْقِيقُ الْأَمْرِ أَنَّ النَّظَرَ نَوْعَانِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ نَظَرِ الْعَيْنِ، وَهُوَ نَوْعَانِ: أَحَدُهُمَا: التَّحْدِيقُ لِطَلَبِ الرُّؤْيَةِ، وَهُوَ بِمَنْزِلَةِ تَحْدِيقِ الْقَلْبِ فِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ لِطَلَبِ حُكْمِهَا. وَهَذَا قَدْ يَحْصُلُ الْعِلْمُ، وَقَدْ لَا يَحْصُلُ. وَلَا يَكُونُ طَالِبُ الْعِلْمِ حِينَ الطَّلَبِ عَالِمًا بِهِ - تَصْدِيقًا كَانَ أَوْ تَصَوُّرًا عَلَى رَأْيٍ مَنْ جَعَلَ التَّصَوُّرَ الْمَطْلُوبَ خَارِجًا عَنِ التَّصْدِيقِ. وَالثَّانِي: النَّظَرُ فِي الدَّلِيلِ، وَهُوَ الْمُفِيدُ لِلْعِلْمِ.
And al-nazar (contemplation) is of two types: one is contemplation of the issue, which is the proposition whose judgment is sought, in order to seek its proof, which is for example the middle term. And this is the contemplation that does not accompany knowledge, rather it opposes it, because this contemplator is a seeker of knowledge of it; if he already knew it, he would not seek knowledge, for that would be seeking what is already attained.

The second: contemplation of the proof, and it is knowledge of the proof that entails knowledge of what is proven by it. It is the conception of the middle term that necessitates the affirmation of the major for the minor, like one who knows that wine is forbidden, and that every intoxicant is wine, then it follows that he knows that every intoxicant is forbidden. And this contemplation is the arrangement of the two premises in the soul, and this contemplation is what necessitates knowledge, and does not contradict knowledge.

People are in great confusion regarding this topic: whether contemplation is productive of knowledge or not, and whether contemplation is the opposite of knowledge or not, and the like. Many of the speculative thinkers say in their books that 'contemplation opposes knowledge', and also say that 'it entails knowledge'. And this is a clear contradiction, for the prerequisite of a thing cannot be its opposite. But the contemplation that entails knowledge is not the same as the contemplation that opposes it. That one is 'contemplation of inference' (al-nazar al-istidlali), and this one is 'contemplation of inquiry' (al-nazar al-talabi). The former is contemplation of the proof: when one conceives of it and conceives of its entailment of the judgment, he knows the judgment. The contemplative inquiry is contemplation of what is sought to be judged, whether he will find a proof that indicates its judgment or not, like the seeker of a lost thing; the object may be found, or may not be found, or he may turn away from both. For the first is a transition from principles to conclusions, and the second is a transition from conclusions to principles.

And the verification of the matter is that al-nazar is of two types analogous to the vision of the eye, and it is of two types: one is straining to see (tahdeeq) in order to seek sight, which is like the straining of the heart towards the issue to seek its judgment. And this may obtain knowledge, or may not. And the seeker of knowledge at the time of seeking is not knowledgeable of it – whether it is assent (tasdiq) or conception (tasawwur) according to the opinion of one who holds that the conception sought is outside of assent. And the second: contemplation of the proof, which is productive of knowledge.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the concept of al-nazar (contemplation/inference) as used by logicians and theologians. He distinguishes two fundamentally different types of nazar. The first, nazar talabi, is inquiry into a proposition to find its proof. This type of contemplation is incompatible with knowledge of that proposition because if one already knew it, seeking would be pointless (تحصيل الحاصل). Hence, it opposes knowledge. The second, nazar istidlali, is contemplation of the proof itself, where one already knows the premises (e.g., 'wine is forbidden' and 'every intoxicant is wine') and arranges them mentally to deduce the conclusion. This type produces knowledge and does not contradict it. Ibn Taymiyya then critiques the inconsistency of scholars who claim that nazar both opposes and entails knowledge, clarifying that these are two different processes. He analogizes nazar talabi to straining the eye to see something (tahdeeq), which may or may not succeed, while nazar istidlali is the actual seeing after the object is in view. This distinction resolves the confusion in the tradition about whether nazar is opposed to knowledge or productive of it. The passage underscores the importance of correctly identifying whether one is seeking a proof or applying a known proof.

Key Terms
نظر استدلالي
نظر طلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, which type of contemplation is incompatible with knowledge?
A) Nazar istidlali
B) Nazar talabi
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's distinction between inquiry into the proposition (talabi) and contemplation of the proof (istidlali) resolves the apparent contradiction that nazar both opposes and produces knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Two Types of Consideration: Seeking vs. Inferring
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes two types of nazar and clarifies their relation to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nazar talabi and nazar istidlali, identify which type contradicts knowledge and why, and apply the distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in epistemological claims about reasoning.

Core Idea
Nazar that seeks cannot coexist with knowledge; nazar that sees the proof produces it.

Many logicians claim that nazar (consideration) both opposes knowledge and entails it—a clear contradiction. Ibn Taymiyya resolves this by distinguishing two fundamentally different types of nazar. In this lesson, you will learn his critical refinement and see how it clarifies the role of reasoning in acquiring knowledge, especially within Islamic legal and theological contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of nazar
Locate in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya defines the first type (nazar talabi) as consideration of the issue to seek its ruling, and the second type (nazar istidlali) as consideration of the proof. Note the phrases 'النظر الطلبي' and 'النظر الاستدلالي'.
The first type is about seeking what is unknown; the second is about using known premises.
2
Understand the contradiction
Find where Ibn Taymiyya mentions that many thinkers say 'النظر يضاد العلم' (consideration opposes knowledge) and also 'مستلزم للعلم' (it entails knowledge). He calls this a clear contradiction because the necessary condition of a thing cannot be its opposite.
The contradiction arises from using the same term 'nazar' for two different activities.
3
Analyze the resolution
Ibn Taymiyya resolves the contradiction by showing that nazar talabi (seeking) is incompatible with already knowing—it seeks what is not known—while nazar istidlali (arranging premises) necessarily produces knowledge. Thus, they are different in kind.
The key: one type is prior to knowledge, the other is productive of knowledge.
4
Apply the eyesight analogy
Map the analogy: tahdiq (squinting to seek sight) corresponds to nazar talabi—it may or may not yield vision; ru'ya (actual seeing) corresponds to nazar istidlali—it yields knowledge necessarily. The heart's 'squinting' at an issue is seeking; 'seeing' the proof is inferring.
The analogy clarifies that the same faculty (sight/consideration) operates in two distinct modes.
5
Extend to Islamic sources
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly connects nazar istidlali to looking into the Qur'an and Hadith to derive rulings. Identify that this is the inferential mode—when one already knows the proof. Nazar talabi is when one looks into the issue to find the proof.
This shows the practical application in ijtihad.
