Ibn Taymiyya's Third Refutation: Conception Does Not Entail Concomitant
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that conceiving a quiddity entails conceiving its necessary concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the equivocation in the claim that conceiving a quiddity entails conceiving its necessary concomitant, and apply this critique to evaluate similar arguments in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Conception is not monolithic: partial conception does not require grasping all necessary properties.

Ibn Taymiyya takes on a key premise of the logicians: that if you conceive a quiddity, you must conceive its necessary concomitants. He shows this premise rests on an ambiguous phrase—'conceiving the quiddity' can mean complete or incomplete conception. Only the complete form entails the concomitant, but the logicians use this to claim that every conception does. This lesson unpacks his refutation and applies it to the science of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the statement that the logicians make: 'If we conceive the quiddity, then we must conceive from it that it, as it is itself, necessitates that necessary concomitant.'
2
Spot the ambiguous term
Notice that 'conceiving the quiddity' (al-mahiyya) is described as mujmal (ambiguous). It could mean complete conception (including the concomitant) or incomplete conception (without the concomitant).
This ambiguity is key to the refutation.
3
Apply the distinction
Show that only if 'conceiving the quiddity' means complete conception does it entail the concomitant. Incomplete conception does not. The logicians' claim conflates the two.
Ibn Taymiyya gives the example of people conceiving quiddities partially without grasping their necessary properties.
4
Check the opponent's proof
The opponent argues: 'Otherwise we would not conceive the quiddity as it is.' Ibn Taymiyya says: This only holds if we assume complete conception. If the opponent means no conception at all without the concomitant, that is denied.
He grants that complete conception includes the concomitant but insists that not every conception is complete.
5
Draw the conclusion
The claim that every intellection of a quiddity obligates conceiving the concomitant is false. People often conceive quiddities partially, so the logicians' argument is invalid.
This refutation uses denial (man') as sufficient, since the claim is not self-evident and lacks proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال : قولكم فإذا عقلنا الماهية وجب أن نعقل منها أنها لما هي هي هي تقتضي ما لا يستلزم ذلك اللازم. يقال: هذا ممنوع وذلك أن قول القائل عقلنا الماهية» و «تصورنا تصور الملزوم خلاف الماهية ونحو ذلك من العبارات لفظ مجمل. فإنه قد يعني به التصور التام والعقل التام الذي يتضمن تصور الملزوم ؛ وقد يعني به أنا عقلناها وتصورنا نوعاً من العقل والتصور وإن لم يكن تاماً، ولا شك أن مثل هذا لا يستلزم تصور الملزوم. فدعواك أن كل عقل للماهية يوجب أن نعقل منها أنها لما هي [هي] تقتضي ذلك اللازم ممنوع، وهو الواقع فإن الناس لا يزالون يعقلون بعض الماهيات نوعاً من العقل، فلا يعقلون أنها لما [هي] تقتضي اللازم وبالجملة فنحن في هذا المقام يكفينا المنع، فإن الدعوى ليست بديهية، [٤٠٩] ولم يقم عليها دليلاً إلا قوله «وإلا لما عقلنا الماهية لما [ هي]. فإن أراد بهذا اللازم المنفي العقل التام الذي هو التصور التام فنحن نسلّم ذلك ونقول : ليس كل من تصور ماهية ما وعَقَلها نوعاً من العقل والتصور، يكون قد عقلها وتصورها عقلاً تاماً وتصوراً تاماً. وإن أراد بهذا اللازم المنفي أنا لا يكون حصل لنا شيء من التصور أو العقل فهذا ممنوع .
It is said: Your statement: 'If we conceive the quiddity, then we must conceive from it that it, as it is itself, necessitates what does not entail that necessary concomitant.' It is said: This is denied. That is because the statement of the speaker 'we conceived the quiddity' and 'we conceived the conception of the substrate contrary to the quiddity' and similar expressions are ambiguous expressions. For by it may be meant the complete conception and the complete intellection that includes the conception of the substrate; and by it may be meant that we conceived it and conceived a type of intellection and conception even if it is not complete, and there is no doubt that such does not necessitate the conception of the substrate. So your claim that every intellection of the quiddity obliges that we conceive from it that it, as it is itself, necessitates that necessary concomitant is denied, and it is the actual case, for people continue to conceive some quiddities with a type of intellection, and they do not conceive that they, as they are themselves, necessitate the concomitant. In summary, in this context, denial suffices us, for the claim is not self-evident, and no evidence has been established for it except his statement: 'Otherwise we would not conceive the quiddity as it is.' If by this negated necessary concomitant he means the complete intellection that is the complete conception, then we concede that and say: Not everyone who conceives a certain quiddity and intellected it with a type of intellection and conception has intellected it and conceived it with a complete intellection and complete conception. And if by this negated necessary concomitant he means that we have not obtained any conception or intellection at all, then this is denied.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that conceiving a quiddity necessarily entails conceiving its necessary concomitant. He begins by presenting the opponents' statement as an objection: 'If we conceive the quiddity, we must conceive that it, as itself, necessitates a certain concomitant.' Then he responds by pointing out that the phrase 'we conceived the quiddity' is ambiguous (mujmal). It could mean a complete conception that includes the necessary concomitant, or an incomplete conception that does not. He argues that only the complete conception would entail the concomitant, but the incomplete does not. He denies that every intellection of a quiddity obliges conceiving the concomitant, noting that people often conceive quiddities partially without grasping their necessary properties. He then addresses the opponents' proof: 'Otherwise we would not conceive the quiddity as it is.' He grants that if they mean complete conception, then it is true that the concomitant is included, but not everyone has complete conception. If they mean that no conception occurs without the concomitant, that is denied. The argument uses an example from everyday experience: a person may conceive of 'animal' without conceiving that it is a 'substance' (a necessary concomitant). This aligns with Islamic legal reasoning where one may know the ruling for a specific case without knowing all its underlying causes.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur): Conception; a mental representation of a thing without judgment; here, distinguished between complete (including necessary concomitants) and incomplete. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses tasawwur broadly, but his critique here targets the logicians' notion of a 'simple conception' free from concomitants.]
ماهية (mahiyya): Quiddity or essence of a thing; what it is in itself. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage across Islamic logic.]
لازم (lazim): Necessary concomitant; a property that necessarily follows from the essence of a thing. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses it in the context of logical necessity; later logicians may differentiate between essential and accidental concomitants.]
مجمل (mujmal): Ambiguous or equivocal; a term with multiple meanings, requiring clarification. [Note: Common in usul al-fiqh; stable meaning.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's third argument, why is the logicians' claim that 'conceiving a quiddity entails conceiving its necessary concomitant' invalid?
A) Because the concomitant is not always necessary.
B) Because the phrase 'conceiving the quiddity' is ambiguous between complete and incomplete conception.
C) Because no one can ever conceive a quiddity without its concomitant.
D) Because quiddities have no necessary concomitants.

Key Takeaway
Conceptions are not all-or-nothing; a partial conception of a quiddity does not require grasping all its necessary concomitants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the kalam argument: 'Whoever conceives the world as created must conceive its Creator.' Use Ibn Taymiyya's critique to identify possible ambiguity in 'conceives as created' and evaluate whether the conclusion necessarily follows.
?
Identify a similar equivocation in a contemporary debate (e.g., 'if you accept Islam, you must accept all rulings'). Apply Ibn Taymiyya's method of distinguishing complete vs. incomplete acceptance.

Ibn Taymiyya's Fourth Refutation: The Impossibility of Simple Conception
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' view makes simple conception impossible and contradicts their own division of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's fourth refutation and identify the self-contradiction he exposes in the logicians' account of conception.

Core Idea
If every conception requires its concomitant, no conception can be simple — contradicting the logicians' own definition.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' requirement for every conception to involve its necessary concomitant leads to an impossible regress and contradicts their own division of knowledge into conception and assent. This lesson unpacks his critique and shows how it illuminates the nature of simple, un-judgmental mental representations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the view that conceiving a quiddity requires conceiving its necessary concomitant. This is the premise he will show leads to absurdity.
The claim is implicitly attributed to the logicians; Ibn Taymiyya phrases it as 'ما ذكره يستلزم...'
2
Extract the logical consequence
Ibn Taymiyya argues: if conceiving X requires conceiving its necessary concomitant Y, then one always conceives at least two things (X and Y), never a single simple concept. This makes simple conception impossible.
The consequence is that no 'مفردات بسائط' (simple singulars) can be conceived alone.
3
Spot the self-contradiction
The logicians themselves divide knowledge into conception (تصور) and assent (تصديق), where conception is defined as a simple representation without judgment. But if conception always involves a concomitant, it would include a relation (a kind of judgment), contradicting their own definition.
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says: 'وهذا مناقض لما زعموه...'
4
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's own position
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that even though he does not consider pure simple conception without any judgment to be knowledge, he still admits that a thing can be conceived partially without conceiving all its necessary concomitants. This preserves the possibility of simple conception in a weaker sense.
His position: 'لا يمتنع أن يتصور الشيء من بعض الوجوه وإن لم يتصور أنه ملزوم للوازم له'
5
Evaluate the force of the refutation
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the logicians' view is false by consensus of rational people. The contradiction shows that their own theory undermines the very possibility of simple concepts, which are foundational to all knowledge.
He says: 'فقد ثبت أن قوله باطل باتفاق العقلاء'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال : ما ذكره يستلزم أن لا يُعقل شيء من المفردات البسائط، ولا يُعقل شيء قوله يناقض كون التصور وحده، بل كل من عقل شيئاً فلا بد أن يعقل لازمه القريب. فيكون قد عَقَل اثنين وتَصَوَّرَ اثنين، لم يعقل واحداً ولم يتصور واحداً. وهذا مناقض لما زعموه من أن العلم ينقسم / ٤١٠ يسمى إلى تصور وتصديق، وأن التصور هو التصور الساذج الذي لا يكون معه حكم بشيء أصلاً لا بنفي ولا إثبات ونحن وإن كنا قلنا أن مثل هذا ليس بعلم، وأنه لا علماً إلا ما تضمن العلم بنفي أو إثبات، فلا يمتنع أن يتصور الشيء من بعض الوجوه وإن لم يتصور أنه ملزوم للوازم له يلزمه بنفسها . فقد ثبت أن قوله باطل باتفاق العقلاء .
It is said: What he mentioned necessitates that nothing of simple singulars be intellected, and his statement contradicts its being conception alone. Rather, whoever intellets something must intellect its proximate necessary concomitant. Thus he would have intellected two and conceived two; he did not intellect one nor conceive one. This contradicts their claim that knowledge is divided into conception and assent, and that conception is the simple conception with which there is no judgment at all, neither negation nor affirmation. And we, even if we have said that such is not knowledge, and that there is no knowledge except what includes knowledge by negation or affirmation, it is not impossible that a thing be conceived in some aspects even if it is not conceived as being a substrate for necessary properties that necessarily follow it. So it has been established that his statement is false by consensus of the rational people.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's fourth argument refuting the logicians' claim. He begins by stating the logical consequence of their view: if conceiving any quiddity requires conceiving its necessary concomitant, then no simple singular can be conceived by itself. Instead, whenever you try to conceive one thing, you necessarily conceive two (the thing and its concomitant). This makes it impossible to have a 'simple conception' (tasawwur sadhij). He notes that this contradicts the logicians' own division of knowledge into conception and assent, where conception is defined as mere representation without judgment. Ibn Taymiyya then clarifies his own position: even though he himself does not consider such simple conception to be true knowledge (since knowledge involves judgment), it is still possible to conceive something partially without conceiving all its necessary concomitants. He concludes that the logicians' argument is false by universal agreement. An example from Islamic theology: one can conceive of 'the Creator' without necessarily conceiving that He is 'all-knowing' as a necessary concomitant in one's mind, though in reality it is a necessary attribute. The point is that mental conception can be incomplete.

Key Terms
مفردات بسائط
تصور ساذج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Ibn Taymiyya point out in the fourth argument?
A) The logicians claim that conception requires judgment, but also say it is simple.
B) The logicians say conception is simple, but their own view forces every conception to involve a necessary concomitant, making it compound.
C) The logicians deny the possibility of simple conception, but they rely on it.
D) The logicians say that simple conception is impossible, yet they define it.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' requirement that every conception include its necessary concomitant leads to an infinite regress and contradicts their own definition of simple conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique to the argument: 'To conceive the meaning of 'salat' (prayer), one must conceive its components (rukn) and conditions.' Does this necessarily make 'salat' a compound concept? Explain using Ibn Taymiyya's distinction.
?
Identify a case from fiqh where a simple conception (e.g., 'riba') is used without its full necessary concomitants (e.g., 'interest'). How does this match Ibn Taymiyya's position that partial conception is possible?

The Ninth Method: Conception Does Not Guarantee Assent
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the claim that complete conception of cause and effect necessarily yields assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the distinction between complete and incomplete conception and explain why mere intellection of quiddity does not guarantee assent to causal relations.

Core Idea
Conceiving a quiddity alone does not reveal its necessary concomitants.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya dismantles the logicians' claim that complete conception of cause and effect necessitates assent. He introduces a crucial distinction between mere intellection of quiddity and comprehensive conception that includes the consequent. This lesson will walk you through his argument and highlight the logical precision required in causal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Locate the statement attributed to the logicians: that complete conception of cause and effect (without a middle term) necessarily results in assent to their relation.
This is the 'ninth method' being critiqued.
2
Distinguish conception types
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya separates 'complete conception' (al-tasawwur al-tāmm) from 'mere intellection of quiddity' (ʿaql al-māhiyya). The opponent's argument relies on the former but only establishes the latter.
Complete conception includes awareness of necessary concomitants; mere intellection does not.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Ibn Taymiyya shows that if mere quiddity intellection sufficed, then conceiving any entailed (malzūm) would force conceiving all its necessary concomitants (lawāzim), which is false since many are unknown.
This exposes the opponent's overgeneralization.
4
Grasp Ibn Taymiyya's alternative
He concedes that complete conception (including the concomitant) yields knowledge, but insists this is not what the opponent originally argued. Thus, the opponent's claim is either trivial or false.
The shift to 'complete conception' does not save the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق التاسع
الأمور موجود
٤١٤ / للملزوم يستلزم أنه قال : إن ذات العلة والمعلول - يعني اللازم والملزوم - بغير وسط عند التصور التام تصورهما يجب حصول التصديق بانتساب أحدهما إلى الآخر». فيقال: هذا أول الدعوى، فإنه إن أراد بالتصور التّامَّ» الذي يحصل معه تصور الملزوم فهذا حق. ولكن العلم باللازم لا ينفعه، فإنه ادعى أن مطلق عقل الماهية يوجب ذلك. وأيضاً فإنا نلزمه مثل ذلك في جميع الملزومات أنها متى تُصُوِّرت تُصُور اللازم، وهو باطل. وأيضاً، فإنا نحن نلتزم مثل ذلك في التصور التام، فيقول في جميع اللوازم متى تصور الملزوم تصوراً تاماً يحصل به تصور الملزوم حَصَل معه العلم بأن هذا لازم وإن لم يحصل هذا التصور لم يجب العلم باللازم في شيء من الأمور، كما سيأتي ذكر ذلك .
وقوله : متى تصورنا حقيقة العلة وحقيقة المعلول امتنع عند ذلك أن لا نصدق بثبوت أحدهما للآخر» إن أراد التصور التام فهذا مشترك بين جميع اللوازم. وإن أراد غيره فالحكم ممنوع . وهو إنما ادعى أن مطلق عقل الماهية يوجب ذلك. وقد بينا أن عقلها أو تصورها ينقسم إلى تام و غير تام ، وأن ما ذكره إنما يلزم في التام) .
The ninth method: Matters exist? [fragment]. [The opponent] said: 'The essence of the cause and the effect – meaning the necessary concomitant and the entailed – without a middle term, when their conception is complete, the assent to the attribution of one to the other must occur.' So it is said: This is the very claim. If he means by 'complete conception' that which includes conception of the entailed, then this is correct. But knowledge of the necessary concomitant does not benefit him, for he claimed that mere intellection of the quiddity necessitates that. Also, we oblige him likewise in all entailed things: whenever they are conceived, the necessary concomitant is conceived, which is false. Also, we ourselves accept that in complete conception: we say regarding all necessary concomitants, whenever the entailed is conceived completely – by which conception of the entailed is obtained – then knowledge results that this is a necessary concomitant; but if this conception does not occur, knowledge of the necessary concomitant is not required in any matter, as will be mentioned. And his statement: 'When we conceive the reality of the cause and the reality of the effect, it is impossible then that we do not assent to the attribution of one to the other' – if he means complete conception, then this is shared by all necessary concomitants. But if he means something else, then the judgment is denied. And he claimed that mere intellection of the quiddity necessitates that. And we have shown that its intellection or conception is divided into complete and incomplete, and that what he mentioned only follows in the complete [conception].
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This is a critical passage where Ibn Taymiyya refutes the ninth method of the logicians. The opponent claims that when one has a complete conception (tasawwur tāmm) of the cause and effect (or entailed and necessary concomitant) without a middle term, then assent (tasdiq) that one relates to the other necessarily follows. Ibn Taymiyya responds that this is just a reassertion of the claim. He distinguishes: if 'complete conception' includes the conception of the entailed along with the necessary concomitant, then it is true but trivial. The opponent, however, asserts that mere intellection of the quiddity (māhiyya) suffices, which Ibn Taymiyya denies. He exposes a consequence: if the opponent's logic holds for all entailed things, then whenever one conceives any entailed entity, one would conceive all its necessary concomitants, which is absurd (since many concomitants are unknown). Ibn Taymiyya then offers his own view: only with a complete conception (which includes the concomitant) does one know the necessity; otherwise, knowledge is not necessary. He concludes that the opponent's claim is only valid for complete conception, which is not what he originally argued. Thus, the opponent's argument fails.

Key Terms
التصور التام
عقل الماهية
اللازم والملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya identify as the key flaw in the logician's claim that conceiving the cause and effect necessarily yields assent to their relation?
A) The logician confuses complete and incomplete conception.
B) The logician denies the possibility of incomplete conception.
C) The logician assumes all causes are necessary.
D) The logician uses an invalid syllogistic form.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's key insight is that mere intellection of a quiddity does not automatically reveal its necessary concomitants; only a complete conception (which includes the consequent) yields such knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain in your own words why Ibn Taymiyya considers the logicians' claim to be 'the very claim' (هذا أول الدعوى). What makes it question-begging?
?
Construct an analogous case from fiqh where a complete conception of the 'illah (effective cause) might be required to know the ruling, but mere conception of the 'illah does not yield the ruling. Provide an example (e.g., the 'illah of intoxication for forbidding wine and nabidh).

Essential Predication Does Not Require Detailed Conception
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that essential predication requires detailed conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the logicians' definition of 'essential' is self-undermining, using their own example of 'man' as 'rational animal'.

Core Idea
The logicians' own definition of 'essential' fails because they allow conceiving 'man' without detailing what 'animal' means.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks a core principle of Aristotelian logic: that essential (dhati) attributes must be fully conceived before the subject can be known. In this passage, he uses the logicians' own example of 'man' as 'rational animal' to show that they themselves do not require detailed conception. This is a powerful internal critique that undermines their entire definitional framework.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish two types of conception
Identify the two types: summary (mujmal) and detailed (mufassal). Note that logicians accept both.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this distinction to show that detailed conception is not always required.
2
Identify the logicians' claim
What is the definition of 'essential' (dhati)? It is that without which the described cannot be conceived.
This implies that the essential must be conceived in detail before the subject is known.
3
Find the counterexample
Ibn Taymiyya points to the definition of man as 'rational animal'. Does a person who says 'man' need to have the full detailed definition of 'animal' in mind?
The logicians themselves do not require that when defining 'man' one must recall all attributes of 'animal'.
4
Analyse the contradiction
Since logicians admit that someone can conceive 'man' without the detailed attributes of 'animal', their claim that every essential must be detailed is false.
This exposes an inconsistency between the rule and accepted practice.
5
Consider the fallback
If they retreat to summary conception, then summary conception of 'man' includes all essential attributes implicitly, so the condition still fails.
Even with summary conception, the logicians' definition of essential is not satisfied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : التصور يكون مجملاً ومفصلاً. الذاتي ما لا يتصور الموصوف إلا بعد تصوره. وحينئذ فقول القائل: إن الذاتي ما لا يتصور الموصوف إلا بعد تصوره إن بطلان [جمل؟] ادعاه في كل تصور فهذا باطل. وهو ممن يسلم بطلانه، فإنه يقول: «قولك عن الإنسان هذا لم يتصور الذاتيات مفصلة. فإنه الموصوف إلا إنه حيوان ناطق حدّ تام يفيد تصور حقيقته»، ومع لم يستحضر في ذهنه أن «الحيوان» هو الجسم الحساس النامي المتحرك بالإرادة. فثبت أنهم يجعلونه متصوّراً لحقيقة بدون استحضار الذاتيات على وجه التفصيل. فلا يجب في كل ذاتي أن يتقدم تصوره المفصل. وأما التصور المجمل فلا يجب فيه استحضار شيء من الصفات. وتصور «الإنسان مجملاً كتصور الحيوان مجملاً. ومعلوم أن الموصوف يشاركه غيره في صفات ويفارقه في صفات. فإذا لم يجب ذكرُ جميع الصفات المشتركة على وجه التفصيل فدعوى الاكتفاء ببعضها دون بعض تحكم محض. وإذا كانت حقيقة «الإنسان» عندهم متصورةً بدون تصور الصفات الذاتية المشتركة على وجه التفصيل عُلم أن ما ذكروه من أن الذاتي ما لا يمكن تصور الموصوف بدونه باطل وإن اكتفوا بالتصور المجمل فمن تصور «الإنسان» مطلقاً فقد دخل في جميع ذلك صفاته. وبسط هذا له موضع آخر.
The Fourth Station - The Ninth Aspect: Conception is either summary or detailed. The essential (dhati) is that without which the described cannot be conceived. Thus, when someone says: 'The essential is that without which the described cannot be conceived,' if he claims the falsity of the totality of his claim in every conception, then this is false. And he is among those who admit its falsehood, for he says: 'Your statement about man that he has not conceived the essentials in detail – rather the described is that he is a rational animal, a complete definition that yields a conception of his reality,' but he does not bring to mind that 'animal' is the sentient, growing, voluntary-moving body. So it is established that they consider him to have conceived the reality without bringing to mind the essentials in detail. Thus it is not necessary for every essential that its detailed conception precede. As for the summary conception, it does not require bringing to mind any of the attributes. And the summary conception of 'man' is like the summary conception of 'animal'. And it is known that the described shares with other things in attributes and differs in attributes. So if recalling all common attributes in detail is not required, then claiming to suffice with some over others is pure arbitrariness. And since the reality of 'man' according to them is conceived without the detailed conception of the common essential attributes, it is known that what they mentioned – that the essential is that without which the described cannot be conceived – is false. And if they content themselves with the summary conception, then whoever conceives 'man' absolutely has included all its attributes in that. And the elaboration of this has another place.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' definition of the essential (dhati) as that which must be conceived before the subject can be understood. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that conception can be summary (mujmal) or detailed (mufassal). He then attacks the logicians' own practice: they claim that man is essentially 'rational animal', but someone who says this often does not have the full detailed definition of 'animal' in mind. Hence, they themselves allow conception without detailed recall of all essentials. Therefore, the requirement that every essential must be conceived in detail is false. He concludes that if they fall back on summary conception, then any summary conception of 'man' already includes all essential attributes implicitly, so the condition still fails. The entire argument uses the example of 'man' and 'animal', common in Islamic logic, to show the inconsistency.

Key Terms
الذاتي
التصور المجمل
التصور المفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the essential attributes are not truly essential.
C) Because the subject can be conceived without any attributes.
D) Because the logicians never actually define 'man' as 'rational animal'.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the logicians' own practice contradicts their claim that essential predication requires detailed conception, thereby undermining their definitional theory.

What does this imply about the logicians' requirement?

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Mashhurat as Non-Certain Propositions
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that mashhurat are not certain by citing widespread rational knowledge of moral truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that mashhurat can be certain by identifying his counterexample from rational moral knowledge and the Sunni authorities he cites.

Core Idea
Mashhurat include self-evident moral truths that are certain, not merely opinion-based.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a core assumption of the logicians: that mashhurat (widely accepted propositions) are never certain. In this passage, he argues that universal moral propositions like 'justice is good' are rationally known and considered certain by many scholars, including Sunnis who affirm divine predestination. This lesson uses his argument to test the logicians' classification of premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
What does the passage say the logicians assert about mashhurat? Find the key phrase: they claim mashhurat are not certain (yaqini).
The logicians classify mashhurat as a type of opinionative premise (zanniyya).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counterexample
Which examples does he give of propositions that are mashhurat but he considers certain? Look for 'justice is good', 'injustice is bad', etc. These are universal moral statements.
He calls them 'al-qadaya al-'ilmiyya kulluha' (all universal propositions).
3
Analyze his evidence from Sunni authority
Who does he cite to support his claim? He lists Hanafis, Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis who affirm rational goodness and badness (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqli). Note that they also affirm predestination, contrary to the Mu'tazila.
This shows that the logicians' assumption is not universally accepted even among Sunni theologians.
4
Assess the logical force of his argument
Does his critique invalidate the logicians' classification? Yes, by providing a counterexample, he shows that the universal claim 'all mashhurat are uncertain' is false. However, the certainty of moral propositions is itself debated.
The argument relies on the acceptance of rational moral knowledge, which was contested by the Ash'aris.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني أن يقال : المراد بـ «المشهورات» عندهم هي القضايا العلمية كلها مثل كون العَدْل حَسَناً والظلم قبيحاً، والعلم حسناً والجهل قبيحاً، والصدق حسناً والكذب قبيحاً، والإحسان حسناً، ونحو ذلك من الأمور التي تنازع الناس هل يُعلم حسنها وقبحها بالعقل أم لا. وأكثر الطوائف على إثبات الحسن و القبح العقليين لكن لا يثبتونه كما يثبته نفاة القدر من المعتزلة وغيرهم. بل القائلون بالتحسين والتقبيح من أهل السنة والجماعة من السلف والخلف كمن يقول به من الطوائف الأربعة، وغيرهم، يثبتون القدر والصفات ونحوهما مما يخالف فيه المعتزلة أهل السنة ويقولون مع هذا بإثبات الحسن والقبح العقليين. وهذا قول الحنفية ونقلوه أيضاً عن أبي حنيفة نفسه. وهو قول كثير من المالكية والشافعية والحنبلية؛ كأبي الحسن الثَّميمي، وأبي الخَطَّاب، وغيرهما من أئمة أصحاب أحمد؛ وكأبي عليّ بن أبي هُرَيْرَة، وأبي بكر القفال الشَّاشي.
The Second Type: That it be said: What is meant by 'mashhurat' [widely accepted propositions] according to them are all universal propositions, such as that justice is good and injustice is bad, knowledge is good and ignorance is bad, truthfulness is good and lying is bad, beneficence is good, and the like – matters about which people disagree whether their goodness and badness are known by reason or not. And most groups affirm the rational goodness and badness, but they do not affirm it as the deniers of predestination from the Mu'tazila and others affirm it. Rather, those who affirm the rational judgment of good and bad from Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, from the salaf and khalaf, like those among the four schools who affirm it, and others, affirm predestination and the [divine] attributes and similar matters in which the Mu'tazila oppose Ahl al-Sunna, yet along with this they affirm the rational goodness and badness. And this is the statement of the Hanafis, and they have transmitted it from Abu Hanifa himself. And it is the statement of many of the Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis, such as Abu al-Hasan al-Thamimi, Abu al-Khattab, and others among the imams of the followers of Ahmad; and such as Abu 'Ali ibn Abi Hurayra, and Abu Bakr al-Qaffal al-Shashi.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that mashhurat (widely accepted propositions) are not certain (yaqini). Ibn Taymiyya identifies mashhurat as universal moral propositions like 'justice is good' and 'injustice is bad'. He notes that most theological groups affirm rational knowledge (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqli) of such truths, albeit not in the same way as the Mu'tazila who deny predestination. He then lists numerous Sunni authorities—Hanafis, Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis—who affirm both predestination and rational moral judgment. The point is that if such propositions are considered certain by these scholars, the logicians' classification of them as merely widely accepted and not certain is false.

Key Terms
المشهورات
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that mashhurat are not certain by pointing to:
A) The fact that mashhurat are not actually widely accepted.
B) That many scholars, including Sunnis who affirm predestination, still affirm rational certainty for moral propositions.
C) That the logicians defined mashhurat incorrectly.
D) That no one actually disputes the goodness of justice.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's argument shows that the epistemic classification of premises in logic is not value-neutral but depends on substantive theological and epistemological commitments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a two-premise argument that Ibn Taymiyya could have used: Major premise: All rationally necessary propositions are certain. Minor premise: 'Justice is good' is a rationally necessary proposition. Conclusion: Therefore, 'justice is good' is certain. Then evaluate whether this syllogism is valid.
?
Identify one other mashhurat example (not from the passage) from Islamic scholarship that could be considered certain, and explain why Ibn Taymiyya might argue it is yaqini.
